Health Project
Credit and Project Summary
Borrower:                Georgia
Inplementing Agency:    Ministry of Health (MoH)
Beneficiaries:           Population at large, Ministry of Health
Poverty Category:        Program of targeted interventions. The project creates t he conditions
necessary to provide a universal package of preventive public health
services and essential clinical care in a financially sustainable manner.
Additional support will be targeted to those Georgians who lack the
income or assets to pay for services beyond the basic package.
Amount:                  SDR 9.7 million (US$14 million equivalent)
Terms:                   Standard IDA terms. Payable in 35 years, including 10 years of
grace.
Commitment Fee:          0.50% on undisbursed credit balances, beginning 60 days after
signing, less any waiver.
Financing Plan:          See para. 2.23.
Net Present Value:       See paras 4.24.4 and Annex D

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
GEORGIA
Health Project
I. THE HEALTH SECTOR
A.  Socio-economic Context
1.1.         Georgia has a population of 5.4 million people in a geographical area of 70,000
square kilometers, bounded by the Black Sea, Russia, Azerbaijan, Armenia and Turkey. At the
time of its independence, April 9, 1991, Georgia appeared to be a relatively well-off republic with
fairly good growth potential. Since independence, however, Georgia has suffered from intense
civil conflict. A large influx of refugees to the capital city (about 270,000) has worsened the
economic plight of the country. In recent years, the Georgian economy has greatly been affected
by the disruption in payments and trade within the FSU, by large trade shocks, and by output
declines in Russia and elsewhere in the region. These factors have been further aggravated by the
effects of weak financial policies after independence; a sharp economic decline and lowered
standard of living are the result.
1.2.         The Government has made progress towards restoring political stability and law and
order. Since early 1994, it has focused on rebuilding the economy and has expressed a
conmmitment to restoring macroeconomic stability while fostering the resumption of growth. To
this end, a comprehensive program of macroeconomic stabilization and structural reforms has been
implemented with the assistance of the IMF and the World Bank. Macroeconomic stabilization has
made important advances: monthly inflation rates in April and May, 1995 were below 1 %, and the
exchange rate remained stable throughout the year. In September, 1995, a new currency, the
Georgian Lari, was introduced. The exchange rate with the US dollar has remained stable.
Privatization of small-scale enterprises has accelerated since the second half of 1994. In 1995, the
Government reduced by 35 % the number of positions in the budgetary sector. The Government has
begun to make radical reforms in the administration and financing of activities which previously
were fully publicly financed. It has made reforms not only in the public administration and civil
service sectors, but also in social services. The Government intends to pursue steady
implementation of health sector reform and to initiate education sector reforms as well.
1.3.         The health status of the Georgian people is poor relative to the rest of Europe, and
is deteriorating rapidly. In 1990, life expectancy was 68.1 for men and 75.7 for women, compared
to 71 for men and 78 for women in Western Europe. The infant mortality rate (IMR) declined
more than 50% in the past thirty years, but remained twice as high as that in Western European
countries. One third of infant deaths occur in the first three days of life and, thus, could be
prevented through better perinatal and neonatal care. The maternal mortality rate (MMR) is
estimated to be more than four times higher than in Western European countries. Adult male
mortality has become an increasing public health problem. Deaths due to cardio-vascular diseases
have been higher than in Western Europe, contributing to lower life expectancy, especially among
men.



- 2 -
1.4.          During the last five years, health conditions have further deteriorated. The IMR
has risen 13%, reaching an estimated 21.4 (adjusted WHO definition) in 1993. Outbreaks of
measles and diphtheria occurred in 1994 and tuberculosis is on the rise among children and adults.
The MMR has increased and is expected to increase further, due to an increase in home deliveries.
Deaths due to cardio-vascular diseases seem to have increased 35 % over the last five years and the
overall age-adjusted mortality rate has risen by 18%. These trends suggest that average life
expectancy has very likely fallen since 1992, but reliable data are not available.
1.5.          The former health system did, however, have positive features. A strong network
of hospitals and polyclinics was available and coverage was universal. Significant steps in controlling
infectious diseases such as tuberculosis had been made. Indeed, the system may have been
particularly adept at tackling health problems resulting from poverty and the environment. However,
there is little doubt that the former system was characterized by severe imbalances and inefficiencies
which need to be changed.
1.6.          The Government is committed to reforming the health sector. Legislation passed in
June 1995 and implemented that August produced important changes: (a) improvement of preventive
public health services, a reduced role for State financing of clinical services, and a focus on
strategic planning, monitoring and policy adjustment by the Ministry of Health; (b) a separation of
provision from financing, effected by setting up a State Health Fund and regional agencies financed
by government and municipal contributions and a wage based social security tax; (c) self-
management of hospitals, with all staff paid by income generated solely by the institutions; (d)
allocation of funds to municipalities on the basis of population size rather than on the basis of norms
related to the supply of health services; and (e) the legitimization of previously illegal direct
payments from users to providers, enabling the privatization of hospitals, polyclinics, dental clinics,
as well as the import and distribution of pharmaceuticals, under effective and efficient government
regulation.
B. Health Sector Organization and Financing
1.7.          The structure of health services is under transformation. Under the model inherited
from the former Soviet Union, the organization, management and finance of health services were
centrally coordinated through the Ministry of Health and services were delivered through a
hierarchical referral system of more than 400 hospitals and 1,400 polyclinics and ambulatories.
Nearly all of the country's approximately 25,000 physicians and 50,000 nurses were employees of
the Ministry of Health. Under the reforms, however, many responsibilities will be decentralized to
newly created health regions, institutions will be privatized and health sector staff removed from the
Government payroll.
1.8.          Decree 400, issued by the Head of State in December 1994 and later ratified by
Parliament, lays the foundation of the health sector reforms. It establishes 12 health regions and
creates two new regional institutions: regional hospitals, which will provide tertiary level in-patient
care; and regional health funds (RHF), which will be responsible for ensuring that adequate services
are delivered in the regions and processing and auditing requests for insurance payments from
providers. A Cabinet of Minister's decree (June 1995) later ruled that all non-tertiary health care
institutions would become administratively and financially autonomous from the State Budget.



- 3 -
1.9.         Privatization of health facilities is being administered by the Ministry of State
Property Management. The regional hospitals will remain publicly owned. All other institutions
are grouped into three categories: those required to maintain existing services levels for 10 years;
those required to remain as health providers but with no specific service requirements; and those to
be used however the new owner desires. Those required to maintain existing service levels will be
offered first to an association of personnel working inside the institution, and only subsequently, if
the personnel are unwilling (or unable) to buy the institution, will it be put up for auction. The first
phase of privatization has begun, and targets more than 700 dental clinics and pharmacies. This
will be followed by 500 polyclinics in early 1996 and a number of small hospitals in mid-1996. The
Government's targets for privatization are included in the Statement of Development Objectives
(Annex A, para 20). Facilities which are not purchased would be liquidated by July, 1998.
1.10.        As part of the reforms introduced in early 1995, approximately 120,000 employees
of the health sector have been gradually removed from the Government payroll between August and
December, 1995. Those that remain in public facilities are paid directly by the now managerially
independent hospitals and clinics.
1.11.        Under the former system, health care services were primarily financed from the
government budget. Localities were allocated funds using a formula based on the number of
hospital beds and physicians. A limited number of facilities were financed and partly controlled by
state enterprises and through parallel systems provided by other Ministries such as Railways and
Defense. With the collapse of the economy, government revenues decreased sharply between 1991
to 1994 and the private sector has become the major source of revenue for the health sector. In
place of the former system of public services, an unregulated amalgam of public and private
services arose. While most services continued to be provided in public facilities, patients paid for
all aspects of treatment, including drugs, supplies, and provider fees. Consequently, between 1990
and 1993 the average number of outpatient visits per person per year fell from 10 to 5. In the
absence of an adequate household expenditure survey, it is not possible to give a credible estimate of
the total value of private expenditures. The amount may, however, total as much as 75 percent of
total sector expenditures.
1.12.        The government's policy response to increase revenues for the sector has been to
introduce a mandatory payroll tax for health with 3 percent employer contribution and 1 percent
employee contribution. Aware of the adverse effects on labor and production costs, the
government has kept the payroll tax for health low compared to other countries and has agreed to
reduce the payroll taxes for other programs by 10 percentage point. In addition to the revenue
from the payroll tax, central budget allocations for health as well as revenues from, privatization,
licensing fees, and interest income will go to the newly created State Health Fund (SHF). The SHF
will act as a financial intermediary and reimburse both public and private health care providers for
a basic package of preventive and essential clinical health care services included in the State Health
Program. The SHF will be administratively independent from the Ministry of Health and governed
by a Board which includes the Minister of Finance and the Minister of Health. A law passed in
February, 1996 gave responsibility for managing the assets of the Fund to the Treasury. In
addition, municipalities are obligated to provide a package of services under the Municipal Health
Program. With these additional sources of revenues, public sector health spending is projected to
reach 6.0% of total public sector expenditure in 1995 and 8.0% in 1996. Health care services not
included in the State Health Program or the Municipal Health Program will be paid for out-of-
pocket by consumers. (Annex C.6 provides a detailed description of the State Health Fund and the
health care financing reform)



- 4 -
C. Key Sector Issues
1.13.         The health care system has already started a dynamic transition from centralized to
decentralized administration and financing, diversifying between public and private providers in a
new competitive environment. As it continues to reform the health care system, the Government
will need to address the following issues: (a) a deterioration in the health status of the population;
(b) inefficient use of inadequate health service inputs; (c) poor quality of health care services; (d)
inadequate access and coverage of health services; and (e) a collapse of government revenues.
Deteriorating Health Status
1.14.         Deteriorating maternal and child health. During the last five years, the health of
women and children has deteriorated. The IMR has risen 13%, reaching an official rate of 21.4
(adjusted WHO definition) in 1993. Two-fifths of infant deaths occurred in the first seven days
after birth. Out of these, 80% occurred in the first three days, suggesting the need for improved
perinatal and neonatal care. Experts estimate that 57 % of child deaths from diarrheal diseases
could be avoided through better management of medical supplies and health services. The maternal
mortality rate (MMR) was estimated to be 39 per 100,000 births from 1990-93, and is expected to
increase due to a recent rise in unsupervised home deliveries. The number of abortions in 1994
was estimated to be between 40,000 - 45,000, almost equaling the number of live births (51,000).
1.15.         Increase in communicable diseases. The country's deteriorating socio-economic
conditions, sizable displaced population, and collapsing health delivery system along with an
increase in drug-resistant strains of microbes, have contributed to a marked increase in
communicable diseases. Immunization of children almost completely ceased in 1992 leading to: (i)
almost 300 cases and 42 deaths from diphtheria since October 1993; (ii) a 3.5 fold increase in the
incidence of measles in 1993; and (iii) an increase in the incidence of pertussis. Acute respiratory
infections (ARI) now account for 43 % of child mortality under 1 year of age. Increasing rates of
drug abuse amongst young adults has lead to an increase in the incidence of Hepatitis-B in the 15-35
year age group. Recent trends also show a sharp increase in tuberculosis (TB) morbidity and
mortality. The TB mortality rate is now 90% higher than any country in Western Europe. TB-
associated meningitis, an indicator of the lack of early detection and effective treatment of
tuberculosis, has increased from 10 cases in 1990 to 32 cases in 1993. The incidence of sexually
transmitted diseases has also increased over the last three years. The incidence of syphilis, for
example, increased 100% between 1993 and 1994. By January 1, 1995, 18 HIV-positive individuals
were registered.
1.16.         The hidden pattern of non-communicable diseases. Adult male mortality has
become an increasing health problem. Although cancer rates are about 25% lower than and
respiratory disease almost equal to the EU average, deaths due to cardio-vascular diseases (CVD) are
higher than in Westem Europe. Cardio-vascular diseases accounted for 72% of total mortality in
1993. Over the last five years, deaths due to cardio-vascular diseases seem to have increased by
35%, and the overall age-adjusted mortality has risen by 18%, suggesting a continuing decline in
the average life expectancy. Likely contributors to the apparent increase in cardio-vascular
mortality are: the socio-economic crisis, the deterioration of health services, a lack of drugs, and less
rigorous death registration practices. Furthermore, high rates of smoking and alcohol consumption
are significant factors of morbidity and mortality (at least 50 percent of males over the age of 18
smoke) and partly explain the sharp increase in I ung cancer for men and breast cancer for women.
The health determinants and health status of the Georgian population are described in more detail in
Annex B.



-5-
Inefficiency
1.17.        Like all countries of the Former Soviet Union, the health system in Georgia has
been insensitive to the principles of both allocative and technical efficiency. Allocatively, Georgia
has over-invested in curative health services at the expense of health promotion and prevention.
Most of the resources have gone to hospitals which are overstaffed with specialized physicians. In
addition to poor allocative decisions, the system has been plagued by technical inefficiency. The
chronic under-financing of recurrent expenditures for drugs, fuel, and maintenance reduce
productivity. Inefficiencies are further exacerbated by the excess supply of hospital beds and
obsolete technology, generating low occupancy rates and long average length of stay. Perhaps
most importantly, the system has been greatly constrained by a lack of management skills.
1.18.        Allocative efficiency. The system is biased toward in-patient care, with a poorly
developed capacity for public health programs. The health protection service, known as the
Sanitary-Epidemiological (San-Epid) Service, is a strictly vertical organization oriented towards
control of communicable diseases. Health professionals lack training in modem public health
programs such as non-communicable disease prevention, immunization, respiratory and infectious
diseases management, family planning, prenatal and neonatal care and emergency obstetrics.
Comprehensive and intersectoral strategies for health promotion do not exist.
1.19.        Deterioration offaciliies and equipment. Over half of the health care facilities
were built before 1940. New facilities were readily financed to replace those that were deteriorating
rather than maintaining the latter. In 1988, for example, 115 facilities were under construction, most
of which remain unfinished today. Groundwater level changes have led to noticeable damage in
buildings, including stability problems and cracks in structural components, walls and floors. Low
quality materials and the absence of routine maintenance are responsible for falling ceilings, damaged
walls, floors, and doors, and open window frames. In many buildings, roofs need immediate repair
to avoid pennanent damage caused by leaks. The 1987 earthquake further contributed to the damage
of health care facilities as have the recent civil wars, which not only damaged health care
infrastructure, but also left many homeless refugees seeking shelter inside health facilities.
1.20.        The existing medical equipment, mostly from Former Soviet Union and Eastern
European countries, is outmoded and does not comply with modem safety standards, resulting in
significant inefficiencies. Few spare parts and frequent power surges have led to a high proportion of
non-functioning equipment, accompanied by a shortage of disposable items for diagnostic equipment
(chemical reagents, X-ray films, and ECG paper). Inadequate disinfection and sterilization of medical
and surgical materials is endemic. Ineffective boilers and dry heat sterilizers are still used in surgical
and intensive care procedures, inhibiting infection control. Annex C.4 provides more background on
the health care infrastructure and medical equipment in Georgia.
1.21.        Misuse of health human resources. Georgia has more than 120,000 persons
working in the health sector. The density of physicians is one of the highest in the world: 1
physician per 197 inhabitants, compared to an OECD average of 1:435. The large number of
doctors is the result of Georgia's traditional esteem of medicine as a career and former Soviet
Union policy emphasizing the number of physicians as a measure of achievement in the health
sector. The number of nurses is also high relative to OECD countries, but they are poorly trained,
unlicensed, and have low professional status and pay. Undertrained, underutilized and
inadequately managed doctors and nurses greatly inhibit the efficiency and effectiveness of the
Georgian health care system.



- 6 -
1.22.         During the Soviet period, one graduate and one post-graduate medical school
provided all medical training in Georgia. After the break-up of the FSU, due to the increasing
number of candidates and the refusal of other republics to admit foreign medical students, the
Ministry of Education (MoE) granted authorization to 28 new private medical schools. There is no
accreditation procedure for these schools, no quality standard for teaching, no career track for
faculty, no reliable register for trainers and trainees, and no licensing or certification procedure for
graduates. The total number of medical students in all schools is now around 2,500, about 10 times
the requirement of the country. Similar problems are found in the post-graduate medical education
system. There are no criteria for selection of persons to receive resident training, no standards to
judge the quality of the training programs, and no process for certification of specialists once they
have completed training. The educational process does not provide the knowledge and skills
necessary to carry out competency-based and evidence-based care comparable to international
standards.
1.23.         The medical training system is characterized by early specialization and little focus
on modern primary care, such as family practice and public health. Furthermore, concepts of
modern public health are rarely covered. For example, the diagnosis, treatment, and prevention of
sexually transmitted diseases (STDs) are unfamiliar to most health professionals. STDs are still
managed in an outmoded separate delivery system of dermatological and venereal disease clinics.
Education in contraceptive technology is also rare. As a result, family planning information and
contraceptive methods for spacing pregnancies are not given in the immediate postpartum period.
Abortion remains the dominant mode of family planning. Annex C.5 provides more background on
health human resources.
1.24.         Pharmaceuticals. The old system of state purchase and distribution of
pharmaceuticals and supplies has collapsed. Currently, an estimated 75 % of pharmaceuticals is
distributed through an unregulated private market. Humanitarian organizations import the rest.
Pharmacies have spontaneously emerged both inside hospitals and other health facilities, as well as
outside, in street kiosks. While the privatization of the pharmaceutical sector is a desirable
direction toward which to move, without an adequate administrative and regulatory framework, the
risk of overpriced drugs of inferior quality is high. Neither public nor private sector personnel
have sufficient experience in pharmaceutical procurement and management. In addition, an
adequate infrastructure for distribution and retail does not exist.
Poor Quality
1.25.         On all dimensions of quality of care -- structure, process, and outcome - Georgia
has seen a marked decline. The physical structures and equipment are deteriorating. Hospital
stocks are depleted and the quality of care received by patients is a function of their ability to obtain
pharmaceuticals and medical supplies outside the hospital or clinic. In addition to the deficiencies
in the physical infrastructure, medical professionals are poorly trained and their practices
unregulated. Furthermore, the excess supply of physicians raises questions about whether the
workload of physicians is sufficient to maintain their skills. In one major hospital, for example, the
week's surgery schedule had only four operations for the staff of six surgeons. The increase in the
number of private medical schools over the last three years raises additional concerns about the
qualification of medical graduates. While outcome measures of quality are scarce in Georgia, the
data which are available suggest that the decline in the structural variables described above are
contributing to poorer health outcomes. The hospital mortality rate increased from 1. 1% of all
patients treated in 1990 to 1.9% in 1993.



- 7 -
1.26.         Quality assurance in the former Soviet model of health care consisted of anmmal
performance reviews, monitoring of patient complaints, and on-going monitoring of activities by
hospital administrators. No formal accreditation process existed. Requirements for facilities were
guided by norms and monitored by the Sanitary-Epidemiological Stations. These norms, oriented
towards quantity rather than quality, were never consistently enforced. Adverse events, when
reported, triggered punishment from authorities. Recent indicators suggest that even this limited level
of quality assurance in Georgia is deteriorating. For example, under the former Soviet system, nearly
all hospital mortalities were autopsied. A recent study found that the autopsy rate had dropped,
ranging from 13% to 48% depending on the hospital. A decline in patient complaints over the last
few years is likely to reflect a perception of unresponsiveness of the prior system rather than an
increase in satisfaction.
1.27.         The Ministry of Health has yet to identify quality assurance as a priority in its health
care reform policy and a responsibility of the Government. Instead, it expects that a more competitive
market will lead to improvements in quality as facilities of poorer quality will not survive. However,
because patients are often unable to assess the quality of services, a Governmental role remains.
Without a quality assurance system, human lives and the health of the population may be in
considerable jeopardy. Annex C.3 provides more description on health care quality.
Inadequate Coverage and Access
1.28.         Historically, coverage of health care services has been quite high in Georgia.
Although there were regional differences in the availability of medical services, access was adequate.
The country's financial crisis, however, has greatly limited the availability of affordable, adequate
quality health services. A 1994 national household survey found that 27% of the poor were unable to
afford medical care. Physical access has been limited by a dramatic reduction in the availability of
functioning ambulances and helicopters, necessary for serving mountainous areas in winter. These
physical and financial limitations to access may explain the decline in utilization rates for
hospitalization and outpatient visits over the last three years.
Sustainable Health Care Financing
1.29.         While Georgia was part of the Soviet Union, the health sector was under-funded,
receiving from 2.8 to 4.1 % of GDP. The situation grew worse with the collapse of the economy.
In 1994, public expenditures on health fell to 0.3% of GDP and 1.3% of public sector expenditures.
Annual public expenditures on health averaged less than US$1 per person in 1994, less than 10% of
1990 expenditure levels in real terms. The fall in real expenditures was accompanied by a shift in
the line item composition of expenditures. The rising costs of heating oil and other hard currency
supplies crowded out spending on wages and maintenance in an increasingly limited budget. In
1990, wages and supplements were 52% of total expenditures. By 1993, they had fallen to 39%
and in 1994 to 32% of the totals. Current real wages in health are equivalent only to a few loaves
of bread per month.
1.30.         Health care was primarily financed from the government budget although private
payments to physicians have been an accepted, if illegal and undocumented, practice for many
years. Over the last three years, however, revenue sources for the health sector have changed
dramatically. The private sector has become the major source of revenues, providing possibly as
much as 75 % of the total sector from 1991 to 1994. Public provision of services essentially
ceased. In 1995, however, with the introduction of a new health reform program and an increased



- 8 -
central budget allocation to health, public expenditures increased. Per capita expenditures are
expected to almost reach US$4 in 1995 and US$9 in 1996. As a percent of total public spending,
health expenditures increased from 1.3% in 1994 to 6.0% in 1995 and are projected to increase
further to 8.0% in 1996. The Government intends to concentrate available funds on those services
which are public goods and the most cost-effective. This "basic package" of public health and
essential clinical services includes immunization, prevention of socially dangerous diseases,
maternal and child health services (including prenatal care and deliveries), treatment of
tuberculous, inpatient psychiatric care, and provision of drugs for terminally ill patients.
Consumers will pay out-of-pocket for services not included in the basic package, with exemptions
for the poorest. While no private insurance exists today in Georgia, the Governments long term
strategy is to develop compulsory insurance through SHF and allow voluntary insurance schemes
regulated by the State, to cover services not covered by the SHF. While this increase in public
spending is a signal of stronger government commitment to the health sector, it is considerably less
than the US$22 per capita the 1993 World Development Report estimated is necessary to offer a
package of public health and essential clinical services in a middle-income country.
1.31.         Key issues the government faces with the new health care financing reforms are:
(i) assuring the financial sustainability of the State Health Fund; (ii) developing an efficient
provider reimbursement system; and (iii) guaranteeing affordable health services for the poorest.
(These issues are discussed in greater detail in Annex C.6)
1.32.         Financial sustainability of the State Health Fund. Currently, government
projections for expenditures for the State Health Fund are 42.6 million lari for 1996, exceeding
revenue projections by approximately 11 million lari. (Table 1.1) In addition to the concerns about
the sustainability of central budget financing (approximately 50% of the Fund), the ability of the
Government to collect the tax revenues earmarked for the Fund (approximately 20% of the Fund)
and the projected degree of privatization (which is an unsustainable source of financing in any
case), a further consideration is the projected volume of services and unit costs. The Government
estimates of expenditures are based on current utilization patterns and do not consider latent
demand that is suppressed by unaffordability or poor quality of services. If the Government is
unable to control utilization, the cost of the basic package will exceed projections and add to the
projected deficit.
1.33.         In the short run, the most likely sources to fill the financing gap of the Fund are: i)
international donors and humanitarian agencies, who currently provide more than 7.5 million lari of
vaccines and medical supplies per year and have pledged an additional 10.9 million lari for 1996 to
continue their support to Georgia within the framework of the new reforms and ii) additional
revenues from privatization, assuming the Government's estimates are approximately one-third of
what they expect. The additional assistance from donors and humanitarian agencies would cover
the projected 11 million lari financing gap of the SHF. In addition, the following factors will be
essential in order to achieve a balance of projected revenues and expenditures for the Fund: i)
strengthened collection of tax revenues; ii) continued support from the central budget; and iii)
implementation of a monitoring and control system. The Government has already introduced a new
control system and technical assistance provided under other World Bank financed projects should
strengthen tax collection.



- 9 -
Table 1.1: Projected Annual Revenues and Expenditures for the State Health Fund, 1996
(Millions of Lari)

1.34.         Provider payment system. The reimbursement system designed by the MoH for the
basic package combines two methods: capitation for some public health activities and a case based
system for hospitals. For public health services covered by the basic package, such as
immunization and maternal and child health services, providers will be paid an age-adjusted
capitation fee ranging from US$1 to US$2 per capita. In the case-based methodology for hospitals,
several diagnostic categories, which the Georgians refer to as "nosological" categories, were
defined under the basic package, and for each one a standard level of health services was defined
and priced. Hospitals are reimbursed by the State Health Fund for all patients who are admitted
with diagnosis covered by the basic package. The unit prices include the quantity of medical
consultations and tests for diagnostic purposes, the 'required' drugs and treatment medical acts and
also the number of inpatient days for each category.
1.35.         The reimbursement system for hospitalization, as currently designed, is clearly too
complicated and administratively costly. To create and monitor a case-based reimbursement
system, a significant amount of reliable information on costs and health services production is
needed which is currently not available in Georgia. Furthermore, the possible perverse effects of
such a reimbursement system are well described in the literature, including a strong incentive to
increase artificially the utilization of more sophisticated resources, causing an increase in health
care costs (exactly opposite to the intentions of the reimbursement system) and possible iatrogenic
effects on patients. Difficulties in containing costs will only add to the difficulties Government
faces in financing the very narrow scope of services included in the basic package. As a minimum,
the Georgians should cap total expenditures for hospitalization under the basic package and lower
the price paid per nosological category if volume rises faster than expected.
1.36.         Affordabilit of servces for the poor. With two thirds of the population under the
poverty line and approximately 800,000 in the most vulnerable groups (including more than 270,000
refugees), the Government is exploring ways to ensure that the very poor will have access to the
essential health services which are not included in the basic benefits package. The two issues which
remain unresolved are: (1) how to identify the poorest in an economy where a large part of the
activity is informal and poverty is a widespread yet new social phenomena and (2) how to finance a
subsidized program to support access to basic health services by the poor when public financial



- 10-
resources are extremely scarce and institutional capabilities are very weak. By the end of 1996,
with technical assistance provided under the Structural Adjustment Credit (SAC) and additional
analytical work completed as part of the poverty assessment, a new program for social assistance
will be established.
1.37.         In the short-term, the Government first chose to address the needs of the poor by
designating Tbilisi City Hospital #9 and several other specialty hospitals in Tbilisi to serve the needs
of these groups. A total of 275 beds were designated to treat the most vulnerable groups free of
charge: single unemployed mothers with three or more children, single pensioners with pensions
below US$2 per month and single invalids. Other institutions, like the Veteran's Hospital, were
financed by the State to continue providing care to their clients. Beginning in February, 1996, the
Government revised its program by providing vouchers to the same target groups and allowing them
to select their own provider.
D. Government Reform Strategy
1.38.         The objective of Georgian health policy is to improve the health of the entire
population. The Government clearly recognizes both the need for reform and the opportunities for
radical change presented by the current situation. Since 1992, the Government has progressively
articulated, approved by legislative action or regulation and begun to implement a comprehensive
reform which is described in detail in its Statement of Development Objectives (Annex A). The
reforms will:
a)  concentrate scarce public resources on a basic package of public health (immunization,
healthy children and safe motherhood, prevention of socially dangerous diseases,
epidemiological surveillance, and health promotion) and essential clinical services
(deliveries, treatment of children under the age of 14, treatment of TB, inpatient
psychiatric care, provision of drugs for terminally ill patients, and emergency
services). The scope of the package would be expanded as the economy improves.
Services outside of the package, such as outpatient visits and non-emergency surgery,
would be provided on a fee-for-service basis, with exemptions made for the most
vulnerable groups;
b)  reduce the size and improve the quality of health sector personnel by: (i) removing
physicians and other personnel from the government payroll, allowing competition to
rationalize the number in the sector; (ii) limiting entry to medical schools; and (iii)
instituting a licensing exam for currently practicing as well as recently graduated
physicians.
c)  reduce the number and improve the quality and efficiency of health care facilities by:
(i) privatizing most facilities and granting management autonomy to those that Temain
in the public sector; creating a mixed, potentially competitive public/private market for
patient services; (ii) reimbursing facilities for services delivered rather than by the
number of beds and other norms; (iii) accrediting and licensing all health facilities; and
(iv) introducing preventive maintenance of facilities and equipment, and upgrading
service quality and management;
d) separate provision of health care services from their financing. Health services will be
provided mostly by the private sector, with a limited number of facilities in the public



- 11 -
sector. A new agency, the State Health Fund, was established in August 1995 and
serves as a financial intermediary, receiving funds from the central budget and a 4 %
payroll tax (3 % paid by employers and 1 % by employees) and reimbursing providers
for services included in the basic package. Municipalities are required to cover health
care services for emergency cases such as heart attacks, appendicitis, etc. In the short-
term, municipalities are also required to provide subsidies to facilities in their
jurisdiction to offset the costs of their utilities.
e)  limit the Ministry of Health's role to one of formulating policy, strategic planning,
monitoring and regulation, and quality assurance;
f)  develop human resources to support the reform in areas such as modem public health,
family practice, health services administration, and management.
E. Role of Other Donors and NGOs
1.39         The project was prepared with the financial assistance and cooperation of several
donors including: (a) the Government of Japan, which provided US$615,500 for project preparation
from the Policy and Human Resources Development Fund (PHRD); (b) UNICEF, which is
developing a comprehensive maternal and child health program for 12 mountainous districts and
has agreed to fit its program into the framework of the project and provide equipment, supplies,
pharmaceuticals, and training for obstetric and pediatric services in feldsher ambulatory practices
(FAPs) and rural ambulatory clinics. UNICEF also supported a household survey on health care
expenditures and utilization which was recently completed; (c) the World Health Organization's
European Office (WHO/EURO), which provided technical assistance on health reform,
reorganization of the pharmaceutical regulatory agency, reorganization of the public health services
and modernization of the health information system; (d) the Europan Coumnunity Hunanitarian
Office (EU-ECHO), with whom a complementary policy was articulated for emergency power
supply and winterization of health facilities included in the rehabilitation component; and (e)
USAID, through the American International Health Alliance Hospital partnership project
implemented by Emory University, which supported project development in the areas of maternal
and child health, health care financing and human resources development. During project
preparation, many international and local NGOs were also consulted. Several donors have shown
interest in providing additional financing for the project. The Governrent of Austria is financing
technical assistance for the planning and programming of rehabilitation facilities and for equipment
maintenance planning. The Dutch Government and the Erenh Gvrnment have expressed a
strong interest in cofinancing the project. Regular contact has been established with the Germnan
Embassy, the European Union Delegation and FIllTAC(S in Tbilisi. The Government disseminated
a list of required technical assistance with terms of reference during a donors' conference in Tbilisi
in September 1995 and continues to actively pursue active donor involvement in the project and the
health reform effort more generally.



- 12 -
F. Lessons Learned from Past Operations
1.40.        The World Bank's experience in Georgia dates only from FY94. An Institution
Building IDA Credit of US$10.1 million equivalent was approved on July 5, 1994. A Municipal
Infrastructure Rehabilitation Credit of US$18 million equivalent was approved on November 8,
1994. A Rehabilitation Credit of US$75 million equivalent was approved on March 30, 1995. This
is the first credit operation for the health sector. Valuable lessons have been drawn from similar
Bank projects in other countries -- particularly in Eastern Europe, Central Asia and Estonia -- as
well as from the experience of other donors. The most frequently encountered problems that would
be relevant to this operation include slow start-up of sector reforms, insufficient financial
commitment to the health sector, political instability and frequent changes in ministerial high-level
staff, inadequate institutional base of the health sector, initial weak project implementation capacity,
and resistance to accept external technical assistance. Project preparation and design addressed
these issues as follows: (i) the health sector is one of the leaders in reforming the economy:
beginning in December 1994, strategic pieces of health reform legislation have been prepared,
approved by parliament and the Head of State, and implemented; (ii) the Appraisal Mission
confirmed an increase of public funding for health for 1995, raising average per capita expenditure
from less than US$1 to almost US$4 between 1994 and 1995; (iii) an experienced and committed
team remained mostly unchanged in the MoH after the November 6, 1995 elections; (iv) starting in
early 1995, the MoH began a reorganization which included the creation of the State and Regional
Health Funds, the Department of Public Health (with regional branches), the National Health Care
Management Center, the Department of Pharmaceuticals and Pharmacy Industry, and the State
Agency for Quality Assurance and reform of the existing Sanitary-Epidemiological, Medical Care
and Maternal-and-Child Care Departments; (v) a PPF for US$500,000 was signed on August 25,
1995, allowing for a strong government role in project preparation and implementation by
establishing a Project Coordination Unit inside the MoH before pre-appraisal and providing training
in Bank procedures; finally, (vi) the Government has been very amenable to foreign technical
assistance during project preparation and plans to use US$1.8 million of the Credit in order to
continue to finance foreign technical assistance.
G. Rationale for Bank Involvement
1.41.        This project is consistent with the limited country assistance strategy, as discussed
by the Board of Directors during presentation of the Rehabilitation Credit in March, 1995.
Specifically, the strategy in Georgia is to help reverse the economic declines of the past few years
and to assist the transition to a market economy. With a particular focus on promoting private
sector development and redefining the role and improving the efficiency of the public sector, IDA is
supporting economy-wide and sector-specific reforms and investments to promote economic growth
in the productive sectors and to rehabilitate and build new capacity in critical infrastructure sectors.
The Association will also assist in strengthening public management and developing human
resources and institutional capacities throughout the economy. Strengthening the social safety net
and targeting scarce public resources to the provision of public goods and to fight against poverty
are the major strategic objectives in the social sectors. The project fully complies with this strategy.



- 13 -
II. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives
2.1.         The proposed project would support the health sector reform efforts the Government
has initiated and would aim to improve the health of the Georgian population. The project's specific
objectives are to:
a) reorient the health system towards cost-effective public health interventions like
preventive and primary health care, assuring, in particular, better quality care for
women and children, and focusing the MoH's activities on regulation of public and
private providers;
b) rehabilitate selected facilities and equipment that were down-sized and dedicated to
cost-effective provision of maternal, perinatal, and pediatric care;
c) develop human resources to support health reform, through continuing education for
public health doctors, family practitioners, nurses, health administrators, and other
personnel; and
d) support the development of an independent health fund with regional branches, in
order to finance a package of basic clinical services in a cost-effective and equitable
manner.
B. Project Description
2.2.         Each component of the proposed Project is defined around a core policy agenda,
described in detail in the Government's Statement of Development Objectives issued by the Ministry
of Health and the Ministry of Finance (SDO/Annex A). In the short-term, the project will focus on
ending the deterioration of basic preventive and maternal and child care services and on separating
financing from the provision of health care. In the medium-term, the project will: (a) reorient the
MoH from direct management to regulatory, planning and quality assurance functions, (b) improve
health promotion programs, (c) demonstrate how to rehabilitate selected health facilities, and (d)
educate health managers. In the long-term, if the economy improves and reform is sustained, a
second health project will be designed to consolidate health promotion activities and to rehabilitate
and improve quality in a larger number of institutions. At negotiations, the Governmentprovided
assurances that it would implement the reforms in the Statement of Development Objectives (SDO) as
agreed upon with the Association and review with the Association annually all actions with respect
to the SDO. [para 5.2(a)]
Component 1.0: Health System Reorientadon
(US$4.0 million, or 24% of total base cost)
2.3.         This component aims to reorient the present health system through (a) the
modernization of primary health care at the central, regional and local levels, and the development
of vertical health promotion and disease prevention and community-based programs; (b) the
improvement of reproductive, perinatal and pediatric care services; and (c) the realignment of the



- 14 -
MoH to planning, monitoring, regulatory and quality assurance functions, including the
reorganization of a pharmaceutical regulatory agency. The component's three sub-components are
described below.
Component 1.1: Modernization of Public Health Services, Health Promotion
and Disease Prevention
(US$1.6 million, or 10% of total base costs)
2.4.         Core policy Agenda. The Government has initiated radical reforms to concentrate
scarce public resources on preventive public health and health promotion services, and to
decentralize health system administration. A Department of Public Health (DPH) was created in
the Ministry of Health. The DPH is responsible for analysis and management of the
epidemiological situation and the coordination of measures for disease prevention, reduction of
disability and early mortality, and health promotion. The Department will have a regional branch
in each of the twelve newly designated health regions, responsible for implementing public health
activities in each region, collecting health statistics, and health planning. In order to ensure
sufficient allocation for health promotion activities and encourage innovative community initiatives,
the Government has established a Health Promotion Program within the Department of Public
Health.
2.5.         Activities. The sub-component will support the institutional development of the
Department of Public Health (DPH) in the MoH and the health regions, including development of a
health information system. The project will also support the Health Promotion Program (HPP)
which would receive financing from the State Health Fund and from the Project and would finance
health promotion and prevention programs at the national, regional and local level. At the national
level, the HPP would finance six high priority programs to address the causes of more than 80% of
the avoidable deaths in Georgia: (i) reduction of tobacco consumption, (ii) cardiovascular disease
prevention, (iii) traffic and occupational injury control, and (iv) family planning, (v) AIDS and
STDs; and (vi) substance abuse. Several of these programs will also address alcohol consumption,
an important risk factor in Georgia. The HPP will also create a competitive funding system to
support community-based health promotion initiatives. The sub-component will finance 11 person-
months of foreign technical assistance, 2 long-term fellowships, 5 short-term visits, local training
programs, community-based and mass-media health promotion programs, audio-visual equipment
and materials, computers, software, vehicles, office equipment and furniture. At negotiations, the
Government provided assurances that the Borrower would (a) allocate at least one percent of the
income of the State Health Fund to the Health Promotion Programs [para 5.2(b)]; and (b) select the
health promotion programs on competitive basis. [para 5.2 (c)]
Component 1.2: Healthy Children and Safe Motherhood
(US$1.2 million, or 7% of total base costs)
2.6.         Core policy agenda. Two-fifths of infant deaths still occur in the first seven days
after birth, with 80% of these occurring within the first three days. These figures demonstrate a
critical need to improve maternal, perinatal and neonatal care. The Government is committed to
restoring essential services for women and children and to improving quality to European levels.
The inmnunization system was restored after breakdown of resources in 1992. The new basic
package guarantees free prenatal care, deliveries and care for children under one year of age. The
municipal package includes coverage for children until age 15.



- 15 -
2.7.          Activities. The sub-component aims to reduce infant and maternal mortality by
30% nationwide, and to reduce infant, perinatal and maternal mortality by 50% in Tbilisi and
Kutaisi. Publicly-funded cost-effective intervention and better training of health professionals are
expected to increase use of contraceptives, decrease abortions and improve the quality of perinatal
care. These activities will be combined with significant downsizing of appropriate health facilities.
Activities geared toward the health of women and infants will also be carried out in other project
components: Health System Reorientation, Rehabilitation and Maintenance of Health Facilities,
and Development of Health Human Resources. The sub-component objectives will be reached
through the following activities: (a) reinforcement of the MoH's institutional capacity to develop
health policies, health promotion activities and services for women and infants; (b) development of
a national policy for care of women and infants, which includes: a minimum benefits package,
protocols of care, rules for licensing of staff and health services, guidelines for privatizing and
reorganizing services, and the establishment of a perinatal mortality surveillance system; (c)
organization of quality perinatal care in downsized and rehabilitated sites in Kutaisi, Tbilisi, and
Tsageri, in order to demonstrate how perinatal services may be improved; (d) development of pilot
referral systems in Tbilisi and Kutaisi; (e) development of training programs in maternal and child
health, obstetrics and pediatrics; and (f) creation of a national Task Force for Child and Maternal
Survival, which will build national commitment, increase funding, and develop public awareness of
child and maternal survival issues. The sub-component will finance 9 person-months of foreign
and 137 person months of local technical assistance, 1 long-term fellowship, 5 short-term visits,
local training programs, educational materials and equipment, medical equipment, computers,
software, ambulances and other vehicles, and office equipment and furniture to assist in the
modernization of maternal and child care and family planning. Before negotiations, the
Government provided satisfactory evidence to the Association that it had: (a) reached formal
agreement with the Kutaisi Municipality for merging maternity and pediatric services in the Kutaisi
Pediatric Hospital, which will be partially rehabilitated under the project [para 5.1(a)]; and (b)
appointed a Kutaisi-based coordinator, acceptable to the Association, to oversee project activities
in Kutaisi and Tsageri [para 5. 1 (b)]. As a condition of presentation to the Board, the Government
has provided evidence to the Association that it has issued regulations creating residency programs
in obstetrics and gynecology and pediatrics. [para 5.3(a)]
Component 1.3: Realignment of the Ministry of Health
(US$1.2 million or 7% of total base costs)
2.8.          Core policy agenda. Under the reform legislation enacted in August 1995, a clear
separation of the sources of finance, the management of finance, and the provision of services was
mandated. This separation is an important aspect of ongoing reforms in many countries. In
Georgia, separation is a key factor contributing to the development of a competitive environment,
where public and private financiers and providers will work under incentives for greater efficiency.
The natural consequence of this separation is the focusing of the Ministry of Health's role in
strategic planning, monitoring, quality assurance and policy adjustment. Self-management capacity
conferred to hospitals will progressively reduce the number of doctors. Doctor's registration and
licensing will be pre-conditions for individual contractual arrangements with hospitals. A new
pharmaceutical law was approved and regulation of this law is under preparation, opening the way
for the privatization of import, distribution and retail sales of pharmaceutical products.
2.9.          Activitics. The sub-component aims to generate monitoring, regulatory and quality
assurance capacity for the MoH, through the design and implementation of measures to reduce the
excess supply of doctors and down-size hospitals, and accreditation and licensing procedures to
ensure that providers, institutions, pharmaceutical products and related products meet adequate



- 16 -
quality standards. The sub-component will: (a) license health professionals and create a data base
of registered doctors and nurses; (b) register, license and inspect health care facilities to create a
data base of physical resources and ensure quality standards; (c) support health care institutions
willing to develop internal quality assurance programs; and (d) reorganize the administrative
structure of the Department of Pharmaceuticals and Pharmacy Agency, where four major activities
will be performed: registration of products, quality assurance, inspection and information. The sub-
component will finance 8 person-months of foreign and 81 person-months of local technical
assistance, 6 short-term visits, local training programs, educational materials and equipment,
medical and laboratory equipment, computers, software, vehicles, and office equipment and
furniture to assist in the new monitoring, regulatory and quality assurance functions of the MoH.
As a condition of project presentation to the Board, the Government has provided to the Association
evidence that: (a) rules on licensing of health professionals were approved and enacted [para
5.3(b)]; and (b) rules on the accreditation of medical and nursing training institutions were
approved and enacted. [para 5.3(c)]
Component 2.0: Rehabilitation and Maintenance of Health Facilities and Equipment
(US$9.7 million, or 59% of total base cost)
2.10.         Core policy agenda. During project preparation, an assessment was made of the
need for and cost of rehabilitating health facilities. On the basis of this assessment, it was clear
that the cost of a comprehensive rehabilitation program was prohibitively high. The Government's
strategy is to target scarce resources to institutions more inclined to reduce their size and use their
existing resources more efficiently by adopting energy efficiency and other cost saving measures.
The following Government initiatives are expected to enhance efficiency in health institutions: (a)
the separation between financiers and providers will reimburse hospitals and polyclinics on the
basis of services provided rather then the number of beds, thereby creating incentives to reduce
excessive and inefficient services by closing or merging health units; (b) the health sector
privatization program will reduce the quantity of the public and private sector facilities and staff;
and (c) decentralization of management will make institutions more accountable to their staff and
their communities. In addition, the facilities that are rehabilitated will serve as centers of clinical
excellence where residency programs in gynecology, obstetrics, neonatology, pediatrics, and
hospital administration will be based.
2.11.         Activities. The component will: (a) partially rehabilitate two unfinished blocks in
Tbilisi City Hospital #2 where the Tbilisi Perinatal Center will be based and general surgery,
cardiology and pediatric services will be provided and residency training in maternal and child care
will be offered; (b) merge Kutaisi Maternity and Pediatric Hospital into a single building, to be
partially rehabilitated, as a demonstration of scaling-down and rationalizing facilities and
modernizing hospital management; (c) partially rehabilitate and equip the maternal and perinatal
services in the Tsageri District Hospital to demonstrate how to provide the pyramid of integrated
primary and secondary perinatal care in a rural and mountainous area with poor access to health
services; (d) build capacity of the MoH's Building Technical Department (BTD) to set standards
for the programming, design, and rehabilitation and construction of health facilities and build
capacity of the MoH's Committee of Medical Technologies and Equipment (CMTE) to identify the
needs, conduct technology assessment of, and procure medical equipment; and (e) provide training
for hospital programmers, architects, biomedical engineers, maintenance specialists and hospital
staff on facilities design, construction and equipment maintenance, in cooperation with the Center
for Continuing Education (CCE) and the Faculty of Medical Engineering of Tbilisi Technical
University. The component would provide 51.5 person-months of foreign and 90 person-months of



- 17 -
local technical assistance, 4 short-term visits, civil works, furniture, office and medical equipment,
vehicles, computers, software and testing instruments. At negotiations, the Government provided
assurances that the municipality of Kutaisi would merge the Maternity Hospital into the Pediatric
Hospital by January 1, 1998. [para 5.2(d)]
Component 3.0: Development of Health Hulman Resources
(US$0.9 million, or 6% of total base cost)
2.12.        Core policy agenda. The development of health human resources in areas of key
importance to support the reform will be a crucial element in the policy agenda and in this project.
Training and continuing education is needed in the formnerly neglected areas of public health, family
practice, health services administration, health insurance and financial management. A National
Health Management Center (NHMC) was established in March 1995 with a mandate to coordinate
and regulate public and private medical and nursing education, create a system of licensing and
accreditation for professionals and training institutions, develop post-graduate medical and nursing
curricula, create standards for specialization, and develop health sciences research. New
legislation on medical education was drafted, creating an accreditation system for schools and
limiting first year entrants; new residency programs were created according to European Union
guidelines; and teams of experts were appointed to review medical specialties curricula. The
NCHM was also instrumental in the first steps to create professional societies of Public Health and
of General Practice. A Center for Continuing Education will be created inside the NCHM with
four major areas of educational activities: Health Care Management, Family Practice, Public
Health and Nursing.
2.13.        Activities. The component aims to fill the existing gap in modem knowledge and
skills among health care professionals in Georgia. The component will: (a) strengthen the capacity
of the National Health Management Center to create a system for accrediting medical schools,
develop a modern curriculum for post-graduate physician and nursing education, support the
development of professional associations, and develop health services research to support the MoH
in policy making; and (b) support the creation of the Center for Continuing Education (CCE) within
the National Health Management Center to provide part-time professional education for newly
emerging health care occupations such as health care management, family practice, and public
health, provide on-site and at-distance continuous training programs for all health professionals,
establish a program of "training the trainers", and create a desk-top publishing unit. The
component would finance 6 person-months of foreign and 32 person-months of local technical
assistance, 2 long-term fellowships, 18 short-term visits, local training programs, civil works,
books and journals, audio-visual equipment and teaching materials, computers, software, vehicles,
office equipment and furniture. As a condition of credit effectiveness, the Government will provide
to the Association evidence that it has issued regulations setting a ceiling, acceptable to the
Association, on new entrants in medical schools, effective for the academic year 1996-97 and
thereafter. [para 5.4(a)]



- 18 -
Component 4.0: Modernization of the Health Financing System
(US$1. 1million, or 7% of total base cost)
2.14.        Core policy agenda. Faced with a complete collapse of government revenues for
health and with the task of modernizing a very inefficient system, the Government has adopted a
radical reform of the health finance system. Public expenditures will be concentrated on provision
of a basic package of preventive and essential clinical health care services. Services not included in
the basic package, such as outpatient visits and non-acute hospitalization, will be financed through
out-of-pocket expenditures. For the poorest, either specific institutions will be set aside, or
exemptions will be accepted. Simultaneously, dental clinics, polyclinics, pharmacies and several
hospitals are in the process of being privatized. The self-management of those remaining in the
public sector will be increased. This policy agenda is quite advanced both in legislative and
implementation terms: (a) the separation between provision and financing of health services became
effective on August 10, 1995, with the creation of the State Health Fund; (b) self-management
capacity was conferred to hospitals, and was operational on December 31, 1995, when all staff
were to be paid by income solely generated by the institutions, thereby reducing dependence on
government budget; (c) rules for allocating funds to municipalities were changed from physical
norms to a per capita allocation; (d) the announcement and list of the first phase of the privatization
has already been published with 714 institutions targeted for privatization before the middle of
1996; and (e) the Government has already taken action to set aside beds to treat the most vulnerable
groups and a quota system for every hospital to provide care for patients unable to pay for health
service is also planned.
2.15.        Activities. The component will support the development of the State Health Fund
(SHF), with thirteen regional branches (Regional Health Funds), responsible for financing the
provision of a basic package of preventive and curative services, by: (a) developing a management
inforrnation system (MIS) and capacity in financial accounting and planning in the State Health Fund
; (b) developing an accounting and financial management system to be used in the Regional Health
Funds; (c) developing a provider/health fund interface; and (d) organizing training in health
financing and management in cooperation with the CCE. The component will also support regular
collection of data on utilization and expenditures of health services not included in the basic package
in order to monitor private expenditures on health and the accessibility and affordability of services
for the poor. These activities will provide the necessary information and infrastructure for the
Government to: (i) monitor the financial sustainability of the health reforns at the central and
municipal level; (ii) develop mechanisms to control administrative costs; (iii) develop an optional
community-rated health insurance model in the future. The component will finance 9 person-
months of foreign and 49.5 person-months of local technical assistance, I long-term fellowship, 2
short-term visits, local training programs, computers, software, office equipment and furniture.
Before negotiations, the Government presented to the Association evidence that it has completed a
study of the financial accounts of the State Health Fund including a comparison of the cost and
utilization of services before and after implementation of the reforms in August, 1995. [para 5. l(c)]
At negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it shall take all necessary measures to
ensure the viability of the State Health Fund without reducing the scope of services included in the
basic benefits package and shall monitor and provide to the Association quarterly financial
statements of the SHF. [para 5.2(e)]



- 19 -
Component 5.0: Project Administration
(US$0.8 million, or 5% of total base cost)
2.16.        The component aims to strengthen project administration and coordination by
establishing and staffing a Project Coordination Unit (PCU) within the Ministry of Health. The
PCU is responsible for overall management and financial management of the project. It would also
strengthen the MoH's capacity to coordinate other donor assistance in the health sector.
2.17.        Consultants will provide expert support to the PCU as follows: (a) ensuring that
funds (credits, grants, parallel financing and local counterpart funds), resources (human, physical
and information), and management support are made available to the project when required through
appropriate budgeting and planning; (b) managing special accounts established for the project; (c)
processing and expediting credit/grant fund withdrawal applications and draw-downs; (d)
consolidating and preparing progress and evaluation reports, financial records, statements of
expenditure, and audits; (e) coordinating implementation of various activities to ensure that project
objectives are met, and that the required actions are executed in an efficient and timely manner in
accordance with the planned project processing and implementation schedule; and (f) procuring
consulting services, goods and civil works for the project. Such support will help to standardize
processes and aim to achieve economies of scale through bulk procurement. By the end of the
project, the functions of this unit would have been institutionalized in the MoH. For the PCU, the
project would provide 27 person-months of foreign and 336 person-months of local technical
assistance, 7 short-term visits, computers and software, and office equipment and materials.
C. Environmental Aspects
2.18.        The project is expected to have a positive impact on the environment through
energy efficiency measures that are part of the overall program to improve hospital efficiency.
Medical waste disposal systems in hospitals and polyclinics were assessed during project
preparation and under the project standards and norms will be developed for the rehabilitation of
health facilities. Improvements in public health will also have a positive impact on the
environment. The project has been designated in Environmental Category C.
D. Participatory Approach
2.19.        The preparation of the project has been carried out in partnership with the Ministry
of Health and its designated project component working groups. In Kutaisi, preparation was done
in close collaboration with the office of the Mayor. Close contact was maintained with the Health
Protection Parliamentary Commission. Local non-govermnental organizations were consulted
during project preparation, including the Georgian Medical Association, the Georgian Planned
Parenthood Federation, Wellstart of Georgia, Women of Georgia for Peace, and the organizers of
the Georgian Public Health Association. The community-based health promotion sub-component
(under Component A) is designed to elicit participation from grass-root organizations interested in
promoting healthy life-style and preventing non-communicable diseases among adults.

E. Cost Estimates
2.20            The total cost of the Project is estimated at about Lari 25.2 million or US$19.7
million equivalent, including contingencies. The total base cost is estimated at US$16.5 million.
Physical contingencies are estimated at US$1.6 million and price contingencies at US$1.7 million.
Total contingencies represent 19% of the total base cost. The foreign exchange component is
estimated at US$11.3 million, including contingencies, or about 58% of total project cost. Project
costs were estimated at September 1995 price levels and include physical contingencies of 10% for
civil works, 5%  for equipment and vehicles, 15% for office equipment and furniture and 5% for
other expenditure accounts. Table 2.1 provides an estimate of the overall project costs. 3

2.21.           The total project cost includes civil works, equipment, medical equipment and

incremental recurrent cost incurred during project implementation. The break-down of costs by

2.22.           The estimated project costs include US$1.6 million to cover incremental operating
costs directly attributable to the project during the four year period. These consist of operational
and maintenance expenditures for equipment, vehicles and buildings, and the salaries of component
coordinators. There are no other incremental salaries included in the project costs since it is
assumed that all new positions will be filled by reassigned MoH staff. These incremental recurrent
costs are expected to be within sustainable limits since measures are being instituted in the health
sector to reduce recurrent costs, improve efficiency and increase collection of user charges.

F. Project Financing Arrangements
2.23.        The proposed IDA credit of US$14.0 million will finance about 71% of total
project costs, comprising 96% of total foreign exchange costs and 37% of local costs. Government
will finance about US$5.3 million of total project cost, including taxes and duties of about US$2.5
million. An additional US$400,000 is expected from foreign donors for technical assistance.
Table 2.2 shows the financing plan. At negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it
would open a local contribution account in its National Bank and replenish that account quarterly
so as to maintain a balance equal to US$400,000 [para 5.2(f)]. As a condition of credit
effectiveness, the Government would provide to the Association satisfactory evidence that it has
opened the Local Contribution Account in its National Bank and has deposited an amount equal to
US$400,000 into such account. [para 5.4(b)]
