G. Procurement
2.24.        The procurement of all items funded from the IDA Credit would be governed by
World Bank Procurement Guidelines of January 1995 and would make use of the Bank's standard
bidding documents. Procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 2.3 and details of the
procurement plan are located in Annex E. The General Procurement Notice was issued on January
31, 1996. PCU members attended a Bank organized procurement seminar in Kiev in January, 1996
and a project launch workshop will be held in July, 1996. Country Procurement Assessment
Report: as in other transition economies, Georgia has not yet developed a legislative framework
for competitive public procurement. However, the country has made progress and demonstrated its
capabilities in procurement under the IDA-financed Municipal Infrastructure Rehabilitation Project.
A country procurement strategy is currently under preparation.
2.25.        Responsibilityfor Procurement. All procurement will be carried out by the PCU in
the MoH which will be assisted by foreign procurement consultants financed under the project
during the first two years of project implementation.
2.26.        The civil works (US$8.3 million) included under the project consist of: (a) partial
rehabilitation of Tbilisi City Hospital #2; (b) refurbishment of Kutaisi Pediatric Hospital and
Maternity Hospital (merged into a single building); (c) refurbishment of the Tsageri District
Hospital; (d) rehabilitation of the Center for Continuing Education (CCE) in the Ministry of
Health; and (e) minor renovation of six different office sites in Tbilisi. These construction
activities will be undertaken during project years 1, 2 and 3. Civil works contracts costing more
than US$500,000 will be contracted out using International Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures,
while contracts costing less than that amount will be procured through National Competitive
Bidding (NCB) procedures using the Bank's sample documents for NCB. Two civil works



- 22 -
contracts for the larger hospitals are estimated to total about US$7.3 million, will be procured
through ICB. The contract for the district hospital and the CCE are to be procured through NCB.
Rehabilitation of six separate office sites will be procured under local procedures and funded by
counterpart contribution. For contracts for works to be awarded on the basis of ICB, a margin of
7.5 % will be granted to domestic contractors, in accordance with the World Bank Procurement
Guidelines (Appendix 2, para 7).
2.27.         The goods (US$5.6 million) to be financed under the project include office
equipment, medical and laboratory equipment, office furniture, vehicles including ambulances,
computer hardware and software and training materials, including audio-visual equipment. To the
extent possible, these items would be grouped into several procurement packages. For five
packages of US$300,000 and above, procurement will be through ICB (US$3.5 million). Three
packages estimated to cost less than US$300,000 but more than US$50,000 will be procured
through international shopping (IS) (US$0.4 million) with at least three quotations from two
different countries. One package for specialized medical and laboratory equipment, where there
are limited suppliers, will be procured by limited international bidding (LIB) (US$1.5 million).
Furniture and other smaller items, packaged into four contracts of less than US$50,000 each, will
be procured through National Shopping (NS) (US$0.2 million) with at least three quotations from
local suppliers. Preference for domestic bidders shall apply to goods manufactured in Georgia in
accordance with the World Bank Procurement Guidelines (paras 2.54-2.55).
2.28.         Consultants for technical assistance and training (US$4.0 million) will be selected
in accordance with the "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by the World Bank Borrowers and
by the World Bank as Executing Agency" (August 1981). TA would be provided in the following
main areas of specialization: architectural and engineering services, benefits package, health
promotion, quality assurance, maternal and child care organization, privatization, modern health
administration, health economics and health finance. In addition, TA would be required for project
management, project accounting, procurement and information technology in the first two years of
the project. TA and training would total 121.5 staff months of foreign technical assistance and
725.5 months of local technical assistance. In-country training of doctors, nurses and health
administrators would be provided by the Center for Continuing Education or foreign consultants.
Technical assistance will be grouped in four different packages for building design and supervision,
project management, health care and training. In addition, activities under the Health Promotion
Programs (US$1 million) will be procured in accordance with World Bank Guidelines for
consultant services. It is proposed that consulting services (TA and training) will be met by single
or multiple firms that have the requisite staff that meet project needs. Other activities that would be
financed by the Bank credit would include fellowships, short-term visits and health promotion
programs. All these contracts will be with firms and training institutions on the basis of short-
listing, to avoid burdening the PCU with too many contracts. Local consultants will be hired on an
individual basis following a competitive selection process.
2.29.         All other project items in the category incremental operating costs purchased under
the project would be procured using the normal GoG procedures.
2.30.         All civil works and bidding packages for equipment and materials estimated to cost
US$300,000 or more would be subject to prior Bank review. In addition, there will be a prior
Bank review process for the first package procured through IS. Terms of reference for consulting
contracts would be subject to prior review by the Bank, regardless of value. In addition the Bank
will also review short lists, letters of invitation, and consultant contracts higher than US$100,000



- 23 -
for firms and US$50,000 for individuals. Selected review of awarded contracts below the threshold
would also be carried out during project implementation.
2.31.         Procurement information would be collected and recorded as follows: (a) prompt
report of contract award information by the borrower; and (b) comprehensive quarterly reports to
the Bank by the Borrower indicating: (i) revised cost estimates for individual contracts; (ii) revised
timing of procurement actions, including advertising, bidding, contract award, and completion time
for individual contracts; and (iii) compliance with aggregate limits on specific methods of
procurement.

2.33.        Disbursement will be the responsibility of the Ministry of Health. The PCU will be
responsible for preparing and maintaining all documentation. Disbursement training will be
organized for the PCU staff.
2.34.        To facilitate project implementation, the Borrower would establish a Special
Account in one of the major commercial banks on terms and conditions satisfactory to the
Association to cover the Association's share of expenditures. (See Schedule 5 of the Credit
Agreement) The Authorized Allocation would be US$500,000, representing about four months of
average expenditures made through the Special Account. During the early stage of the project, the
initial allocation to the Special Account would be limited to US$250,000. However, when the
aggregate disbursements under the Credit have reached the level of SDR 2,000,000, the initial
allocation may be increased up to the Authorized Allocation of US$500,000 by submitting the
relevant Application for withdrawal. Applications for replenishment of the Special Account would
be submitted monthly or when one-third of the amount has been withdrawn, whichever occurs
earlier. Documentation requirements for replenishment would follow the standard Bank procedure
as described in Disbursement Handbook, Chapter 6. Monthly bank statements of the Special
Account which have been reconciled by the Borrower would accompany all replenishment requests.
2.35.        Disbursements for civil works and goods contracts valued at more than US$300,000
will be made against full documentation. Similarly, disbursements for contracts to consultancy
firms valued at more than US$100,000 and for individual consultants valued at more than
US$50,000 will also be against full documentation. Disbursement for contracts for civil works,
goods and consultancy services valued below these thresholds may be made on the basis of
Statements of Expenditures (SOE). Supporting documentation for SOEs would be retained by the
implementing agency for at least two years after disbursement. Direct payment applications would
be accepted for minimum amounts of US$30,000 and applications for special commitments for
minimum amounts of 20% of the current authorized allocation to the Special Account.
I. Accounting and Auditing
2.36.        Project accounts and the Special Account will be audited in accordance with
"Guidelines for Financial Reporting and Auditing of Projects Financed by the World Bank." The
Project Coordination Unit will maintain a consolidated account of all expenditures under the
project. The Borrower will provide the Association within six months of the end of each fiscal year
an audit report. The project accounts would be audited annually by independent auditors
satisfactory to the Association in accordance with appropriate auditing principles, with terms of
reference approved by the Association. The audit reports should include a separate opinion on the
adequacy of SOEs and on the Special Account.



- 27 -
III. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Organization and Management
3.1.          Project Implementation Responsibilities. Primary responsibility for project
implementation would be assumed by the Departments and Agencies of the Ministry of Health
responsible for each of the project components, as shown in Table 3.1 (See organizational chart of the
MoH in Annex E). These agencies, while unfamiliar with World Bank procedures, have been
actively involved in project preparation. Many have already begun to initiate activities under their
components. Each of the components and sub-components will have at least one component
coordinator who manages the activities in his or her component. A Project Coordination Unit (PCU),
located in the Ministry of Health, will be responsible for the overall management of the project and
will be staffed by local and foreign consultants with expertise in planning, administration, accounting,
procurement and disbursement. Detailed Terms of Reference for the PCU are included in the
Project's Implementation Plan (Annex E, page 3). Technical assistance needed for project
implementation is included in the project cost estimates of each component. At negotiations, the
Government provided assurances that the Borrower would: (a) employ throughout the project
implementation period seven component coordinators whose experience and qualifications are
acceptable to the Association; [para 5.2(g)] and (b) maintain throughout the project implementation
period a Project Coordination Unit (PCU) with functions and responsibilities acceptable to the
Association and employ, at all times, individuals for the positions of director of the PCU, procurement
officer (two positions), financial officer, planning and monitoring officer and administrative assistant,
whose experience and qualifications are acceptable to the Association. [para 5.2(h)]

3.2.         Implementation Schedule. The proposed project would be implemented over a four
year period, with an additional twelve months for the payment of financial obligations committed
prior to the project completion date and full withdrawal of Credit proceeds. The project is expected to
be completed by June 30, 2000 and the Credit closed on June 30, 2001. The PCU would submit an
Implementation Completion Report (ICR) to the Association within six months of the closing date.
B. Project Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation
3.3.         A Project Implementation Plan has been developed including institutional and
individual implementation responsibilities, a timetable for implementation, and monitoring indicators
and policy-related benchmarks (Annex E). Two sets of indicators have been defined: process
indicators (or input and output indicators) to measure progress in project implementation and impact
indicators (or outcome and impact indicators) to evaluate improvements in the health system and in
the health status of the population. The most important process indicators (most to be measured
annually) include: (i) the number and value of awards granted by the Health Promotion Fund; (ii) the
number of health care institutions licensed; (iii) the number of health professionals licensed; (iv) the
percentage of pregnant women completing four prenatal visits and having a full risk assessment; (v)
the number of people trained in public health, family practice, nursing, and management by the
Center for Continuing Education; and (vi) the number of health facilities that are privatized. The
most important impact indicators, also to be measured annually, include: (i) the prevalence of
smoking; (ii) the incidence and mortality rate from circulatory diseases; (iii) the incidence and
mortality rate from traffic accident related injuries; (iv) the knowledge, attitudes, and practices of all
health personnel working in maternal and child health; (v) perinatal and infant mortality rate; and (vi)
total health expenditures by sources and expenditure category. The project will also promote the use
of monitoring indicators throughout the country by requiring recipients of the health promotion
grants to document the impact of their respective projects. In addition, key policy benchmarks have
been identified in the Government's Statement of Development Objectives and these will be reviewed
annually by the Association and the Government as part of project monitoring. Responsibility for
project monitoring will fall principally to the PCT T, which would monitor project tasks as defined in
the project implementation plan as well as key indicators to assess project progress and impact in
relation to specific objectives.
3.4.         Semi-annual progress reports would be prepared for each of the components by the
responsible implementing Agencies or Departmeuts and would be consolidated by the PCU into a
semi-annual report on project activities which would be submitted to the World Bank. A proposed
outline for the Progress Reports is included in Annex E. A mid-term review, to be undertaken by
project personnel, World Bank staff, and a team of independent observers, would be undertaken after
year two of the project to ensure that the direction and contents of the project continue to respond to
stated objectives. In order to closely monitor project implementation, five supervision missions are
planned prior to the mid-term review (see para 3.5). At negotiations, the Government provided
assurances that: (a) the Borrower would prepare and furnish to the Association written semi-annual
progress reports on all project activities in accordance with performance indicators satisfactory to the
Association; [para 5.2(i)] and (b) conduct a mid-term review of the project no later than September
30, 1998, indicating the project's progress from inception and proposing additional measures as
necessary to ensure achievement of the project objectives. [para 5.2(j)]



- 29 -
C. World Bank Supervision Strategy
3.5.          In addition to the review of procurement actions, audits and semi-annual reports on
project progress, the Association will conduct regular supervision missions. It is also likely that other
donors such as UNICEF, WHO, and USAID, with whom the Association has collaborated close'y
during preparation, will participate in these supervision missions. Given that Georgia is a new
borrower and the project has several implementing departments and agencies, the project is estimated
to require 26 staff weeks of supervision per year, with more intense supervision in the first two years
of project implementation. A proposed Supervision Plan is included in Annex E (page 5). One
mission would be timed at mid-year, to review implementation accomplishments and plan for the next
year's activities. Sufficient time should be allowed between this annual review and the preparation of
the Government budget, in order to ensure that project costs and Government counterpart funds are
included in the national budget.



- 30 -
IV. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
4.1.          Although it is difficult to quantify the monetary and non-monetary benefits of the
proposed project, the benefits can be grouped into: (a) real per capita increases in health
expenditures; (b) reductions in the burden of disease; and (c) efficiency and equity gains in the
allocation and use of resources. The project's support of key institutional health reforms, training of
health personnel, scaling down of facilities and down-sizing staff in hospitals and polyclinics, and
major changes in health service financing would, in the short-term, increase efficiency in the delivery
of health services and contain costs. In the medium-term, the emphasis on healthy children and safe
motherhood, added to the imnproved quality of health personnel and clinical treatments, would lower
infant and maternal mortality and morbidity, reducing the economic burden of disease. In the long-
term, preventive measures and promotion of healthy lifestyles, will curb avoidable adult mortality due
to causes related to tobacco and alcohol consumption, unhealthy diets and injuries, further reducing
the economic burden of disease.
Quantitative analysis of benefits
4.2.          Using the approach recommended by the World Bank's 1993 World Development
Report, lnvesting in Health, an analysis of three of the health promotion programs to be included in
the proposed project (smoking, cardio-vascular disease, and traffic prevention) as well as the maternal
and child health activities in Kutaisi was carried out as part of project preparation. The study had the
following objectives: (a) to estimate the burden of disease in Georgia, studying its distribution by
nosologies, age, and gender; (b) to measure the burden of disease associated in particular with the
main preventable health problems and diseases that could be reduced by the prevention programs; (c)
to estimate the present health care costs associated with those health problems and diseases; (d) to
estimate potential health gains achievable through the interventions; and (e) to analyze the cost-
effectiveness of the three preventive programs and the Kutaisi MCH program proposed under the
project. Detail of the study are included in Annex D and summarized below.
4.3.          The results of the "burden of disease" analysis, using the disability adjusted life year
(DALYs) methodology, show that the two groups where the most DALYs are lost due to premature
deaths are infants (population under one year of age) and individuals aged 60 to 64 years. These
results are consistent with the project's strong emphasis on maternal and child health and disease
prevention. Looking across diseases, the study found that cerebro-vascular disorders and ischemic
diseases are the most important health problems in Georgia, followed by accidents for men and breast
cancer for women. The prevention programs proposed under the project could save nearly 200,000
DALYs by the year 2005. This is almost half of the burden of the diseases targeted under the
prevention programs. Each DALY saved will cost, on average, US$2.83. For each dollar spent on
the three prevention programs, US$63 could be saved in treatment and indirect costs. Total,
discounted savings in direct and indirect costs over nine years would be US$35.5 million. Over three-
quarters of the savings would come from the cardio-vascular disease prevention program.
4.4.          The MCH program in Kutaisi, which includes higher investment costs
(refurbishment and equipping the Kutaisi Maternal and Pediatric Hospital) and benefits which are
harder to quantify, would save an estimated 16,489 DALYs at an average cost of US$138 for each
DALY. The total cost of refurbishing and equipping the 60 bed perinatal center in Kutaisi,



- 31 -
US$2.3 million, is less than the estimated US$5.9 million it would cost to build a new center.
Although a cost-effectiveness analysis of the rehabilitation and maintenance of Tbilisi City Hospital
#2 was not included in the study, least cost analysis confirmed that refurbishing and equipping the
facility as proposed under the project would cost US$5.7 million compared to US$10.9 to construct
a new 126 bed hospital with the same function.
Qualitative analysis.
4.1.          While the analysis above demonstrates that the net present value of some of the
project's health promotion programs exceeds total project costs, in fact, most of the project's benefits
are unquantifiable. The project's activities will produce the following benefits:
a) separating provision from financing of health services through an independent Health
Fund will create conditions for the privatization of hospitals, polyclinics, dental clinics,
as well as for pharmaceutical imports and distribution, under effective and efficient
government regulation;
b) improving preventive public health and maternal and child care services, and focusing
the Ministry of Health on strategic planning, monitoring and policy adjustment will
modernize and strengthen public management;
c) reducing the State's financing role to a basic package of curative medical services and
targeting the poorest through exemptions from health care costs, will improve efficiency
in the health sector spending, and will free resources for the reinforcement of the social
safety net;
d) developing self-management capacity in hospitals and paying staff according to services
provided by income solely generated by the institutions will improve efficiency and
develop institutional capacities through the public sector and the economy at large;
e) rehabilitating health facilities selected for their specific role in the project, according to
modern programming and architectural standards to improve service effectiveness,
safety, quality and energy efficiency, will generate positive examples of down-sized
remodeling of critically needed and badly maintained social infrastructures;
f) developing health human resources through continuing education and training will
develop participation, will enlarge the circle of reform-minded stakeholders and will
create conditions for the future multiplication of the reform results.
B. Risks
4.6.          The Government faces substantial financial, political, and institutional risks in
implementing both its ambitious health care reform program as well as the project. A more
detailed discussion of these risks is included in Annex D. The main risks include: (a) the fmnancial
sustainability of the health care reform; (b) the ability of the Government to contribute its part of
project costs; (c) the Government's commitment to continuing with the health care reforms; (d) the
difficulties involved in transforming a curative health system into one more focused on disease



- 32 -
prevention and health promotion; (e) the complexities attached to restructuring institutions and
strengthening the management of the hospitals and the health insurance funds; and (f) the lack of
experience of the Government in implementing Bank financed projects. Many of these risks are
common to any health reform process. Meeting the challenge is essential for both the transition to
a market economy and the modernization of a badly functioning health care system. These risks
have been addressed as follows:
a)  In order to ensure continued Government financial commitment to the health sector, both
the Health Project and the Structural Adjustment Credit (SAC) have included minimum
spending levels on health as a condition of lending. The SAC specifies that at least 7 % of
public spending must go to health and that this amount should rise as revenues increase.
The Government has provided assurances that it would not reduce the scope of service
included in the basic benefits package and would provide to the Association quarterly
financial statements of the fund.
b)  The MoH reform record for 1995 is an outstanding one: (i) creation of a basic package of
services, (ii) purchaser-provider split with the implementation of the State Health Fund, (iii)
self-management capacity conferred to hospitals reducing dependence on govermnent
budget, and (iv) change in the municipal health funding rules from physical norms to a per
capita allocation, among other decisions. This effort is worthy of continuation and support.
c)  A new focus on adult health and on modem health promotion strategies to prevent the high
economic and social burden of avoidable deaths and disability is an essential step to reach
health levels similar to those in Western European countries.
d)  The lack of administrative, accountant and managerial experience inherited by the health
administration from former Soviet times is a handicap that needs to be overcome in any
case. Doing that simultaneously with a decisive health reform will generate synergy to the
whole process.
e)  The creation of a PCU by pre-appraisal is improving project ownership and familiarity
with Bank procedures and will allow for a quick start-up. Moreover, design includes
features to attenuate risks, such as ensuring continued technical guidance and training for
strengthening organizations and management practices, a detailed midterm review by the
end of the second year of the project, and such matters as procurement, disburse ments,
accounting and auditing.



- 33 -

1998 (para 2. 11);

The Republic of Georgia
STATEMENT
OF DEVELOPMENT OBJECTIVES
The World Bank supported Georgia Health Project



Annex A
Page 2 of 10
STATEMENT OF DEVELOPMENT OBJECTIVES
Introduction
This document states the health policy of the Government of Georgia. It lists the strategic
objectives in the health sector, indicating the legislation already passed to support these
objectives. Each of the strategic objectives is analyzed: the major initiatives currently underway
are identified, the planned activities are indicated and benchmarks for policy action are listed and
scheduled.
Strategic Objectives
1.     The central objective of the Georgian health policy is to improve the health of the entire
population through the design and implementation of a primary care-based system which
emphasizes health promotion, disease prevention, and health protection. Health policy should
move beyond curative medical services if it is to meet the full needs of the citizenry. Health and
health care services also ought to be viewed as an investment in the creation of human capital,
through the production of specific services in a transitional economic environment.
2.     Consistent with the Health For All strategy devised by the World Health Organization
European Regional Office, Georgia is in a process of reconstructing a curative delivery system
severely damaged by intense civil conflict that greatly affected the Georgian economy and the
health sector. To rebuild the economy and restore macroeconomic stability and foster the
resumption of growth, our Government has been implementing a comprehensive program of
macroeconomic stabilization and structural reforms with the assistance of the IMF and the World
Bank. The Government commitment encompasses the reform of the health sector, and legislation
has been passed and is under implementation with the following strategic objectives:
(a) concentrate scarce public resources in better preventive public health and healtlY promotion
services, and decentralize the health system administration;
(b) restore the capacity of essential services for children and women and improve their
performnance and quality to attain European levels;
(c) limit the Ministry of Health's role in prevision and instead, refocus it on strategic planning,
monitoring, quality assurance and policy adjustment;
(d) scale down facilities and staff, introduce preventive maintenance of facilities and equipment,
and upgrade service quality and management;
(e) develop health human resources on areas of key importance to support the reform such as
modem public health, family practice, health services administration and financial management;
(f) privatize dental clinics, polyclinics, pharmacies and several hospitals and increase the
selfmanagement of those remaining in the public sector;



Annex A
Page 3 of 10
(g) guarantee a core set of curative medical services for the whole population (basic package of
care), and finance the non-basic package of services through co-payments, exempting the
poorest;
(h) change the previous financing system to one based on Government funding for the public
health service, an independent payroll-based health fiund for the basic package, municipal
funding for emergency acute care and individual payments for the remaining services.
Recent Legislation
3.     An important set of new health sector legislation is composed of the following pieces:
(a) The Decree 400, issued by the Head of State on December 23, 1994 and later ratified
by the Parliament, contains the first statement of the health reforn. The Georgian health sector
will change from a centrally directed system, financed almost entirely from government
revenues, into a decentralized system with a basic program financed by a pay-roll tax, selected
specific earmarked taxes and government and municipal subsides. The remaining curative
services will be financed through the private sector. There will be a clear separation between the
sources of finance, the management of finance and the provision of services. This is intended to
create a competitive environment with incentives for greater efficiency in service provision.
Hospitals and other providers have been given great latitude in staffing and the administration of
services. The role of the national Ministry of Health is being greatly reduced to strategic
planning, monitoring and policy adjustment, including licensing and accreditation, quality
assurance and provider regulation. The management of the health service delivery will be
decentralized and 12 regional health authorities will be created to administer the transaction of
the health fund, and to carry out the responsibilities of the regulatory agency at the local level.
(b) Seven Cabinet of Ministers' decrees on the implementation of the health reform were
issued on June 30, 1995:
* Decree number 390 on the composition and implementation of State Medical Programs,
including five supplements: (i) public health programs financed by the State: immunization,
healthy children and safe motherhood, prevention of communicable diseases, inpatient treatment
and health promotion; (ii) budget plan of the State Heatth Fund for 1995; (iii) basic municipal
package of care; (iv) organization of regional municipal health administrations; and (v)
organization and functions of the regional (municipal) Hoalth Funds.
* Decree number 399 on the role of the MoH in the reorganization of the health care system.
* Decree number 389 on the reorganization of the Sanitary Epidemiological Department in the
MoH. Later, the Department of Public Health was created taking the public health functions out
of the former SanEpid Department.
* Decree number 392 on the privatization of health care facilities. Institutions will become
private through purchasing them by staff and not privatized will be auctioned. The institutions
are grouped into three categories: (a) institutions that will remain with the same curative profile
during next 10 years and will have to carry out state orders; (b) institutions that will maintain



Annex A
Page 4 of 10
their health service profile for not less then a ten-year period; and (c) institutions to be privatized
without any limitation..
. Decree number 388 on the future development of the pharmaceutical sector. The decree
created a compulsory licensing system for importing, distributing and selling pharmaceuticals, a
registration and quality assurance mechanism for all pharmaceutical products, and an inspection
system for all pharmnaceutical related activities.
. Decree number 391 on the financing of the State Health Fund, including a supplement
describing the rules for the collection of the payroll tax of 3% from employers and 1% from
employees, which is one of the most important sources of financing for the basic package of
care.
a Decree number 398 on the payment of a special fee to the State Health Fund for the production
and distribution of health damaging products (tobacco, asbestos, alcohol, among others).
(c) Decree number 269 issue by the Head of State on July 5, 1995, establishes additional
activities to improve the health care system under a market economy. This decree has the
following major innovations:
* By August 1995 all health care institutions will became administratively and financially
autonomous from the State budget. Debts of former public institutions as identified on August
10, 1995, will be covered by the Ministry of Finance. All existing institutions need to be
reregistered at MoH. This registration is valid till January 1997, after which they will have to
follow common legislation. Those who are not registered are not allowed to provide care.
* The basic package of care, to be supported by the State (through the municipal budget and the
State Health Fund) will include: care of pregnant women and children under one year of age, all
kinds of delivery, emergency care for children 1-15 years, care for war veterans and population
under the poverty line, prevention of communicable diseases, including AIDS and STDs, critical
and urgent care, inpatient and out-patient care for tuberculosis and mental health problems,
insulin for insulin-dependent patients, pain relieving drugs for terminal oncological patients, and
free care for the victims of disasters and epidemics.
* The Ministry of Labor and Social Protection will establish criteria for the definition of the
poverty line and will identify those citizens under that line, by place of residence. The Ministry
of Health will identify those health care institutions that will provide free health care to the poor
after providing appropriate certificates.
* The Ministry of Health will guaranty the quality of medical care through the licensing and
aecreditation procedures of staff and facilities, quality control, registration and licensing of drugs
and medical supplies: medicines, food, fuel and other commodities provided through
humanitarian aid will be monetized, in agreement with donors; and included in the state medical
program. Persons or institutions without a corresponding license are not allowed to sell drugs or
medical supplies.
* The Ministry of Health will prepare a 2-3 year program to aid the recovery and further
development of the Georgian medical science.



Annex A
Page 5 of 10
* The Ministry of Health will prepare legislation and regulation for the transition of the present
system into a health insurance model and will introduce legislation on the restructuring of its
own departments in accordance with the health reform needs.
(d) The Decree # 351 issued by the Head of the State, on September 13, 1995, with two
decisions: (a) creation of medical personnel State Social Insurance Company; (b) creation of a
municipal obligation to pay 90% of the cast of health care services utilities in their areas.
Analysis by Objective
(a) Modernization of Public Health Services, Health Promotion and Disease Prevention.
4.     Major Initiatives Currently Underway. By Decree of the MoH, the Department of
Public Health was created in the Ministry of Health and is responsible for the analysis and
management of the epidemiological situation and to organize, coordinate and implement public
health measures for the prevention of diseases, the reduction of disability and premature
mortality, and the promotion of healthy life-styles. The Department will have a regional branch
in each one of the future health regions, and has the authority to supervise the health information
system, guarantee the epidemiological surveillance, organize state, regional and municipal
programs for health promotion and disease prevention, and ensure international cooperation in
these areas. The Department inherits the epidemiological surveillance function (stations) of the
former SanEpid Service, now called the Department of Sanitary Supervision and Sanitary
Norms. Another important activity is the design of vertical programs for Smoking Cessation,
Cardio-Vascular Diseases Control, Prevention of Accidents and Occupational Injuries and
Family Planning.
5.     Planned Activities: (a) the implementation of the vertical programs; (b) the design,
creation and management of a Fund for Health Promotion Activities to provide grants to
community-based initiatives on a competitive basis; (c) the integration of part ofthe former
SanEpid staff in the new Department and the modernization of their mission and work plan; (c)
health services planning according to needs and utilization for the whole country; (d) capacity
development for epidemiological and economic analysis; and (e) the preparation of an annual
report of the health of the nation.
6.     Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) first set of contracts with NGO's and independent
institutions (schools, enterprises) to act as health promoters, to be signed by January 1997; (b)
health protection legislation on tobacco, traffic regulations and food stuff labeling passed and
implemented by July 1997; (c) the first report on the Health of the Nation, to be published by
July, 1998; (d) evaluation of the impact of the first two years of the health promotion programs
by July, 1999.
(b) Healthy Children' and Safe Motherhood
7.     Major Initiatives Currently Underway. Thanks to UNICEF and other international
organizations the immunization system was restored after a severe breakdown of resources that
totally interrupted immunization during 1992. To prevent the increase in home deliveries



Annex A
Page 6 of 10
generated by the inability of users to pay for hospital delivery, with its damaging effects in the
perinatal, neonatal and maternal mortality, the recently implemented basic package of care
includes prenatal care, deliveries and care for children under one year of age in the list of
services to be provided free to all women and children. With the support of international
organizations a "Well-Start" Breast-Feeding Program is under implementation. Rooming-in is
now practiced in major maternity's and training modules in newbom resuscitation, thermal
control, diarrheal diseases (DDR), acute respiratory infections (ARI), breastfeeding and pediatric
neurology have been prepared and are being disseminated. An Interministerial Commission for
the Protection of Women and Children was created.
8.     Planned Activities: (a) inclusion of family planning in the basic package of care; (b)
development of protocols and training modules in perinatal care for obstetricians, pediatricians,
family planners, neonatal care specialists, family practitioners, nurses and midwives; (c)
development of a residency program in Tbilisi in Obstetrics and Pediatrics; (d) implementation
of an integrated perinatal care program in Kutaisi, merging the Maternity with the Pediatric
Hospital, developing pre-natal care in outreach facilities and modernizing communications and
transportation in the referral system; (e) creation of a task-force for Child Survival, under the
Commission for Protection of Women and Children.
9.     Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) enlarging the basic package to family planning, by
March, 1996; (b) official adoption of protocols of care and guidelines for women's and children's
service organization by March, 1997; (c) starting the Tbilisi residency program in Obstetrics and
Gynecology by September, 1997; (d) reducing maternal mortality by 50% by July 2000; (e)
reducing infant and perinatal mortality by 50% in Tbilisi and Kutaisi by July 2000; (f) reducing
infant and perinatal mortality by 30 % in the entire country, by July 2000.
(c) Realignment of the Ministry of Health
10.    Major Initiatives Currently Underway. A pharmaceutical law was approved (Decree
of Cabinet of Ministers number 388). Regulation of this law is under discussion dnd will be
published soon. The first round of registration of providers for the purpose of future accreditation
was mostly completed in September, 1995 except those in far peripheries and under different
ministries. In the licensing of health professionals, the first expert panels were set up for the
definition of standards for medical practice and medical specialties' examination.
11.    Planned  Activities: (a) reorganization  of the administrative  structure  of the
Phannaceutical Department and of the Pharrnaceutical Agency, where four major activities will
be performed: registration of products, quality assurance, inspection and information;
(b)licensing and accreditation of enterprises involved in designing and repairing of medical
equipment and items, implementation period - June, 1996; (c) licensing of medical equipment in-
service staff, implementation period - October, 1996; (d) developing of data base and State
Register for medical equipment and items, implementation period - May, 1997; (e) developing of
strategic planning system for upgrading of medical technologies in Health care, implementation
period - March, 1996; (f) developing of competitive bidding system for procurement guidelines
for federal and municipal medical programs, implementation period - March, 1996; (g)
improvement of the capacity of the Laboratory for Quality Control; (h) performing extended
registration of health care facilities with site visits and inspections and building-up a data basis
on physical health resources; (i) licensing providers to perform clinical activities and creation of



Annex A
Page 7 of 10
a data basis with registered doctors and nurses; (j) preparation and approval of standards for
generalists and GP's practice and other specialists; (k) support to health care institutions willing
to develop internal quality assurance programs.
12.   Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) Publication of an executive order of the MoH on the
structure of the Pharmaceutical Department by March, 1996; (b) licensing of 90% of
pharmaceutical storehouses and retailers by July, 1997; (c) publication of the first Georgian
Pharmnacopoeia by July, 1998; (d) first license issued to a physician by July, 1996; (e)
Parliamentary approval of the Law on Medical Education, including limits to new entrants and
rules for the accreditation of medical faculties and staff by March, 1996; (f) creation of the
Division for Medical and Nursing Licensing, in the National Center for Health Management
(NCHM) by April, 1996; (g) publication of a conceptual paper on the licensing of health
professionals by NCHM staff by July 1996; (h) licensing of 50% of Georgian physicians by
October, 1998.
(d) Downsizing Health Facilities
13.    Major Initiatives Currently Underway: With the support of the World Bank Municipal
Infrastructures Rehabilitation Project (MIRP) and the European Community Humanitarian
Organization (ECHO), a start has been made in the winterization of several hospital facilities.
The rayonal hospital in Chiatura could be a good example of Bank's seed money to start an
operation that will be co-financed by the local municipality. However, this effort needs to be
combined with the down-sizing of the public sector health facilities. The following initiatives
will contribute to the downsizing: (a) the health sector privatization program, started in
September 1995, will dramatically reduce the quantity of the public sector facilities and staff; (b)
the bed needs quick assessment, prepared by the World Bank for the Georgia Health Project,
provides a rough indication of the excessive number of beds, and serves as a guide for down-
sizing; (c) the separation between financiers and providers, initiated on August 10, 1995, pays
hospitals and polyclinics on the basis of services provided rather then supply norms. This creates
incentives to reduce excessive and inefficient services by closing or merging; (d) the enormous
amount of needed rehabilitation and the preliminary cost-assessment performed by'the World
Bank call attention to the high cost of rehabilitation and to the need to target scarce resources to
those institutions that are reducing their dimension, merging themselves and adopting energy and
other input efficiency measles.
14.   Planned Activities: (a) the merging of Kutaisi Maternity and Pediatric Hospital into a
single building, to be partially rehabilitated by the World Bank Project, will serve as a
demonstration of scaling-down and rationalizing facilities and modernizing hospital
management; (b) a similar experience will be performed in Tbilisi, in a location to be designated,
where a joint residency program for maternal and pediatric care will take place; (c) the
preparation of standards for the reprogramming, rehabilitation and maintenance of health
facilities, under a possible second World Bank project.
15.   Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) the number of active and stated hospital beds will be
reduced by 15% by December 1996 and by 25% by December 1997; (b) the rehabilitation and
equipment modemization of Kutaisi Pediatric Hospital, including the Kutaisi Matemity will be
completed by July 2000, according to a schedule prepared through the Georgia Health Project;
(^) The revised bed needs assessment for the whole country and the publication of new



Annex A
Page 8 of 10
programming and construction norms for health facilities will be done by the July 1998, in useful
time to prepare of a second Georgia health project.
(e) Human Resources Development
16.    Major Initiatives Currently Underway. The National Center for Health Management
(NCHM) was established in March 1995 with a mandate to coordinate medical and nursing
education, create a system of licensing and accreditation for professionals and training
institutions, develop post-graduate medical and nursing curricula, create standards for
specialization, and develop health sciences research. The Center has spent most of the year
performning institutional development tasks. Five new heads of department have been recently
appointed, and the following has been accomplished: new legislation on medical education was
drafted creating an accreditation system for schools and limiting first year entrants; new
residency programs were created for three needed specialties, Pneumology, Radiology and
Psychiatry, following European Union (EU) guidelines; and teams of experts were appointed to
review curricula in sixty medical specialties. The NCHM was also instrumental in the first steps
to create an association of Public Health and an association of General Practitioners. The
association of Family Doctors has been established recently. The decision was made to locate
the Center for Continuing Education (CCE) in the NCHM and a functional program was
prepared around four major areas of training: Health Care Management, Family Practice, Public
Health and Nursing. A coordinator and a team of experts will be appointed for each area.
17.    Planned Activities: (a) the panels of experts will prepare curricula and licensing
procedures for all medical specialties, by October 1996; (b) legislation on accreditation of
medical and nursing training institutions, licensing and accreditation and limits to new entrants
in schools will be presented to the new Parliament; (c) residency programns will be prepared for
all medical and nursing specialties; (d) heads and technical experts teams will be appointed for
each one of the four continuing education areas; (e) the design of the refurbishment of the CCE
facilities and the procurement of civil works and equipment will be prepared; (f) a work program
for the first two years of the CCE's activity will be prepared.
18.    Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) the NCHM will be full operational by January 1997;
(b) an architectural brief for the refurbishment of the CCE will be ready by March 1996, to be
followed by procurement of civil works and goods, in order to have rehabilitation finished by
December 1996; (c) the first training course in hospital administration will start by January 1997,
the first training activities in Public Health and General Practice and the first post-graduate
training course in Nursing will start by April 1997; (e) the evaluation. of the first two years of
activity of the NCHM will take place by December 1999.
(f) Privatizatlon and Self-Management
19.    Major Initiatives Currently Underway. The privatization of health care institutions
will be performed by the ministry in charge of privatization. It will take 7% of the gross income,
one per cent will be the estimated preparation cost and the State Health Fund will collect 92% of
the income generated. The first phase of privatization will target 714 institutions (Group A:
dental clinics and pharmacies). The announcement and the list of institutions to be privatized has
been already published. Auctions will start by October 1995, after allowing 20 days for staff

applications. The second group of 500 institutions (Group B: polyclinics) will be announced by
November, 1995. The privatization of the third group of institutions (Group C: small hospitals)
will be announced by April-March, 1996.
20.    Benchmarks of Policy Action. (a) 50/a of the institutions listed under Group A will
become private by July, 1996, and 80% by July 1997; (b) 40% of institutions listed under Group
B will become private by October 1996 and 70% by October 1997; (c) 25% of institutions listed
under Group C will become private by December 1996 and 50% by December 1997; (d)
institutions unable to get bids for privatization will be liquidated by July, 1998.
(g) Basic Package and Targeting the Poor
21.    Major Initiatives Currently Underway. The basic package has been implemented
since August 10, 1995. Simultaneously, the municipal basic package was launched, financed by a
municipal contribution proportional to the number of inhabitants, to cover acute care activities.
The implementation started in the three major cities and corresponding regions of Tbilisi, Kutaisi
and Batumi, and will cover all the twelve health regions before December, 1996. Tbilisi Medical
Academy Clinic and several specialty hospitals in Tbilisi have set aside a total of 275 beds to
treat the most vulnerable groups: single unemployed mothers with three or more children; single
pensioners with monthly pensions below 6 Lari and single invalids. Other institutions, like the
Veterans Hospitals are financed by the State to continue providing care to their clients. Besides,
together with Ministry of Social Welfare, criteria for identification of vulnerable groups has been
improved and all these people will receive special certifications and get free medical care in any
medical institution.
22.   Planned Activities: (a) a revision of the basic package is expected to be performed at the
end of the first six months of implementation; (b) to avoid the stigma effect, the Government is
planning a quota system for eve hospital to provide care for patients unable to pay for their
health bills outside the basic package of care; (c) improved means testing with a World Bank
supported household income survey planned for 1996.
23.   Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) a revision of the basic package occurs by February
1996, and annual revisions take place in August 1996 and August 1997; (b) a second willingness-
to-pay survey, replicating the one prepared by UNICEF in August-September, 1995 is initiated in
September, 1998, to provide information on poverty evolution and its implications on health.
(h) Modernization of the Health Financing System
24.   Major Initiatives Currently Underway. The State Health Fund was created and
installed in August 1995. It has been collecting financial resources from the State Budget and
payroll taxes, taxes on harmful products and will collect income from privatization in the coming
future. In September, 1995, it will begin to pay for hospital invoices presented through the
banking system. It has been decided in prospect to transform the State Health Fund into State
Medical Insurance Company.
25.   Planned Activities: (a) for monitoring the financial sustainability of the. Fund, baseline
information on health service expenditure needs to be collected for August 1995 and compared

with corresponding data from six and twelve months later; (b) creation of a basic financial
information system on the Health Fund; (c) statistical assessment of the provider's invoicing

26.   Benchmarks of Policy Action: (a) a basic and computerized financial information
system will be designed and in place in the State Health Fund by October 31, 1996; (b) the first
statistical assessment of payments profile, done by the State Health Fund, will be performed by
April 1996, to be repeated by August, 1996 and August, 1997; (c) by the end of 1996 the State
Health Fund will be transformed into State Medical Insurance Company; (d) Regional Health
Funds will be operational in the regions of Tbilisi, Rustavi and Batumi by January, 1996, and in
the remaining health regions by the end of 1996; (e) a hospital accounting plan, implemented on
a pilot basis in one region, will provide the first real costs of care by March, 1997, in three
additional regions by March, 1998, and in all the health regions by March 2000; (f) the initial
health financing infornation system for the Fund will be enlarged and transformed into a
management information system, to become operational in the Tbilisi Region by March, 1999.

HEALTH STATUS: KEY ISSUES AND HEALTH DETERMNINANTS
A. Demography
1.    The population of Georgia is currently about 5.4 million with an average density of 73 persons
per km . Fifty-six percent of the population is urbanized and one third of the poulation lives in the four
largest cities: Tbilisi (1,300,000), Kutaisi (230,000), Batumi (200,000) and Rustavi (180,000). Georgia
is a heterogenous society, comprising ethinic Georgians (70% of the population in 1989); Armenians
(8 %), Russians (6%) and Azeris (6%). Recent civil wars left almost 5 % of the population as refugees
(270,000).
2.     The population had been relatively unstable over the last decade. The civil wars and declining
socio-economic status have resulted in emigration and a dramatic fall in fertility (from 89,000 births in
1989 to 64,000 in 1993) which, when accompanied by increasing mortality, have contributed to an overall
decline in population since 1991. Although the Soviet Union had undergone the demographic transition,
in Georgia the elderly (65 and older) made up only 9.6 percent of the population while children (under 16)
were 24.5 percent.
B. Health Status
3.    Information on recent trends on health status is difficult to obtain. The statistics are neither
reliable nor consistent. This is characterized by contradicting information about the same indicator
obtained from different sources, and low levels and unrealistic decline in several health status indicators.
For example, very low cancer mortality figures compared to other countries are a likely indication of
registration and/or reporting problems. The same applies to accidents and poisonings. Information gaps
make it difficult to evaluate the deterioration or improvement of health status, to plan and evaluate public
health programs, estimate the costs of basic benefits package, etc. The following are likely to be the
causes of unreliable and inconsistent information: (a) social and economic disruptions; (b) duplication of
data collection and data processing; and (c) information was not used for decision-making.
4.     From the available information, the health status of Georgia's people appeared to compare well
with the rest of the countries of the FSU. There were significant improvements between 1950 and
1970. Infant mortality was greatly reduced, and preventable communicable diseases were rare.
However, since mid 1970s, like elsewhere in the FSU, the health status stagnated and started to
deteriorate in late 1980s. This dynamic is partly due to emerging economic difficulties, but also a
result of failing to recognize new health problems, in particular deteriorating adult health. Socio-
economic disruption and civil war associated with the disruption of the Soviet Union have only sped up
the deterioration of health status. Old problems of communicable diseases have re-emerged, and
combine now with new health challenges, aggravated by the society's complete inability to respond.
5.     Liffp=tany at birth in 1990 was 76.1 years for women and 68.7 years for men. For both
sexes, life expectancy was higher (except for Lithuania in the case of women) than in any other FSU
republic. There are no data available on the recent dynamics in life expectancy. The recent rise in
overall mortality and comparative data about recent trends in other FSU countries make it reasonable to



ANNEX B
Page 2 of 13
assume declining life expectancy in Georgia. I Main causes of death are cardio-vascular diseases,
neoplasms, and deaths from external causes (Tables 14, Figure 1).
6.     The following are the key issues related to health status which Georgia faces today: (i) a
deterioration of maternal and child health; (ii) recent re-emergence and increase of communicable diseases
such as tuberculosos, AIDS, and STDs; and (iii) a hidden pattern of non-communicable diseases including
high and rising cardio-vascular mortality, neoplasms, and accidents and poisonings.
Deteriorating maternal and child health.
7.     Infant mortality is more than twice as high in Georgia than in high income countries. In 1994, the
official figure was 17.8/1,000 live births. Adjusting this figure for the WHO definition of IMR, the
estimate would be 21.4/1,000. Given the problems with the credibility of information, this figure can still
be an underestimate. IMR improved remarkably over the last 20 years (38 percent), but remains still more
than two times higher than higher income countries. The WHO target for IMR for Europe is 20/1,000.
IMR showed a net increase of 13 percent during the last three years. (Table 5).
8.     In 1992, the main causes of infant mortality were perinatal conditions (5.6/1,000 live births, 38
percent of infant deaths), respiratory diseases (2.8, 19 percent of infant deaths), and infectious diseases
(1.8, 12 percent of infant deaths). Twenty-seven percent of infant deaths were classified under 'other and
ill-defined' causes, suggesting poor diagnosis and registration practices, as well as that the main causes of
death may be even more important than stated. Two-fifths of infant deaths occurred in the first seven days
after birth, and from these, 80 percent occurred in the first three days. These statistics clearly indicate
inadequate health services for the perinatal period (Tables 6 and 7).
9.     IMR is higher in towns than in villages. Gori, Batumi and Caducei had an IMR respectively of
56, 35, and 24 in the period from 1991 to 1993. Reducing the rates in Gori and Caducei to the national
average would reduce IMR at the national level by one-sixth.
10.    Incidence of acute respiratory infections (ARI) and diarrheal diseases is very high, according to
MoH data. Incidence of diseases that are a target of the Expanded Program on Immunization (EPI) (Table
4) have been increasing lately. Outbreaks of measles (180 cases) and diphtheria (+ 100) occured in
Adjaria in 1994. There are cases of diphtheria all over the country and across all age groups, suggesting
long-term breaks in immunization coverage.
11.    In 1991, only 30 percent of newborns were breastfed until the sixth week of life, 22 percent at 3
months, and 3 percent at six months. There is malnutrition among under-five children, as well as micro-
nutrient deficiencies.
12.    Maternal mortality was 39 per 100,000 live-births in the period from 1990 to 1993 (Table 8). This
indicator is 2.5 times higher than the WHO/EURO target (15/100,000 births), and 4 to 20 times higher
than in high-income countries. As the absolute number of deaths is small   (an average of 31
cases per year in the period 1990-93), it is difficult to interpret annual variations and to predict future
ratios. However, maternal mortality is expected to further increase as a result of the rise in home
1 In Russia, male life expectancy declined from 63.5 years in 1991 to 62.0 years in 1992 attributed mainly to the
increase in mortality among adult male population; the dynamics of female mortality have been less dramatic but
many age-specific death rates rose by more than 50% during the same period.



ANNEX B
Page 3 of 13
deliveries (representing almost 10 percent of all deliveries in 1992, and probably more in 1993 and 1994),
and the diminishing capacity of health services to deal with delivery complications.
13.   The main causes of maternal death are hemorrhage (33 percent of all maternal deaths), infection
(15 percent), and pulmonary embolism (12.5 percent). Abortion and its complications accounted for
about 10 percent of maternal deaths in the period 1990-93, yet of the 22 deaths that occurred in 1993,
none was due to this cause. The two major causes indicate again poor quality of health services.
14.   The abortion ratio to live births varies with the source reporting, from 0.4 to 0.7 (for reference, in
the UK it is 0.2), but both the Ministry of Health and the Zhordania Institute of Human Reproduction
report declines in the number of abortions in the period from 1990 to 1994 (Table 9). Abortion by
vacuum-aspiration (VA) and modern contraception are available in Tbilisi, at the Institute of Human
Reproduction, but abortion by dilatation and curettage (D&C) is still four times more common in the
country than abortion by VA. Illegal abortion (performed outside the health services) is reported to be
unavailable. An abortion costs between 20,000 and 50,000 rubles (approximately US$7-17), while the
insertion of an IUD costs 30,000 rubles (US$10). Replacement of D&C by VA may not constitute a
problem, as soon as equipment is available. However, replacement of abortion by free, modern methods
of contraception may face the opposition of physicians now charging fees for abortions.
15.   Contraception prevalence also varies with the source reporting, from 8 to 35 percent in the
country, and from 25 to 55 percent in Tbilisi. Furthermore, according to UNICEF (1994), there is a rise
in fertility among 15-19 years-old, as well as a rise in extra-marital births (from nil in 1975 to almost a
fifth of all births in 1990), suggesting a need for family planning promotion among younger groups. In
private pharmacies, a box of oral contraceptives costs US $0.35 (1 month) to $1.70 (3 months), and one
condom costs between 2 and 4 cents.
Increase in Communicable Diseases
16.   Vaccine preventable diseases. Since October 1993, 292 cases of diphtkr*a with 42 deaths have
been registered. Both children and adults have been affected. Only sporadic cases of diphtheria have
occurred in the 1980s. Since 1990s, the incidence of pertussis has increased to 5.02/100,000. The
incidence of mneasle in 1993 was 3.5 times higher than in 1992. The outbreak of epidemics can be
attributed to deterioration of inimunization coverage. Tuberculosis is also an increasing problem (see para
18). Lack of vaccines, distribution problems, and the appropriate choice of vaccine type have contributed
to the inadequate immunization coverage. See tables 10 andl 1.
17.   Acute respiratory infections (AR) contribute 43% of child mortality rates under 1 year old.
Diarrhoeal diseases are another main contributor to children's morbidity and mortality. Due to poor
diagnostic capacity, about 33 % of cases remain unsolved. It is estimated that 57% of mortality from
diarrhoeal diseases could be avoided by timely and adequate medical attention.
18.   Tuberculosis (TB). Recent trends show a sharp increase in the incidence and prevalence of TB.
The incidence was 42.6/100,000 in 1993, going up from 28.3/100,000 in 1990  (Table 12).
Prevalence was 246.3/100,00 in 1993 compared to 140.7/100,000 in 1990. The mortality rate of
5.3/100,000 is considerably higher than that in West-European countries where in 1990, the highest rate
of 2.8/100,000 was in Portugal.2 The existing Georgian rate is likely to be underestimated, as home
2 Source: Tubercle and Lung Disease (1994) 75, 400-416.



ANNEX B
Page 4 of 13
deaths may not have been reported and properly registered. Incidence of TB-associated meningitis, an
indicator of the quality of early detection and effective treatment of tuberculosis, has increased from 10
cases in 1990 to 32 cases in 1993. Worsening socio-economic conditions, internal migration and the low
standard of living conditions of displaced population (family TB), drug-resistant strains, and the collapse
of the formal health care delivery system are cited as principal causes of the worsening TB situation.
Immunization coverage of children that used to be in the range of 95-98% dropped to 78% in 1991, 0% in
1992, and 80% in 1993. The Mantoux test for early detection (formerly routine practice in the FSU) is no
longer performed because of its high cost.
19.    WHO is supporting Georgia in the control of the TB problem. Georgia has adopted a 5-point
policy program proposed by the WHO consisting of the following components: (1) Government's
conmmitment to national TB program; (2) passive case detection; (3) standardized definitions of TB cases
for treatment options; (4) system of regular drug-supply; and, (5) standardized recording and registration
procedures.
20.    Sexually-transmitted diseases (STDs). Incidence of sexually-transmitted diseases (STDs) has
been increasing lately, with Georgia having the highest incidence of syphilis (13.4 per 100,000 population
in 1992) among the republics of the former Soviet Union. It is estimated that the incidence of syphilis in
1994 was 1,800 cases, an increase of 100% from 1993. The incidence of other STDs (gonorrhea,
chlamydiosis, mycoplasmosis) show an increasing trend over the past 3 years. Worsening socio-economic
conditions have created an increase in skin diseases (scabies and mycoses).
21.   AIDS. By January 1, 1995, 18 HIV-positive individuals were registered. AIDS has been
diagnosed in 4 cases, 2 of whom have died from AIDS-related diseases.3
22.    Other. During one week in November-December 1994, there was an outbreak of 100 cases of
botulism. The source was imported smoked fish from Armenia. This outbreak reflects relaxed regulation
for food markets. An increase has been observed in the incidence of Hepatitis-B in the population age
group 15-35 years old. This increase is being attributed to increase in drug-abuse. An estimated 200,000-
250,000 people use drugs. A survey performed by the national AIDS center estimates that 72 % of
intravenous drug users occasionally/regularly use non-sterilized needles.4
Hidden Pattern of Non-Communicable Diseases
23.    Cardio-vascular mortality is considerably higher than in developed Western countries but less
than in other FSU republics (see table 4). Cardio-vascular diseases (CVD) accounted for 72 % of total
mortality in 1993, represented by an age-adjusted mortality rate of 730 per 100,000 population. Cardio-
vascular mortality is up from 65% of total mortality, or 544.2/100,000 in 1990. The MoH has questioned
the reliability of official figures citing the problems associated with reporting (validity of death
certificates). Nevertheless, the trend correlates very well with similar dynamics in Russia where cardio-
vascular mortality increased from 617.4 to 768.9 per 100,000 between 1990 and 1993. Socio-economic
crisis, deterioration of health services, lack of drugs and related high out-of-pocket costs are likely
contributors to the recent increase in this mortality rate, in particular affecting the system's ability to cope
with chronic cardio-vascular diseases and responses to acute illness episodes.
3 Source: National AIDS Prevention and Control Programme
4 Source: National AIDS Prevention and Control Programme



AN'NEXB
Page 5 of 13
24.   Neoplasms. The official statistics appear to be unreliable and inconsistent, reflecting declining
official cancer mortality rates. Lung cancer for men and breast cancer for women have sharply increased
during the last two decades. Smoking and high concentration of carcinogenic agents in many mills and
factories in Georgia are likely factors in oncogenesis.5 A perceived problem related to female
reproductive system cancers in Georgia can be attributed to insufficient secondary prevention (lack of
screening, early detection, and effective primary treatment). See tables 13 and 14, Figure 3.
25.    Accidents and Poisonings. The official mortality data from accidents and poisonings are likely to
be underestimated. Given the traffic patterns and the lack of enforcement of safety standard procedures,
the resulting mortality and disability have to be close to the magnitude of other FSU countries (Table 11).
Reporting and registration problems may contribute to the underestimation for two reasons: (i) mortality
from traumatism is registered according to anatomical location, not by cause; and (ii) there is no link
between the Ministry of Interior's register of traffic accidents and the MoH information on related
morbidity and hospital and clinic admissions.
26.    Psychiatric Morbidity. Incidence and prevalence of psycho-somnatic diseases have been stable: in
1993, there were 15,208 cases of schizophrenia and 8,632 cases of endogenous psychotic conditions.
New problems in psychiatric morbidity are post-traumatic stress syndrome and neurotic disturbances,
prompted by the worsening socio-economic situation and wars. The exact figures are not known because
professional attention is being offered anonymously, because of cultural reasons and considerable internal
migration of population.
C. Health Determinants
27.    Preker and Feachem6 suggest that the health gap between Central and Eastern European countries
and Western countries may be partly due to lifestyle risk factors, and partly to differences in wealth and
associated socio-economic factors, environmental pollution and occupational risks, and deficiencies in the
preventive and curative health care services. The mortality gap between Georgia and high-income
countries may steeply increase in the near future, due to aggravation of all mentioned factors.
28.    Socio-econonic status. GNP per capita decreased 48 percent from 1991 to 1992, with 85 percent
of families living under the Georgian poverty line in 1993, according to the Socio-Economical Infonnation
Committee. In 1993, the average monthly salary was US$ 0.66. There is evidence, today, that many poor
people do not have access to health services. High out-of-pocket costs of health services and drugs
discourage people from using health services. The 270,000 thousand refugees are a particularly
disadvantaged social group. Almost three-quarters of the displaced population have no income. Famnilies
suffer from stress, poor living conditions in temporary housing, and organic diseases like family
tuberculosis and parasites are common. Access barriers to health services (informal fees) have deferred
many from seeking medical attention for their health problems.
29.    Extended family plays an important part in Georgian society, and has proved to be one of the
coping mechanisms with harsh-socio economic realities. This is one of the reasons why symptoms of
S Source: National Cancer Control Programme. Oncology Research Institute.
6 Source: A.S. Preker and R. Feachem, 'Searching for the Silver Bullet: Market Mechanisms in the Health
Sector in Central and Eastern Europe," presented during the Conference on Health Care Reform Strategies in
Central Europe, Prague, November 10-12, 1993.



ANNEX B
Page 6 of 13
social febrility like alcoholism, juvenile crime, divorce, suicide and self-inflicted injury have occurred in
much lesser scale than elsewhere in the FSU countries during recent socio-economic decline.
30.    Environment. There is little information on environmental impact on health in Georgia. The
country suffered from some fall-out of the Chemobyl disaster, and this has been blamed for significant
increases in the incidence of pregnancy anemia, diseases of the endocrine system, diabetes mellitus, blood
diseases and malignant tumors. No evidence has been produced to substantiate these claims. Tbilisi has a
serious nitrogen dioxide, sulfur anhydride, lead and cadmium air-pollution. The collapse of public
services has resulted in uncollected garbage and compromised water and sewage systems which pose a
potential health hazard for the population.
31.   In the past, drinking water was widely accessible. In 1990, 6 percent of water samples failed to
meet chemical standards, and 16 percent failed biological standards. Since then, the situation has
worsened, mainly because of poor maintenance and lack of electricity to operate pumps. Western Georgia
appears to be the most affected region. Reduced access to continuous and safe water has been partly
responsible for increased incidence of water born diseases, including diarrhea, dysentery, hepatitis A, and
other infections associated with lack of hygiene, such as scabies.
32.    Nutrition. The diet during the FSU era was rich in sugars and starches. About one-third of
population was reported to be overweight. Since 1991, a decline in quality and quantity of Georgian diet
has been observed. Consumption of bread, potatoes and oil has remained almost constant in absolute
terms, whereas consumption of animal protein and dairy products has declined sharply. In 1993, daily
consumption of total and especially animal proteins was below recommended standards (57 g of total
protein compared to 88 g standard, and 9 g of animal protein compared to a standard of 46 g).
33.    Micro-nutrients. About 1.5 million people live in mountainous areas suffering from endemic
iodine deficiency. No complete data exist on the incidence of iron deficiency disorders. Small-scale studies
by the State Medical University suggest that up to 60 percent of children in endemic areas have
pathological conditions of various degrees. Economic hardships do not allow the import of iodized salt
from Armenia nor iodine tablets from Russia.
34.    Lifestyle choices. There are no recent data about cigarette consumption, but it is assumed that this
practice is highly prevalent among men (60-80 percent), and increasing among women. In the 1960s,
78.5% males and 7.5% females smoked; in the 1980s the respective figures were 68-72% for males and
8-16% for females.7. Georgians are smoking United States brands, that are aggressively marketed both in
urban and rural areas. It is also assumed that drug addiction is increasing, specially among young
people.
35.    Health system. The ability of the health care system to respond to the country's worsening health
situation has been seriously compromised. The access to health services is not universal, but depends on
the ability to pay informal fees for medical services, drugs and other essential supplies. Lack of resources
and inadequate management practices have resulted in deteriorated buildings and non-functioning
equipment. Standards for professional expertise are lacking, and patients' perception of the quality of care
received is low. The health care system has been oriented towards curative care, infrastructure and
professional expertise for cost-effective primary care interventions is lacking. More detailed analysis of
the health care system is presented in another annex.
7 Source: Oncology Research Institute and Institute of Cardiology data.



ANNEX B
Page 7 of 13
D. Conclusions
36.    There appears no room for the health system to contribute significantly to the improvement of the
health status of the Georgian population. This should be done by ensuring access to basic health services
for all social groups, reorienting the system towards cost-effective primary care interventions, increasing
efficiency of the system through proper incentives and improved management practices, and ensuring
standard quality for health care provision in Georgia.
37.   In order to design, implement and evaluate interventions intended for the improvement of health
status in Georgia, several actions need to be taken. There is a need to consolidate and improve data
collection, processing and utilization functions; capacity to control non-communicable disease needs to be
enhanced allowing better understanding of morbidity and mortality patterns and link them to risk factors.
This would provide adequate estimates of the burden of disease in society, and help to design effective
health promotion programs.
38.    The recently-established National Health Management Center (NHMC) has an important objective
to consolidate and improve the national health information system. This effort is intended to coordinate
the activities of the following institutes: Institute of Informatics, Institute of Medical Information and
National Medical Library, Department of Statistics in the MoH, and the Sanitary-Epidemiology System.
