HEALTH PROMOTION AND DISEASE PREVENTION'
A. Objectives
1.           Worsening adult health is a major factor in the decline of the health status of the
population of Georgia. Current trends in lifestyles and changes in social and physical environment will
contribute to the increase of health problems and deaths markedly by the year 2000 if vigorous measures
in promoting and protecting health are not applied. More than 430,000 disability adjusted life years
(DALYs) are lost per year to ischemic heart disease, lung cancer, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease,
cerebro-vascular disorders, and traffic accidents.
2.            There is an urgent need to establish health promotion and disease prevention programs.
Community-based inter-sectoral and vertical program activities to reduce health risks and to create
availability of healthy choices and environment are called for. Special attention should be paid to school
and youth programs. Estimated savings from the planned health promotion and disease prevention
programs are nearly 200,000 DALYs at the cost of US$ 2.83 per DALY saved.2
3.            The health promotion and disease prevention sub-component seeks to:
(1) establish sustainable conditions for health promotion in Georgia, this will include policy development
and creation of appropriate structures for administration, funding, program activities, research and
evaluation; and (2) launch immediately programs in priority areas including reduction of tobacco
consumption, cardiovascular disease prevention, traffic and occupational injury prevention, reduction of
alcohol consumption and family planning.
B. Supportive policies for health promotion
4.            Long term and sustainable commitment. The Government of Georgia shares with its
citizens the responsibility to promote and protect health and recognizes that the major benefits from
health promotion will come from longer-term investments. Legislation is called for in many areas such
as environmental control, food policy, and production and sales of harmful goods. In order to promote
inter-sectoral coordination, a National Health Council should be formed, with representatives from all
relevant ministries. In order to ensure sufficient allocation for health promotion activities and encourage
innovative community initiatives, a Health Promotion Program (HPP) has been established. Government
funding for the HPP will be mandatory and independent from political changes. Resources should be
allocated on a rational basis to programs that address issues of greatest public health importance using
strategies based on evidence.
5.            Recognition of the social, economic and environmental detenninants of health. The
Government of Georgia recognizes the impact of access to education, inequality of income, deficiencies
in housing and dangers in the physical environment on health. National health goals and objectives will
be analyzed and documented. A comprehensive health promotion strategy will be developed and adopted
1 The sub-component draws on the work of Dr. Matti Rajala, consultant, and his Georgian counterparts Drs. Ramaz
Urushadze and Archil Kobaladze.
2 See Annex D for more details on the Burden of Disease and Cost-Effectiveness Analysis, by Costa and
Gouveia.



Annex C. I
Page 2 of 6
involving actions directed not only to individuals, but also to the social and economic conditions that
enable people to make health choices. This work will be coordinated by the MoH's Department of
Public Health, and then discussed and approved by the National Health Council. Wide participation of
all relevant sectors will be used to create, publish and disseminate A National Health Strategy document.
The impact of different public policies on health should be systematically assessed and made explicit (e.g.
taxation, housing, transportation). Analytical tools, such as an health impact assessment, will be
developed.
6.           The role of the Regional Health Departments is crucial in the development and
coordination of health promotion activities. As the Government becomes increasingly decentralized,
Districts may provide the best opportunity to develop and demonstrate practical models of health
promotion. Considering the limited availability of resources and expertise, a step-wise model should be
applied. Pilot projects in a number of regions and districts should be launched. Partnership with non-
governmental organizations in policy development, program development and delivery will also be
encouraged.
C. Sub-Component Description
Department of Public Health
7.           A Department of Public Health (DPH) has been established at the Ministry of Health.
The Department has responsibility for monitoring and analyzing the health status of the population (both
communicable and non-communicable diseases), managing the epidemiological surveillance and rapid
response systems, advising the Government on public health policy issues, coordinating inter-sectoral
health programs through the National Health Council, coordinating regional public health offices, and
coordinating and analyzing the impact of health promotion and disease prevention programs. A Division
for Health Promotion and Disease Prevention (DHPDP) has been established in the new Department of
Public Health to provide leadership for health promotion and disease prevention in Georgia. The DPH
will also oversee the Center for Health Information and Statistics, Center for Disease Control, and a
number of other research institutes.
8.           The DPH will have the following functions and responsibilities.
a.    Comprehensive analysis of needs and resources. MoH, advised by DPH will
prepare a national overview of health issues. The DHPDP analyzes the range of
possible health promotion strategies, and assesses resources available in order to
shape health promotion policies and develop priorities for action.
b.    National monitoring of health behaviour. For sound policy-making and
assessment of health gains, systematic monitoring of health behaviour should be
started. Planning and implementation will be DHPDP's responsibility.
c.    Policy analysis and development. DHPDP/DPH advises MoH to develop and
propose new policies within the health system, and assess the health-impact of
policies, and propose modifications when needed.
d.    Alliance building for health. DHPDP supports the development of partnerships
for health strengthening policy development and health promotion action at all
levels. A national multi-sectoral committee will be established to coordinate and



Annex C. 1
Page 3 of 6
support health development. Special attention should be paid to youth and
schools.
e.     Program support to serve regional and local activities. DHPDP will support
regions, districts, community groups and practitioners either by the direct
provision of financial (e.g. grants) and technical (e.g., information and
consultations) resources, and by co-ordinating decentralized program
implementation.
f.    Research and evaluation. Collection of information on the effectiveness of
various health programs and strategies is essential to review work programs as
well as making this information available. DHPDP will implement an ongoing
evaluation of the program activities and will support research in this field.
g.     Human resources development. DPH and DHPDP will support education and
training, and ensure access to information on best practice. They will also
encourage rapid dissemination of practical experience.
h.    International relations. To ensure the exchange of knowledge and experience
active international collaboration is needed. Georgia will participate in the work
of WHO's European Committee for Health Promotion Development. DHPDP
will assume national membership in the International Union for Health
Promotion and Education.
9.          Trained professionals at the national, regional and local levels are required to plan and
implement health promotion programs. Foreign technical assistance in behavioral, political and social
sciences and health economics will help to develop multi-disciplinary technical expertise in the DPH,
where the first priority for training will be given. In the second phase, aided by the Center for
Continuing Education, graduate, post-graduate and in-service training will be provided to professionals at
the regional and local levels. Universities and health professional associations will also have a crucial
role to play in health promotion.
10.           Access to reliable epidemiological and health services data is required to monitor the
health of the population and assess the progress of the various health promotion activities. In
collaboration with the WHO, the project will support the creation of a health information system. The
system will regularly monitor health status, health-related behaviours, and social and economic
determinants of health, and issue annual reports to the MoH and the public.
Priority Disease Prevention And Health Promotion Programs
11.          In order to promote inter-sectoral collaboration and local initiatives in health promotion,
a Health Promotion Program (HPP) has been established in Georgia as part of the Project. The Program
will be financed by a mandatory allocation from the State Health Fund (1 % of annual income from the
government budget, payroll tax, and tax on hazardous products) as well as by proceeds from the IDA
credit.
12.           Programs in cardio-vascular disease prevention, anti-smoking, injury prevention, and
family planning will start imnmediately under the project. These program have the potential to save
200,000 DALYs by the year 2005. They are implementable, will produce measurable results, and will



Annex C. I
Page 4 of 6
provide health promotion with a high profile. Given the scarcity of resources, the programs will target
areas with the potential for the greatest impact, such as schools.
13.           Cardiovascular disease prevention. Cardiovascular disease (CVD) mortality is
considerably higher than in Western Europe and North America, but lower than in other FSU republics.
CVD mortality is by far the most important cause of death. Although the reliability of the available data
is questionable, the trend is similar to Russia where CVD mortality increased from 617 to 769 per
100,000 between 1990 to 1993. In Georgia, CVD represented 72% of total mortality in 1993.
Deterioration of the curative services may have contributed to the increase. In the long run, however,
preventive measures will provide the only sustainable solution to the problem. Between 1996 and 1999,
the project's health promotion and disease prevention programs target a 2% yearly reduction in incidence
and mortality rates from ichaemic heart disease and 3% yearly reduction in incidence and mortality rated
from cerebro-vascular diseases.
14.           Smoking, high blood cholesterol, high blood pressure and excess body weight are the
main risk-factors for high mortality and morbidity from circulatory diseases. These will be the areas for
the public health intervention under the project.
15.          National strategy for tobacco control. Tobacco smoking is the main risk factor for
cardio-vascular disease. In Georgia, more than 50% of men smoke and rates amongst both men and
women are increasing. As the markets in the industrialized western countries are shrinking, the trans-
national tobacco companies have started to exploit FSU and CEE markets, further increasing the
possibility someone will smoke. An effective tobacco control strategy will have the following
components: (i) legislation restricting advertising and promotion of tobacco products, restricting sales to
youngsters, and requiring warnings about the health effects of tobacco; (ii) economic policies which
include progressive significant increases in sales tax on tobacco; (iii) "Smoke free" public policies,
starting with the MoH, which emphasize that the right for smoke-free air is stronger than the right to
smoke; (iv) health education in schools, targeting people before they start smoking; and (v) counselling
and support for smokers who wish to stop. The project's target for the anti-smoking program is a 15%
decrease in smoking prevalence by 1999.
16.          Healthy nutrition. The Government needs to develop a national food policy which is
consistent with acceptable nutritional concepts and ensures the nutritional quality of food. Such a policy
should be developed by a multi-sectoral committee, comprising all Ministries whose policies affect
production, distribution, and food consumption such as Agriculture, Education, and Health. Animal
production policies should be assessed in order to ensure that existing incentives do not promote an
excess of saturated fats. High priority should be given to the production and availability of foods which
are low in fat, sugar, and salt. This may call for new legislation bearing on agriculture and food, to
ensure that it is compatible with the prevention of CVD. The MoH and Ministry of Education should
ensure that, as part of nutrition and health education for teachers and students, attention is paid to the
prevention of diet related chronic diseases. In the future, the DPH should start a campaign to promote a
healthy Georgian diet and monitor the nutritional intake of the population.
17.          Promoting physical activity. Lack of physical activity is a major determinant of CVD
and other chronic conditions such as osteoarthritis, osteoporosis, and obesity. Even moderately intense
physical activities, such as walking and dancing, can be valuable in helping to reduce the risk of CVD
and in preventing muscle wasting, which is the most common cause of disability in the elderly. DPH
should start an awareness building campaign among health professionals as well as among the general
public about the benefits of physical activity in its widest sense as a part of daily life. Children should be



Annex C. 1
Page 5 of 6
encouraged in schools and during their leisure-time to enjoy a wide range of sports, games and other
activities which they can continue after they have left school.
18.           Multiple risk factor prevention activities. The risk factors for CVD are synergistic, with
each extra risk factor multiplying the risk in a given individual. Structural, socio-economic, and psycho-
social factors determine the opportunities people have for choosing a healthy lifestyle. Schools provide
an important and effective arena for comprehensive health promotion efforts. Georgia should consider
joining the WHO Healthy Schools project to link healthy nutrition, non-smoking and physical activity in
the school. Health promotion activities in the workplace should be increased and improved by
collaborating with the employers and employees to develop a workplace good-practice initiative. This
should address work patterns, environment, and management culture as well as smoking, nutrition, and
physical activity. The Government should also encourage its municipalities to join the WHO Healthy
Cities initiative, which forges 'healthy alliances' between different agencies inside and outside the health
sector.
19.           Traffic and occupational injury prevention. Reliable data on injury epidemiology are
scarce. It is estimated that traffic accidents cause approximately 2,000 deaths and 2,000-3,000
permanent disabilities per year. Occupational accidents contribute an additional 15,000 injuries each
year. Given the lack of enforcement of traffic regulations and safety procedures as well as data from
other FSU countries, these numbers very likely understate the magnitude of the problem.
20.           To reduce mortality and injuries caused by accidents, cooperation between the police,
schools, occupational safety and labour organizations should be developed. The existing traffic safety
regulations should be reviewed in collaboration with the law-enforcement organizations in order to
develop measures that are feasible and realistic for decreasing accidents leading to death and disability.
In this context, the use of seat-belts, speed limits, drunk driving, and traffic arrangements may be
relevant issues. Occupational safety regulations should also be reviewed and due actions taken. Under
the project, a 5 % annual reduction is targeted in traffic accidents between 1996 and 1999.
21.           Family planning. Contraceptive prevalence rates in Georgia are low, ranging from 2%
to 25%, depending upon the source. Among women practicing birth control in 1992, 32% used pills,
18% IUDs, 19% condoms, 12% spermicides, and 15% "traditional methods". Elective abortion remains
the primary means of birth control and the number of elective abortions is reportedly increasing due to
shortages of pills, IUD's and condoms. At the same time, the risks associated with abortions are
increasing as the situation in hospitals and clinics deteriorates.
22.           Given the scarcity of resources and need to demonstrate an impact, the project will fund
a national family planning campaign targeting high school and college students. Sex education in
Georgian schools has been limited. Basic knowledge on sexuality, contraceptives and family planning
needs to be included in the school curricula. Culturally sensitive educational materials - books, leaflets,
posters, videos, and radio and TV programs -- will be developed and disseminated. Means to improve
the availability of modern and safe birth control methods at affordable price will also be given priority.
Under the Healthy Children and Safe Motherhood sub-component, training in family planning will be
incorporated into the medical school curricula and family planning counselling will be made an integral
part of primary care. A project target of a 30% reduction in the number of abortions and STDs in high
school and college students has been set. The impact will be evaluated based on the base case established
by the DPH.



Annex C. 1
Page 6 of 6
23.           National strategy for alcohol control. A national strategy for alcohol control needs to
be developed, paying attention to the cultural, social and economic factors related to drinking. The
Georgians are reluctant to run a separate vertical alcohol control program. However, they have
introduced a special tax on alcohol designed in part to reduce consumption. Legislation to control
production, import, wholesale, and distribution should also be developed. Alcohol as a risk factor will
be addressed in the cardio-vascular disease and traffic accident prevention program.



Annex C.2
Page I of 4
HEALTHY CHILDREN AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD
A. Introduction
1.           In the 1990s, fertility dramatically decreased in Georgia, while infant and maternal
mortality have been rapidly increasing. The Georgia Health Project is designed to reduce infant and
maternal mortality in the whole country, bringing the death rates to values closer to the WHO targets
for the European region: fewer than 15 infant deaths per live births and 15 maternal deaths per 100,000
live births.
2.            The total number of births decreased from 89,000 in 1989 to approximately 64,000 in
1993. The crude birth rate dropped from 19.2 in 1970 to 17.0 in 1990, and 11.8 in 1993. This drop is
occurring despite a stable abortion ratio (0.6-0.7 abortion per live birth), and a contraceptive
prevalence rate that may be as low as 2%, or as high as 25%, according to different sources.
3.            The infant mortality rate declined between 1960 and 1990 from 35.3 to 15.1 infant
deaths per 1,000 live birth, but in 1993, the estimated IMR had risen by 13 percent, reaching 21.4
infant deaths/1,000 live births. However, in specialized reports, the IMR is estimated to be between
30-50 infant deaths/1,000 live births. Forty percent of all infant deaths occur in the first seven days
after birth and an estimated four-fifths of these occur in the first three days of life.
4.            The maternal mortality rate for the period 1990-1993 was approximately 39 deaths per
100,000 births. The major causes of maternal mortality are hemorrhage (38%), causes related to
obstetric management (29%), abortion (14%), sepsis (9%), toxemia (3%), and other causes (7%).
Both age of the mother and her parity are associated with a higher relative risk for maternal death.
Women over 30 years of age account for 48 % of the deaths. Three-fourths of maternal deaths occur
among multiparous women, with 34% occurring among women with greater than five pregnancies.
B. Objectives
5.            The project will carry out a set of integrated actions intended to reduce perinatal and
maternal deaths in a situation of scarce resources. The sub-component Healthy Children and Safe
Motherhood specifically aims to decrease infant and maternal mortality by 30% countrywide, and to
decrease infant, perinatal and maternal mortality by 50% in Tbilisi and Kutaisi in the four years
duration of the Project. Project activities in this area are expected to ensure public funding for some of
the most cost-effective health interventions, to increase contraceptive coverage and decrease abortion,
and to improve the quality of perinatal care. Activities with an impact on women's and infants' health
will be carried out in the other components of the project, including Health System Reorientation,
Rehabilitation and Maintenance of Health Facilities, and Development of Health Human Resources.



Annex C.2
Page 2 of 4
C. Sub-component Description
6.            The sub-component objectives will be achieved through the following activities: (a)
reinforcement of the MoH's institutional capacity to develop health policies, health promotion
activities and services for women and infants; (b) development of a national policy for care of women
and infants, which includes: a minimum benefits package, protocols of care, rules for licensing of
staff and health services, guidelines for privatizing and reorganizing services, and the establishment of
a perinatal mortality surveillance system; (c) organization of quality perinatal care in downsized and
rehabilitated sites in Kutaisi, Tbilisi, and Tsageri, in order to demonstrate how perinatal services may
be improved; (d) development of pilot referral systems in Tbilisi and Kutaisi; (e) development of
training programs in maternal and child health, obstetrics and pediatrics; and (f) creation of a national
Task Force for Child and Matemal Survival, which will build national commitment, increase funding,
and develop public awareness of child and maternal survival issues.
Build capacity of the Department of Maternal and Child Health
7.            The project aims to reinforce the MoH's capacity to develop health policies, health
promotion activities and services for women and infants. The MoH's Department of Maternal and
Child Health (DMCH) is planning and will implement the sub-component activities. Under this
activity: (i) the DMCH office will be rehabilitated, and provided with furniture and supplies; (ii) the
staff will be reinforced with three specialists; and (iii) three specialists will make short study tours to
similar Departments and Programs of Maternal and Child Health in Western countries.
Develop modern protocols of care and guidelines for health services
8.            Sustainable basic package of health benefits. The project will ensure that the basic
package will cover in a sustainable way some of the most cost-effective health interventions, such as
immunizations, management of the sick child (newborn infants sick at birth, ARI, and DDR), family
planning, prenatal care (including care for high-risk women), and delivery care (including deliveries
with complications). The services which are currently included in the package have been selected
according to their impact on safe childbirth and survival of children under one year of age, given the
revenue projections for the State Health Fund. In the current version of the package, care of the sick
child under one year, four prenatal care visits, and normal deliveries are free of charge. The MoH
plans to include family planning in the basic package by March 1996. Under the Project, the DMCH,
assisted by foreign consultants (health economist, obstetrician, and pediatrician-neonatologist) will
review the basic package annually, and will make recommendations to the Minister of Health and the
State Health Fund.
9.            Modern protocols of care and guidelines for health services. The existing protocols
of care and guidelines for the delivery of women's and children's services are out-of-date, generating
considerable variation in practices. Modem protocols of care and guidelines will be development
under this sub-component to be disseminated and implemented across the country. Priority will be
given to developing protocols for interventions covered by the basic package, with a focus on risk
assessment, referral to an appropriate level of care, resuscitation and care of the newborn infant, and
management of the sick child. These protocols and guidelines will provide the basis for developing
training modules for health professionals and curricula for residency programs in Obstetrics,
Gynecology and Pediatrics, with a focus on Neonatology. These protocols and guidelines will also be
incorporated into the basic education medical, nursing and midwifery curricula, so that new health



Annex C.2
Page 3 of 4
professionals will be trained in their application and use. In the first year of the Project, the DMCH
will: (i) select national experts to draft protocols and guidelines from panels jointly developed with the
National Health Management Center, and foreign experts; (ii) organize meetings for discussion and
development of the guidelines; and (iii) publish the guidelines, disseminating them through six meetings
in different regions of the country.
10.           Reproductive and perinatal health data for decision-making. This activity includes:
(i) the design and implementation of a National Reproductive Health Survey that will provide
information about fertility, general health and risk factors, use of contraception, abortion, and sexual
experience of young adults; and (ii) design and implementation of a Perinatal Mortality Surveillance
System that will provide information about perinatal and maternal mortality. The DMCH has requested
foreign technical assistance to implement this activity. This assistance could be provided by the U.S.
Center for Disease Control (CDC), which has an ongoing, USAID-funded, epidemiological
surveillance project with the MOH.
Inprove Perinatal Care in Tbilisi, Kutaisi, and Tsageri
11.           The Project will establish Regional Perinatal Centers in Tbilisi and Kutaisi with the
purpose of: (i) reducing infant, perinatal and maternal mortality rates by 50% in both regions; (ii)
demonstrating how perinatal services can be reorganized and improved in the whole country; and (iii)
organizing suitable sites for the development of training activities. The Perinatal Centers in Tbilisi and
Kutaisi will provide integrated primary and secondary care, as well as serve as the highest referral level
for the Eastern and the Western part of Georgia, taking care of most complicated deliveries in each
area. Residency programs in Obstetrics, Gynecology and Pediatrics (focusing on Neonatology) will be
located in these Perinatal Centers. The rehabilitated perinatal services will receive modern essential
equipment and will implement protocols of care and guidelines developed by the Project. In
cooperation with UNICEF, the project will also support an outreach program in the Tsageri District.
12.           Tbilisi Perinatal Center. This sub-component will reorganize and improve delivery of
perinatal care in Tbilisi, creating the Perinatal Center within Tbilisi City Hospital #2, which will be
rehabilitated under the project. The Tbilisi Perinatal Center will: (i) provide integrated modern
prenatal, delivery and postpartum care to both low and high-risk pregnant women; (ii) serve as the
highest referral level in Eastern Georgia in a new, integrated model of providing primary and
secondary maternal and child healths services; and (iii) become the main location for residency
programs in Obstetrics and Gynecology, Pediatrics and Neonatology.
13.           Kutaisi Perinatal Center. The Kutaisi Perinatal Center will be located in the Kutaisi
Maternity and Pediatric Hospital, which will be created when the existing Regional Maternity is
merged with the Children's Hospital. This new facility, which will result in a significant downsizing of
space and beds, will be partly refurbished under the project. Under the Healthy Children and Safe
Motherhood sub-component, reproductive, perinatal and pediatric services will be reorganized and
improved. The Kutaisi Perinatal Center will: (i) provide integrated modern prenatal, delivery and
postpartum care to both low and high-risk pregnant women; (ii) serve as the highest referral level in
Western Georgia in a new, integrated model of providing primary and secondary maternal and child
healths services; and (iii) serve as a second location for residency programs in Obstetrics and
Pediatrics. Rehabilitation will give priority to: (i) improving prenatal care facilities, (women's
consultation center or other existing prenatal outpatient services), delivery, and postpartum services;
(ii), improving sterilization in the surgical theater and labor and delivery rooms; and (iii) installing
neonatal resuscitation equipment.



Annex C.2
Page 4 of 4
14.          Tsageri Outreach Program. The Tsageri Outreach Program is a joint-venture of the
World Bank, UNICEF and the Republic of Georgia. Coupled with the organization of the Kutaisi
Perinatal Center, and the development of a pilot referral system with improved communication and
modern ambulances, the Tsageri Outreach Program aims to demonstrate how to provide the pyramid of
integrated primary and secondary perinatal care in a targeted rural, mountainous area with very poor
access to secondary health services. The Project will: (i) rehabilitate and provide modern equipment
and supplies to the Tsageri District Hospital (referral level 1); and (ii) in cooperation with UNICEF,
provide selected FAPs and ambulatories with essential modem equipment and supplies and training.
Develop Referral Systems in Tbilisi and Kutaisi
15.          The Project will develop pilot referral systems (radio communication and ambulance
transportation) for Tbilisi and Kutaisi. The DMCH will be responsible for designing and setting up the
referral system, and for selecting public or private organizations (emergency services or firemen
associations) to run each region's system. In Tbilisi, communication equipment will be placed at the
Perinatal Center, maternity houses and departments of obstetrics, and at the Children's Hospital and
pediatric departments admitting newborn infants. In Kutaisi, communication equipment will be placed
in the Perinatal Center, and in district hospitals. Two ambulances will be allocated to each region, one
of them specifically equipped to transport newborns.
Train Health Professionals
16.          The DMCH has already developed training modules in neonatal resuscitation, thermal
control, diarrheal diseases (DDR), acute respiratory infections (ARI), and breastfeeding. In
cooperation with the National Health Management Center and the Center for Continuing Education, the
sub-component will support the development of other training modules for health professionals and
residency programs in Obstetrics, Gynecology, and Pediatrics, focusing on Neonatology. Priority will
be given to train physicians (obstetricians, newborn care pediatricians and general practitioners), nurses
and nurse-midwives working in the services to be rehabilitated. Training will include family planning,
immunization and management of the sick child.
Create Task Force for Maternal and Child Survival
17.          The project will support the creation and activities of a national Task Force for Child
and Maternal Survival (TFCMS), which would build national commitment, raise funds and public
awareness about child and maternal survival issues. This Task Force will be under the supervision of
the Inter-ministerial Comnmission for Protection of Women and Children



Annex C.3
Page I of 5
REALIGNMENT OF THE MINISTRY OF HEALTH:
QUALITY ASSURANCE, MONITORING AND REGULATION
A. Objective
1.            The objective of this sub-component is to strengthen the capacity of the Ministry of
Health to monitor, regulate and assure the quality of health care services. The sub-component will: (a)
license health professionals and create a data base of registered doctors and nurses; (b) register, license
and inspect health care facilities to create a data base of physical resources and ensure quality
standards; (c) support health care institutions willing to develop internal quality assurance programs; (d)
reorganize the administrative structure of the Department of Pharmaceuticals and Pharmaceutical
Industry, and support a quasi-governmental Agency for Drug Regulation and Quality Control which
will have the responbility for registering, assuring the quality, inspecting, and providing information on
pharmaceutical products. These activities will help to reduce the excess supply of doctors and hospital
beds and ensure that providers, institutions, pharmaceuticals and related products meet adequate
quality standards.
B. Quality of Care
2.            Georgia is in a state of economic crisis. The health care system is not capable of
responding to the declining health status of the population because of a lack of resources, regulation and
management. Consequently, the quality of health services is deteriorating rapidly. Quality of inputs --
pharmaceutical and medical supplies, buildings and technology, human resources, and financial resources
-- is poor and declining. Data on the process and outcomes of health care are scarce, but preliminary
findings point to problems in the appropriateness of care, which affect both the costs and the outcomes of
medical interventions.
3.            Input variables are often most important in low income countries. In middle income
countries, which already have the required inputs, process variables become a priority. Georgia falls
somewhere between these two categories: surplus inputs need to be optimized and their quality improved
and processes need to be changed before better outcomes will be achieved. For countries with scarce
resources, improving quality becomes especially critical in order to maximize health benefits with the
given amount of resources.
4.            Deteriorating Health Facilities. Georgia has an extensive network of poorly maintained
health care facilities: 338 hospitals and 1,252 outpatient facilities in 1992. More than half of the facilities
are over 50 years old. According to the Government's own estimates, 122 hospitals need significant
repairs and renovations. Public resource allocation for maintenance and capital investmnent accounted for
only 1.7% of public health care expenditures in 1992 and has continued to decline.
5.            Poor diagnostic and therapeutic capabilities. Most of the diagnostic and therapeutic
equipment is of Soviet or Eastern-European origin. It is poorly maintained and technologically out-of-
date. In the past, public sector institutions had no incentives to maintain equipment because the central
level decided when and how much equipment to buy. A visit to one of the three intensive coronary care



Annex C.3
Page 2 of 5
units in Tbilisi (Municipal Hospital #1) revealed that 8 patients with acute heart conditions had to share a
single functioning monitor. The unit was short of medicines and disposables, and unable to offer modern
treatments such as thrombolytic therapy and central haemodynamics monitoring. Diagnostic capacity was
impaired by a lack of paper for the electrocardiograph and batteries for the Holter monitoring device.
Ambulatories providing primary health care at the local level also lack diagnostic and therapeutic
capacity.
6.            Quality of health professionals. Quality of health professionals in Georgia is poor for
several reasons. First, an excess supply of physicians relative to other health professionals has
downgraded the activities performed by doctors. Excess supply also results in a lower than optimal
workload, important for maintaining adequate skill levels. Second, there are no training or practice
standards. A diploma from a medical school does not guarantee a standard quality for health
professionals. Professional organizations are weak, and do not exercise self-regulation. Relicensing of
existing health professionals, and introducing standards for new graduates, is necessary to ensure the
minimum standard of professional expertise in Georgia. Finally, low salaries and few opportunities for
professional advancement contribute to low staff morale.
7.            Phannaceuticals. The pharmaceutical sector is largely uncontrolled and unregulated by
the Government. Administrative structures have been created for quality control and inspection, but are
inefficient due to a lack of legislation, improper infrastructure, poor financing and management. Private
kiosks selling pharmaceuticals are appearing everywhere. Between 1991and 1994, 1,500 licenses were
issued and it is estimated that another 500 kiosks operate without licenses, not meeting standards of
storage sale. Such an uncontrolled pharnaceutical market poses great health risks to the population.
8.             Access to pharmaceuticals deteriorated after the collapse of the Soviet pharmaceuticals
market and the Georgian economy. Although it is estimated that 90% of essential pharmaceuticals are
available in the country, their distribution is uneven, quality is poor, and they are unaffordable for many.
Limited raw materials and packaging have reduced the production of ex-tempore pharmaceuticals. Ready
made pharmaceuticals are available but the choice is poor. One international NGO, UMCOR, estimates
that 50% of the population cannot afford to pay for pharmaceuticals. Donations of pharmaceuticals have
been vital for Georgia, and international assistance has been able to avoid the worst of the crisis. During
forthcoming years, however, NGOs plan to gradually phase out their activities. There, it is critical to
reform the pharmaceutical sector into a well-regulated, affordable and financially sustainable system.
Process variables
9.            Process variables which affect the quality of care include the organization and
management of services, accessibility and continuity of care, and the appropriateness of care. In Georgia,
a centralized health system is less responsive to local needs. Poor management has limited the ability of
the Government and health insitutions to monitor and influence the quality of care, efficiently procure and
distribute drugs, and adequately define standards for services and inputs. Current utilization of hospital
services by the population is considerably lower now than three years ago. Annual outpatient visits have
declined from 10.4 per capita in 1990 to 5.3 in 1993. Although current outpatient utilization indicators do
not compare badly with international data, it is unknown whether the decrease in utilization represents
unmet need. There is little evidence on the appropriateness of medical interventions in Georgia. No cost-
effectiveness or small area variations studies exist. Standardization of medical procedures and guidelines



Annex C.3
Page 3 of 5
for appropriate distribution of medical technologies are lacking. Options for intervention often lie out of
reach of medical professionals.
C. Government Strategy
10.           The changes in organization and financing of the health system included in the
Government's health care reform strategy will lead to improvements in the quality of care. Reduction of
excess capacity should make the system more efficient; decentralization should make the system more
responsive to local needs; introduction of a competitive market should increase the importance of quality
to health care providers. The Government is also taking specific steps to improve the quality of the health
services' inputs by establishing standards for health care providers and health professionals and regulating
the pharmaceutical sector. But, limited progress and even possible decline in quality could occur if
regulation of private health care market fails, clear accountability is missing and management skills are
lacking.
11.           Establishing standards for health care providers. As part of the Government's health
care reform, a State Agency of Quality Assurance for Health Care Providers has been established. This
Agency will be responsible for registering and licensing health care facilities, based on standards for
physicial infrastructure, equipment and staffing. Government legislation will ensure that no health care
facility operates without a license. A likely consequence of mandatory licensing will be the closure or
redesign of many institutions. In order to ease the financial hardship of closing facilities, the Government
may consider making funds available for the adaptation of hospitals to other purposes, such as refugee
centers or long-term facilities for chronic patients. Eventually, the Government plans to develop
accreditation standards and procedures. An accreditation agency would be established and accreditation
required for reimbursements by the State Health Fund.
12.           Ensuring standards for health professionals. The National Health Management Center
has been assigned the responsibilities of drafting a Medical Education Law, defining training requirements
for health professionals (both undergraduate and postgraduate), defining standards for an approved (and
reduced) list of specialities, and relicensing currently practicing health professionals (physicians and
middle level health personnel). To ensure the sustainabiity of these activities, the Government plans to
eventually pass the monitoring and standard setting functions to professional organizations.
13.           Regulating the pharmaceutical sector. The Govermnent is in the process of drafting a
National Drug Law and plans to restructure the administration of the pharmaceutical sector by assuming a
regulatory role and consolidating technical functions of the various committees and agencies into one
single agency. This model has been used in most of the EU countries, as well as in some CEE and FSU
countries.
D. Project Description
14.           The project will help the Ministry of Health: (i) draft legislation setting up effective and
transparent regulation of the health care sector; (ii) define processes required for developing and
implementing standards and protocols for health care institutions and various categories of health



AnInex C.3
Page 4 of 5
personnel; (iii) design and implement organizational arrangements for the licensing of health facilities
and health personnel; (iv) establishing an Agency for Drug Registration and Quality Control for
regulating the pharmaceutical sector; and (v) initiate a supportive quality assurance system in a few
hospitals, with full involvement of clinical and managerial staff. Tbilisi City Hospital #2 and the
Kutaisi Maternity and Pediatric Hospital, which will be rehabilitated under the Bank project, would be
included as pilot sites. The subcomponent will be implemented by four institutions: the State Agency
for Quality Assurance of Health Care Providers (SAQA), the National Health Management Center
(NHMC), the Department of Pharmaceuticals and Pharmacy Agency, and the National Agency of
Drugs Registration and Quality Control (NADRQC).
State Agency of Quality Assurance for Health Care Providers (SAQA)
15.           The State Agency of Quality Assurance for Health Care Providers (SAQA) will have
the following functions: (i) developing, monitoring and enforcing standards for health care providers;
(ii) performing accreditation and licensing of health care providers; (iii) monitoring the quality of the
basic package; (iv) establishing and maintaining a register of health care providers of Georgia; and (v)
supporting quality assurance initiatives at all levels of the health care system.
16.           The project will finance minor rehabilitation for the SAQA, local and foreign technical
assistance, long- and short-term training, computer hardware and software, office equipment, a vehicle
for site visits and inspection, and office equipment. Technical assistance will help the MoH to identify
the loci of responsibility, data to be collected, delineate scope of care, identify indicators, establish
thresholds for evaluation, determine needs, collect and organize data, identify threshold breakers, take
appropriate actions, assess the relationship between improvement of quality and reducing waste, assess
the budget implications of QA, and, finally, evaluate the tradeoff between marginal investment costs
and benefits. The project will support pilot studies of quality assurance in selected hospitals which could
assist in developing national programs and serve as models for other instituions.
The National Health Management Centre (NHMC)
17.           The National Health Management Center will have the following functions: (i) provide
leadership and coordination for developing standards for certifying health professionals, including
general licensing of all graduates and specialist credentials; and (ii) establish and maintain a register of
health professionals. Human resources planning will define the needs of the country, taking into account
the distribution of resources across rural and urban areas. Definitions and functional descriptions of
different specialities will be developed and standards for professional education and performance set.
Licensing requirements will be defined and medical practitioners will be required to obtain the licence in
order to be allowed to practice. Setting up s computerized register of practising physicians with their
qualifications will be done parallel with the licensing.
18.           The project will provide local and foreign technical assistance for licensing procedures,
computer hardware and software for the registration of health professionals, and office equipment and
training materials. Technical assistance would help the Center to define the requirements for the
continuing education of health professioAals, develop licensing rules for health professionals, and
support creation of a system of self-regulation by the medical profession.



Annex C.3
Page 5 of 5
The Department of Pharmacy and Pharmaceutical Industry and The National Agency for Drug
Registration and Quality Control (NADRQC):
19.           The newly created Department of Pharmaceuticals and Pharmaceutical Industry will
reorganise the functions of the Pharmacopoeia Committee, the Pharmacology Committee, the Quality
Control Agency, the Information Agency, and the Inspection Agency. Under the project, the Department
will: (i) develop a National Drug Policy concept; (ii) draft legislation to regulate the pharmaceutical
industry; (iii) coordinate and supervise ongoing projects and activities; (iv) monitor the implementation
of the National Drug Policy; and (v) advise the National Agency for Drug Regulation and Quality
Control on overall priorities.
20.           The National Agency for Drug Registration and Quality Control will have the following
responsibilities: (i) implementation of the Drug Law and subsequent rules and guidelines; (ii)
registration of medicines, registration and analysis of sales, and information dissemination (including
the publishing of a National Formulary); (iii) quality control of pharmaceuticals; (iv) inspection of
manufacturers, wholesalers and pharmacies, following Good Manufacturing Practice guidelines;
approval of labels for locally produced pharmaceuticals; (v) control of narcotic drugs according to
international conventions; (vi) issuance or advise to the Government about import and export
certificates; and (vii) issuance of licenses for organizations in the pharmaceutical sector, setting
standards and licensing sector personnel.
21.           The component will provide local and foreign technical assistance, short term visits,
computer hardware and software, office equipment, laboratory equipment for the quality control
laboratory, and vehicles for The Department of Pharmacy and Pharmaceutical Industry and The
National Agency for Drug Registration and Quality Control. Technical assistance will aid in preparing
laws regulating the registration of pharmaceutical products, medical supplies and clinical trials. TA
will also develop procedures for drug registration, including the introduction of a pharmaceuticals'
classification system and the publication of a National Drug Formulary.

Page 1 of 6
REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE OF HEALTH FACILITIES AND EQUIPMENT
A. Objective
1.            The poor condition of health facilities and equipment in Georgia is an obstacle to the
reform of the health system and improved delivery of medical care. The old system was plagued by a
lack of experience in functional programming, architectural design, sound construction and preventive
maintenance. These problems, combined with the 1987 earthquake and recent civil wars, have led to a
deterioration of health facilities which is both inefficient and unsafe. Furthermore, a centralized
administration and the lack of a reliable communication system have undermined the ability of the
government to respond to deterioration of health services. This deterioration of the health care
environment is most likely one of the reasons the health status of the population has worsened. The health
services are less able to deal with complications; polyclinics have a diminished consulting capacity and
hospitals show lower occupancy rates than before. Essentially, the delivery of services has collapsed.
2.            This component would support the rehabilitation and maintenance of health facilities in
Georgia by refurbishing three facilities in Tbilisi, Kutaisi, and Tsageri which will serve as centers of
clinical excellence for the provision of perinatal health services; building capacity in the MoH's
Building Technical Department (BTD) to set standards for the programming, design, and rehabilitation
and construction of health facilities; building capacity of the Committee of Medical Technologies and
Equipment (CMTE) to identify the needs, conduct technology assessment of, and procure medical
equipment; and providing training for hospital programmers, architects, biomedical engineers,
maintenance specialists and hospital staff on facilities design, construction, and equipment maintenance.
B. Background
3.          Decades of centralization have left users without a sense of control and ownership of the
systems that should be serving them. The distance between the users and authorities has eliminated, at all
levels, any incentives to administer and protect user-friendly, functional, cost-effective, and sustainable
health facilities. Further obstacles include lack of experience in: (i) establishing building standards; (ii)
programming spatial functions; (iii) designing architectural spaces in a cost-effective manner; (iv) planning
and managing complex medical equipment; (v) practicing competitive procurement procedures that will
result in the acquisition of economical, quality civil works and goods; and (iv) organizing, managing and
financing effective maintenance systems to protect occupants, buildings and equipment.
4.            Design. Health facilities are located in large, underutilized buildings planned under the
standards of the former Soviet Union. The Eastem pattern of concentrating facilities in "large
complexes" can also be found in Tbilisi and other large Georgian cities. Over-dimensioned areas of
hospital departments mean additional costs for heating, lighting, maintenance, cleaning, and safety.
Furthermore, poor use of space leads to increased circulation time for patients and staff.
5.            Building Maintenance. About half of the facilities were built before 1940 with no
further maintenance. Damages in building structures are the results of stability problems caused by



Annex CA
Page 2 of 6
earthquakes, ground water, mediocre material, and poor construction techniques: cracks can
frequently be seen in structural components, walls and floors. Heavy deterioration of buildings and
infrastructure is evident. Deteriorating building materials commonly seen include: leaky roofs, hanging
gutters, broken drains, plaster falling from the ceilings, floor material peeling off, non-insulated
windows, and broken sash and glass. The building systems are often unsafe or non-operational:
blocked sanitary facilities, cracked heating pipes, exposed wiring, and fluorescent tubes destroyed by
fluctuating current.
6.             Equipment and supplies. The average age of essential medical equipment such as X-ray
machines, laboratory and physiotherapeutic equipment, endoscopes, ventilators, intensive-care monitors,
and surgical equipment is 8 to 15 years. The equipment no longer complies with modem technological
and safety requirements. Sterilization in some surgical and intensive care units is done using boilers and
dry hot sterilizers. Stocks of health care supplies are insufficient and shortages of spare parts, reagents, X-
ray films, and ECG paper impair the functioning of 70% to 80% of the equipment. Oftentimes, patients
must provide their own linen, food, drugs, medical supplies and transportation. Humanitarian aid,
however, has contributed to acquisition of some modern equipment.
C. Description of the Rehabilitation and Maintenance Component
7.     The component's main activities include: (i) the rehabilitation of health facilities; (ii) the
procurement of critically needed medical equipment; and (iii) the strengthening and modernization of tools
and standards adopted by the Ministry of Health central units for future facility and equipment planning.
Rehabilitation of Health Facilities and Medical Equipment
8.            During project preparation, an assessment was made of the need for and cost of
rehabilitating health facilities. On the basis of this assessment, it was clear that the cost of a
comprehensive rehabilitation program was prohibitively high. Three facilities were chosen for
rehabilitation under the project: Tbilisi City Hospital #2, Kutaisi Pediatric Hospital (which would be
merged with the existing Maternity Hospital to become the Kutaisi Maternity and Pediatric Hospital), and
the Tsageri District Hospital. These facilities were chosen from a priority list drawn up by the
Government during project preparation based on: 1) the technical feasibility of rehabilitating the facilities;
2) their willingness to improve efficiency by reducing their size, improving management, introducing
energy saving measures and other cost saving measures; and 3) their linkages to other components of the
projects, particularly activities in maternal and child health. The current situation of each of these
facilities is described below.
Tbilisi City Hospital #2
9.            General Overview. The current campus consists of two existing buildings and two
unfinished buildings. The main building was constructed around 1942. It is a four-story structure which
contains administration, surgery-recovery, and 120 beds. The second functioning building is a five story
structure built in 1977 and contains most diagnostic departments, 180 beds, the emergency room, and an
outpatient dental clinic. The condition of these two buildings can be described as badly deteriorated and
unsafe. An analysis of the MEP/Life systems in these two facilities is available. However, project



Annex CA
Page 3 of 6
activities will concentrate on the two unfinished towers and the other two buildings would eventually
close. The new facility will be a general hospital, housing the Thilisi Perinatal Center and also offering
surgery and cardiology and a set of other complementary services such as radiology, intensive care,
emergency department, and diagnostic
10.           Building Condition. The codes under which the two unfinished buildin,gs were designed
are the same as the building constructed in 1977. Most of the structure and foundation are finished with
the exception of some internal staircases. The roof and windows need to be replaced. Water and
sewerage systems have must be modified and sanitary devices installed. The quality of construction is
poor and MEP/Life systems mirror those of the 1977 building.
11.           Electrical Systems. The electrical distribution system is relatively simplistic and appears
to have limited capacity. A great deal of wiring is installed in plastic conduit that is buried in the mortar
topping placed on top of precase panel floor assemblies. Much of this conduit is broken and will be
useless in the event rewiring is necessary. All switchboards have been vandalized and the electrical power
station is not finished. Lifts have been installed but either need extensive repair or replacement.
12.           Mechnical Systems. Only heating tubes and heaters have been installed, and about 30%
of the heaters are missing. The boiler house is unfinished and there is no laundry or kitchen equipment.
13.           Medical Equipment. The hospital has received some medical equipment from Emory
University Hospital such as beds, radiological devices, laboratory devices, etc... Much of this equipment
remains in boxes, awaiting completion of the facility. An assessment of this equipment has been made
and a supplementary list drawn up. This list would be finalized once the architectural brief is complete.
Kutaisi Maternity Hospital 1
14.           General Overview. This 395 bed matemity hospital serves as the primary referral
center for Western Georgia. Nonetheless, occupancy rates are low, generally around 50 percent.
The hospital is located near two other health facilities in Kutaisi: the Pediatric hospital and the
outpatient polyclinic. The maternity hospital has a main building with five floors for the
emergency, reanimation, outpatient department, functional diagnostic, obstetric and gynecological
departments, delivery rooms and newborns, radiology, laboratory and operating rooms. The
kitchen, laundry, boiler house, generator, power station and oxygen station are in separate
buildings. The maternity hospital has no incinerator.
15.           Building Condition. The walls, ceilings and floors need repair. The roof and
water and sewerage systems must be replaced. The hospital has two underground water reservoirs
with 250 cubic meter capacity each and a 15KW pump (in need of maintenance). Fire equipment
must be changed.
i The functions of this facility will be merged with the existing Kutaisi Pediatric Hospital and the
Maternity Hospital closed and the equipment consolidated. These two facilities are situated at less then
two hundred meters from each other, but they have completely autonomous support buildings and
equipment (boiler houses, laundries, kitchens, transformer stations and diesel generators), which is
clearly not cost effective.



Annex CA
Page 4 of 6
16.           Electrical Systems. The electric power supply conditions are similar to the
Pediatric Hospital but there is a well maintained 100 kW emergency generator that could function
well with minor repairs. The transformer station high voltage equipment must be replaced. The
low voltage switchboard most likely would also need to be replaced, although the main and floor
switchboards are in reasonable condition. According to local information, fifty percent of the
distribution cables and fixtures must be replaced. Lighting was designed to be 90% fluorescent
but is presently incandescent due to a lack of tubes and ballast. Five out of 7 lifts are working -
better than in any other sampled facility. Fire detectors are installed but out of order.
17.           Mechanical Systems. The boiler house has 4 installed units that are in reasonable
condition, but have not converted from gas to fuel oil. Each unit is smaller than those in the
Pediatric Hospital, but may be sufficient for both facilities (without reserve boiler) if their building
"envelope" is of good quality. Piping, valves and pumps must be repaired. There is also an
incinerator but it is out of order. The main building ventilation is not working and must be
repaired. The kitchen is poorly equipped and laundry has only one badly working machine.
Central storage of oxygen was not finished.
18.           Medical Equipment. Very little medical equipment is reliable in this maternity
hospital, including the incubators. A large amount of equipment would be necessary to adequately
equip all of the hospital's department. Sixty percent of the medical equipment is maintained by in-
house staff, 35% by outside services and 5% not at all.
Kutaisi Pediatric Hospital
19.           General Overview. The Kutaisi Pediatric Hospital, with 360 beds, serves
approximately 900,000 people in the Kutaisi cachement area. It is considered the second
children's' hospital in Georgia and is the primary referral center for Western Georgia. The
hospital consists of two health care buildings connected to one another. The main building of six
floors includes the emergency, outpatient department, reanimation, laboratory, neurology,
radiology and functional diagnostic, therapy, traumatology and orthopedic and general surgery. In
the second building, two stories high, are the neonatology and newborns departments. The
building is too large for its current functions, but sufficient to assume both its current functions as
well as those of the Maternity Hospital.
20.           Building Condition. The structures are in good general condition but in the main
building the stairs must be repaired. It is necessary to repair walls, ceilings and floors, mainly in
the 2nd, 3rd, 5th and 6th floors. The roof needs to be repaired. Water and sewerage networks
are to be replaced or upgraded and all bathrooms repaired. The hospital has an underground
water reservoir of 500 cubic meter with a pumping system (needing maintenance).
21.           Electrical Systems. In 1994, the hospital estimates that it was without electrical
power supply for 50 days. The frequency drops at times to 41-42 Hz and voltage to lower than
150 V. The 30 kW emergency generator is out of order. The main switchboard is in poor
condition, but the floor ones are in regular condition although with severe limitations. According



Annex CA
Page 5 of 6
to local information, fifty percent of distribution network and fixtures must be replaced. Internal
lighting has the usual problems, and the external lighting has been stolen or vandalized. Of the
four lifts, two are still in working condition. The two lifts that were connecting the main building
with the kitchen are not working due to heavy infiltration problems in the tunnel between both
buildings. There are no fire detectors.
22.            Mechanical Systems. This hospital has one boiler house with four units burning
gas: two for steam and two for hot water. All of these need significant repairs as well as the water
treatment equipment, which needs to be replaced, piping, valves, pumps, etc. Considering the
estimated heating power needed for the Children's and Maternity Hospitals, the installed boilers
are apparently oversized, but this must be confirmed. The kitchen has equipment that is out of
order and in the laundry, only one centrifuge machine is still working. There is no central storage
of liquid oxygen.
23.            Medical Equipment. In this hospital, there was more reliable and repairable
medical equipment than was found on average in other similar facilities. However, there is a lack
of essential
devices for reanimation and surgery as well as for infant ventilation and intensive care. Radiology
and functional diagnostic should also be replaced.
Tsageri District Hospital
24.            The Tsageri District Hospital was built in 1925. A new block was under construction in
1989, but construction has stopped. The general condition of the building is poor, especially the roof,
floors, and water and sewerage systems. Rehabilitation under the project will focus on only Block B,
where maternal and pediatric services are offered as well as ensuring an external water and sewerage
system for the entire facility. The project will also provide essential equipment for the maternity and
pediatric services.
25.            An architectural brief will be prepared for each facility to be rehabilitated. The Brief
would contain: (i) the as-built construction drawings which describe the facilities to be rehabilitated; (ii)
site data, soil surveys and seismic studies, if required; (iii) functional programs, with areas in the metric
system; (iv) a strategy for phasing rehabilitation of facilities in use; (v) a preliminary furniture list; (vi)
guidelines for proposed safety, security and quality; (vii) a maintenance strategy and manuals; (viii) cost
estimates for rehabilitation and furniture; (ix) an implementation schedule; and (x) the technical
specifications (including space and service requirements) for the medical equipment. A team of four
short-term specialists will be recruited to assist the BTD in preparing the architectural briefs, namely, a
building standards specialist, a health facilities programmer, a maintenance specialist, and a medical
equipment specialist. Terms of Reference for the architectural services have been prepared.
26.            The project will finance medical equipment and furniture for the three facilities to be
rehabilitated. The architectural briefs will include the agreed preliminary medical equipment list. A
consultant medical equipment specialist will be hired to prepare detailed technical specifications for the
medical equipment. The briefs will include criteria, prototypes, and the preliminary list of furniture to
guide the architect in design, costing and specification of furniture.



Annex CA
Page 6 of 6
Capacity Building and Training
27.           Responsibility for implementing this component of the project falls to two component
coordinator, one who is the director of the BTD and the other who is the director of the CMTE. They will
be assisted by a component coordinator based in Kutaisi who will oversee project activities in both Kutaisi
and Tsageri. The component coordinators will be assisted by staff of the BTD and CMTE, staff at the
three facilities, and foreign and local consultants. The refurbishment activities planned for the three
facilities will include the development of standards which can be used nationwide and strengthen the MoH
capacity to plan future rehabilitation and construction and procurement and maintenance of medical
equipment.
28.           In addition, the component will support training of designated staff by offering a Health
Facilities Training Workshop which will be based in the Center for Continuing Education. Volunteer
artisans in the building trades will be recruited to enhance the skills of local building artisans on a short-
term basis. A coordinator will organize this small task force. Local professionals, who may contribute to
the improvement of the health care environment, will be registered and may be invited to work with the
specialists on a part-time basis on work and training programs designed to support and continue the impact
of the specialists. A coordinator will be recruited to mobilize this part-time local consultants and organize
their work.

Page I of 4
HUMAN RESOURCES
A. Introduction
1.            Georgia has more than 120,000 persons working in the health sector. The density of
physicians is one of the highest in the world: I physician per 197 inhabitants. There are approximately
30,000 practicing physicians compared to an estimated need of 10,000-12,000. The large number of
doctors is the result of Georgia's traditional esteem of medicine as a career and former Soviet Union
policy emphasizing the number of physicians as a measure of achievement in the health sector. The
number of nurses is also high relative to OECD countries (70,000 nurses compared to a need of
40,000), but they are poorly trained, unlicensed and have low professional status and pay. The nursing
education system was oriented towards basic sciences, preparing nurses to act as physician-assistants.
Undertrained, underutilized and inadequately managed doctors and nurses greatly inhibit the efficiency
and effectiveness of the Georgian health care system.
2.            During the Soviet period, one graduate and one post-graduate medical school provided
all medical training in Georgia. After the break-up of the FSU, due to the increasing number of
candidates and the refusal of other republics to admit foreign medical students, the Ministry of
Education (MoE) granted authorization to 28 new private medical schools. There is no accreditation
procedure for these schools, no quality standard for teaching, no career track for faculty, no reliable
register for trainers and trainees, and no licensing or certification procedure for graduates. The total
number of medical students in all schools is now around 2,500, about 10 times the requirement of the
country. Similar problems are found in the post-graduate medical education system. There are no
criteria for selection of persons to receive resident training, no standards to judge the quality of the
training programs, and no process for certification of specialists once they have completed training.
3.            The educational system does not provide the knowledge and skills necessary to carry
out competency-based and evidence-based care comparable to international standards. The
fragmentation of the medical system is evident. In medical schools, separate faculties exist for paediatrics,
sanitation and hygiene and military medicine. Few of the various health care institutions or agencies
serving special populations collaborate with each other, which creates an inefficient system. Several key
educational components are absent:
a.     There are no medical personnel qualified as general practitioners. Professionals who
serve in this area lack proper training. As a result, many areas of care, and especially
maternal and child health care, are inadequately served. Both the continuity of primary
care and the ability to provide comprehensive care are seriously impeded.
b.     Public health is narrowly defined to mean infectious diseases control. Simple
enforcement of hygienic norms does not address health promotion. Disease prevention
programs are needed as non-communicable and life-style related diseases contribute
increasingly to the burden of disease in the society.
c.     The administration and management of the health care system are hampered by
insufficient management skills. These are essential in the changing financial and
regulatory environment arising from an increased autonomy of providers, performance-
based financing schemes and the introduction of health insurance.



Annex C. 5
Page 2 of 4
B. Govermnent Strategy
National Health Management Center
4.            In March 1995, the Government established the National Health Management Center
(NHMC) to regulate public and private medical education, license health care professionals and
develop curricula for post-graduate medical training. In addition, the NHMC is to establish a health
services research program in order to support the policy-making ability of the Ministry of Health. The
NHMC has drafted legislation on health education and assembled expert panels to develop standards
and programs for specialty training which draw on the guidelines of the European Union. The NHMC
has also been instrumental in starting professional societies for public health and family medicine
practitioners.
Center for Continuing Education
5.            According to the plans of the Ministry of Health, a Center for Continuing Education
(CCE) for health and allied professionals will be created inside the NHMC. The goals of the CCE are:
(i) to establish a complete program of courses, workshops and seminars to improve the practice of all
health care professionals in the Republic of Georgia; and (ii) to establish a cost-effective and efficient
means to deliver these new skill areas to practitioners throughout the Republic of Georgia.
6.            Proposed activities for the CCE are the following:
a.     To assist in the provision of professional training for the newly emerging health care
occupations in Georgia, in particular: health care administration and management,
nursing management, family practice medicine, health promotion and community
public health programs.
b.     To provide continuing education for all health care professionals practicing in Georgia
in order to enhance the knowledge base and capabilities of practitioners at all levels of
the system, and especially for nurses, midwives, physicians in community clinics, and
managers.
c.     To establish and maintain an ongoing program of training trainers in all fields and
enhancing medical faculty members in their teaching areas.
d.     To improve access to these education programs throughout Georgia through: (i)
developing remote-areas courses; (ii) developing the capability to deliver programs in
all 11 major centers (Tbilisi, Poti, and the nine regional centers); (iii) becoming a
resource center for the education of h NHMC health personnel, including an
information center for research and Fellowship grants, study abroad programs and a
library of current publications; and (iv) publishing current health sciences and practice
information, student and teacher course materials, monographs, professional
association and study materials and health promotion information



Annex C. 5
Page 3 of 4
7.            The CCE could become an applied research operation to investigate the educational
needs of health professionals, improve the methods used in epidemiological or other research projects
which relate to education, and to assist in carrying out other research as requested.
8.            The Center will have few staff members and instead will contract out curriculum
development and training to Georgian universities and practitioners, as well as international specialists.
The permanent administrative staff will consist of: (i) an administrative director, who will have a
strong understanding of continuing education programming and the education of health services
personnel at various levels across disciplines; a program coordinator for each of the following four
areas: family practice education, nursing education, public health education, and health management
education. These managers will identify necessary programs, develop program curricula, course
marketing and delivery on site in Tbilisi and elsewhere; (iii) a desk top publishing unit manager who
will become an expert in desk top publishing technology and the publishing process; and (iv) support
staff, including a receptionist/word processor, and an assistant for each of the positions described
above.
9.            The CCE will cooperate with other health care assistance programs, such as the
proposed Health Promotion Fund, the USAID-supported Atlanta/Tbilisi Hospital Partnership Project
and the Association of University Programs in Health Administration.
C. Component Description
10.           The Human Resources component will assist the Government in implementing the
above strategy by: (a) strengthening the capacity of the National Health Management Center to create
a system for accrediting medical schools, develop a modern curriculum for post-graduate physician and
nursing education, support the development of professional associations, and develop health services
research to support the MoH in policy making; and (b) supporting the creation of the Center for
Continuing Education (CCE) to provide part-time professional education for newly emerging health
care occupations such as health care management, family practice, and public health, provide on-site
and at-distance continuous training programs for all health professionals, establish a program of
"training the trainers", and create a desk-top publishing unit. As these programs become increasingly
active and self-sustaining, the need for outside financing will decrease.
Il.           National Health Management Center. The component will provide foreign technical
assistance in accreditation and curriculum design to help the National Health Management Center carry
out its designated functions.
12.           Center for Continuing Education. The project will refurbish and equip 1350m2 in the
NHMC building, where the CCE will be located. The CCE will have a 200-seat auditorium, equipped
seminar rooms with a capacity of 25 students each, a computer classroom, and a resource center to
include print, audio and video materials and equipment for lending, a desk top publishing unit, a
cafeteria, and offices for administrative staff. The NHMC building will also accommodate a resource
center to be developed by the USAID-supported Atlanta/Tbilisi partnership providing access to the
Internet and other medical educational resources. These three centers will complement each other,
ensuring an efficient use of resources.



Annex C. 5
Page 4 of 4
13.           The following technical assistance (foreign and local) will be included as part of the
project: (i) curriculum development specialists, who will prepare training program materials. If
possible, these specialists should also act as the pilot teachers of the courses, to ensure that the
materials they have designed are suitable; (ii) teachers for courses offered at the Center. These teachers
should be well-regarded specialists trained in adult education; (iii) a continuing education management
spccialist to assist in the planning and implementation of Center operations. This person will assist in

and curriculum design specialists in each of the Center's four areas of concentration.

the auditorium and the seminar rooms, computers for classroom training as well as administrative
purposes, communications and Desk Top Publishing equipment, and a van for transporting staff and
equipment for outreach activities.
