MODERNIZATION OF THE HEALTH FINANCING SYSTEM
A. Background
1.            The Georgian health care financing reforms are being launched amidst general
economic decline, worsening levels of health status, collapse of sector organization and finance, long
term deterioration of health facilities, and long-standing inefficiencies and inequities in medical
provision.
2.            As in all formerly Soviet countries, there is a tendency to ignore positive features of the
former health system. The system was universal and general. A strong network of hospitals and
polyclinics was available. Significant steps in controlling infectious diseases such as tuberculosis had
been made. Indeed the system may have been particularly adept at tackling health problems due to
poverty. Nevertheless, there is little doubt that the former system was characterized by severe
imbalances and inefficiencies. For example:
a)     The method of resource allocation exacerbated regional and inter-household inequalities
in service provision. Resources were allocated on the basis of capacity number of beds
and number of doctors and past levels of activity and political influence, rather than on
the basis of patient need, efficiency or other suitable criteria.
b)     Line budgeting favored the employment of staff over expenditure on material items
(e.g. drugs, medical supplies, food and fuel, etc.).
c)     Management capacity needed for efficient finance and provision of health care was
severely limited.
d)     First-contact facilities were frequently staffed by poorly qualified or inexperienced staff
with little financial motivation to treat patients effectively and efficiently.
e)     Poorly planned investment and training programmes resulted in an accumulation of
acute care facilities that have left Georgia with a doctor/population ratio that is among t
he highest in the world. Occupancy rates are well below 40 percent and the surplus of
personnel is around 60 percent of current staffing.
3.            A key feature of the system inherited from the Soviet period is illegal or under-the-table
gratuity payments. These payments are deeply ingrained in the country's culture and have only a
tenuous relationship to levels of activity or quality of care. By their very nature, no reliable figures are
available on the extent of such payments. Anecdotal evidence suggests that they are obtained by staff
at all levels of the health service although the main payments are likely to go to hospital doctors.
4.            Though under-the-table payments are unquantifiable, there is broad agreement that they
are a major distortion to the health care delivery process. For example, it is likely that specialists in
psychiatric care receive little extra income. In contrast, surgical specialists, particularly gynecologists
providing a quick and plainly beneficial service, can increase income substantially. Some gratuity
money is given to encourage good quality care but money is also given by patients who fear not being
treated at all. Despite the possibility of some type of price discrimination in determining the size of



Annex C.6
Page 2 of 11
gratuity payments, it is likely that access to effective treatrnent is hindered by doctors who give priority
to those able to pay or who keep patients waiting in hospital for operations.
5.            Over the last three years the MoH has developed a reform program aimed at
guaranteeing an acceptable and affordable level of services to the population and correcting structural
deficiencies of the health service that existed before the collapse of the Soviet Union and the subsequent
civil war. These reforms will greatly change the way in which services are organized and financed,
and have the potential of arresting the current imbalances and inefficiencies.
B. Health Expenditures: Levels, Trends and Composition'
6.            While Georgia was part of the Soviet Union, the health sector was underfunded,
receiving from 2.8 to 4.1 percent of GDP. With the current collapse of the economy the situation has
become worse. Since 1992, the real value of public expenditures on health has fallen dramatically.
While the exact fall in real expenditures is difficult to estimate due to a very high rate of inflation,
using IMF price indices, the real value of public expenditures on health in 1993 were estimated to be
less than 15 percent of the value for 1990 and in 1994 less than 10 percent of the 1990 value. In 1994,
public expenditures on health fell to 0.3 percent of GDP and 1.3 percent of public sector expenditures.
In 1994, annual public expenditures on health averaged less than US$1 per person.
Table 1: Public Sector Health Expenditures, 1990 - 1994
1990       1991        1992   1993       1994
Nominal health Expenditures*             0.45        0.78        3.57     70      4300
Real Expenditures (% of 1990)             100         103         30      11         8
Real Per Capita Expenditures (1994 USD)  9.62       10.10        2.98    1.12      0.81
Healthas%ofGDP                           3.5%       4.1%        2.8%    0.4%      0.3%

*In billions of coupons (1995 =thousands of lari). Rubles 1990-92 converted into coupons at 1:1.
7.            Even before the recent reduction in the budget, chronic under funding interacted with
low staff motivation and commodity shortages to contribute to a low quality of health services.
Although the real value of recurrent public expenditures on health increased in both absolute and per
capita terms until 1992, the total remained modest as a percent of GNP (about 3.5 percent in 1990) in
comparison with the lowest perforning OECD countries (Greece 5.3%, Portugal 6.4%).  Capital
expenditures, primarily on hospitals, also increased throughout the first part of the 1980s, driving the
need for increased recurrent expenditures. In spite of the steady increase in recurrent public
expenditures on health throughout the 1980s, low wages in the health sector and low expenditures on
supplies contributed to the overall low level of expenditures on health.
This section is based on analysis done for the Georgia Public Expenditure Review.



Annex C.6
Page 3 of 11
C. Collapse of Government Revenues
8.            Under the structure of health financing that Georgia inherited from the Soviet system,
health care was primarily financed from the government budget. Localities were allocated funds using
a formula based on the number of hospital beds and physicians. Parallel agencies, such as the Railways
and the Interior Ministry, were also provided funds from the central budget for their own health
activities.
9.            A small amount of revenue also came from official charges for some drugs and a
limited set of health care services and goods such as eye glasses and hearing aids. The amount of
revenue collected in Georgia from these payments is not known, however, based on information from
Russia, Kyrghyzstan and Estonia, it is estimated that these private sources were equal to 15 percent of
the total state expenditures on health. In addition for many years it has been an accepted, but illegal
and undocumented practice to make informal private payments to physicians for services. Presently,
no data are available for estimating these informal payments.
10.          The structure of revenue sources for the health sector has changed dramatically over
the last three years. With the collapse of government revenues from 1991 to 1994, the private sector
has become the major source of revenues. In the absence of an adequate household expenditure
survey, however, it is not possible to give a credible estimate of the total value of private expenditures.
The amount may, however, total as much as 75 percent of total sector expenditures.
11.          The government's policy response to increase revenues for the sector has been to
introduce a State Health Program which is financed from a mandatory payroll tax for health with 3
percent employer contribution and 1 percent employee contribution. This will be supplemented by
revenues from privatization, licensing fees, and interest income as well as a sizable allocation from
general government revenues in order to cover costs to administration, public health services, services
for the poor, and services for veterans.
12.          In addition to the State Health Program, which is administered by the State Health
Fund, municipalities are obligated to provide a package of services under the Municipal Program. For
the period from August 10 - December 31, the government mandated that municipalities spend a
minimum of 1.3 lari (US$1) per inhabitant for this program. The minimum annual spending level for
municipalities for 1996 has been set at 2.2 laris per person. Many municipalities, such as Tbilisi,
Kutaisi, and Rustavi, are spending two to three times the minimum amount. A recent government
decree (September 13, 1995) further obligates municipalities to pay for 90 percent of the utility
expenses of health facilities located in their jurisdiction. This subsidy will be phased out over time.
13.          The introduction of the State Health Fund along with the mandated expenditure levels
at the municipal level will increase total resources going to the health sector. For example, projected
monthly expenditures for December 1995 are 3.5 million laris compared to average monthly
expenditures of 1.2 million lari for the first half of the year. With these additional sources of revenues,
public sector health spending is projected to reach US$3.68 per person in 1995. In 1996, spending
should reach US$9 per person per year and around 89% of public spending. The assumptions used in
estimating health expenditures for 1995 are described in the Box below.

Total public sector health spending is estimated to be 25.6 million lari, or 20 million dollars in 1995
(assuming an average exchange rate of 1.28 for the year). This is equal to approximately 6.0 percent of
all public sector expenditures for the year. The following assumptions were used to reach this estimation:
*   actual public sector health expenditures for January - May 1995 were 5.887 million lari (central
= 1.812 million, local = 3.325 million, other = 0.750). (Source/MOF)
*   projections for June are based on monthly average for January-May (1.177 million lari per
month)
*   from July through September, central and other expenditures are estimated at 1.883 million lari
per month, based on IMF data which include extra budgetary support to the Ministry of Health.
*   from October through December, central government spending is based on reported
expenditures of the State Health Fund (5.213 million lari or 1.741 lari per month)
   from July through August 10th, local government expenditures are assumed to continue at the
monthly rate estimated from January through June (0.665 million lari per month)
*   from August 10th through December 31, local expenditures are assumed to equal 1.3 lari
(US$1) per person which is the minimum level of expenditures for the municipal package
mandated by the reforms. Total local expenditures during this time would therefore equal
7.058 million lari (1.3 lari * 5,429,500 population) Note that this may underestimate total local
expenditures because: i) some municipalities have chosen to provide more services and ii) a
September 13th decree holds municipalities responsible for paying 90 percent of utility costs of
facilities located in their jurisdiction. On the other hand, we do not know if all municipalities
complied with the decree.
*   total public expenditures are estimated to be 427.8 million lari for 1995 based on estimates of
the RAF.
14.             The new policy reforms, however, introduce a number of practical issues that must be
overcome if the reforms are to be successful. An essential first step is to develop an appropriate
regulatory framework and provider payment procedures with appropriate incentives. The institutional
framework needs to be revised under a decentralized structure, and local capacity in accounting and
management needs to be greatly strengthened.
D. Key Issues
Financial Sustainability of the Health Care Reform
15.             The State Health Fund has been fully functional since January 1996, when revenues
from the tax on hazardous products and privatization became available in addition to continued
allocations from the state budget and revenue from the "3+1" payroll tax. The Ministry of Health
projects that the Fund will collect approximately 24.1 million lari in revenue in 1996. As seen in the
table below, the principal source of revenues will be the central budget, which is expected to contribute
16.9 million laris.



Annex C.6
Page 5 of 11
16.           Payroll and sin taxes will contribute another quarter of the projected revenues. The
government made the following assumptions on tax compliance when estimating tax revenues: i)
payroll tax -- assumed to be the same rate (approximately 35 percent) as currently achieved by the
social security tax and ii) excise taxes -- assumed to be 20 percent of potential revenue given current
use.
17.           Projections from privatization, which account for less than 5 percent of total revenues,
assume that the government will proceed with its planned program of privatization of health facilities
for 1996. The government feels that its estimates of revenues from privatization are very
conservative, and may underestimated actual revenues by half. On the other hand, with no prior
experience of privatizing health facilities in Georgia and given the poor state of most health facilities,
these figures may be optimistic.
18.           Currently, government projections for expenditures for the State Health Programs are
42.6 million lari for 1996, exceeding revenue projections by approximately 18.5 million lari. In
addition to the concerns about the sustainability of central budget financing and the ability of the
government to collect the tax revenues earmarked for the Fund, a further consideration is the projected
volume of services and unit costs. The government estimates of expenditures are based on current
utilization patterns and do not consider latent demand that is suppressed by unaffordability or poor
quality of services. If, as is predicted by the experience with case based reimbursement elsewhere, the
volume of services increases, the cost of the basic package will exceed projections and add to the
projected deficit.
19.          In the short run, the most likely sources to fill the financing gap of the Fund are: i)
international donors, who currently provide more than 8 million lari of vaccines and medical supplies
and are committed to continue their support to Georgia within the framework of the new reforms; and
ii) additional revenues from privatization, assuming the Government's estimates are approximately one
third of what they expect. In addition, the following factors will be essential in order to achieve a
balance of projected revenues and expenditures for the Fund: i) strengthened collection of revenues; ii)
continued support from the central budget; and iii) implementation of a monitoring and control system.
20.           In addition to ensuring the financial sustainability of the State Health Fund, the
government also has an obligation to ensure that municipalities contribute to the municipal program at
the rate required (see para 13). The municipal program is responsible for covering emergency services
and reimburses providers using the same nosological classification system as the State Health Fund. If
municipal expenditures continue at the same rate mandated for 1996, they would total approximately 12
million lari in 1996, or approximately 20 percent of all public sector spending on health.

The Basic Package and Priority Seffing
21.           The "basic package" refers to a minimum program of preventive and essential clinical
health care to be financed by the government. The government used what appears to be a rational
explicit methodology for arriving at the "basic package." This is based on six criteria: epidemiological
significance, social significance, life-threatening conditions, need for emergency care, effectiveness of
medical intervention and cost of treatment. The package which the government has devised for 1995,
considering the expected revenues of the State Health Fund, includes the following services and
activities:
Disease prevention: immunization, prevention of socially dangerous diseases, safe motherhood
and healthy children, epidemiological surveillance, and health promotion.
Curative services: inpatient treatment for deliveries, all infants under the age of 1, treatment of
tuberculosis, inpatient psychiatric care, and provision of drugs for terminally ill patients.
22.           The items included in the curative services suggest that criteria other than those
explicitly cited above were used to determine what could be covered. This package has undergone
continuous delays, but a July 5, 1995 order of the Head of State clearly sets out what is covered in the
package. Additionally, the order specifies that municipalities must finance a third component of the
basic package -- "emergency care" as well as health services for children age 1-14. Precisely what
constitutes "emergency care" is as yet unclear although it does appear that there is some, albeit weak,
coordination between the central government and municipalities in defining responsibilities.
Additionally, the MoH is currently defining minimum "standards" for the Municipalities' programs.
23.           The government's budgetary constraints mean that only part of total health spending
will be financed out of the State Health Fund and included in the basic package. The remaining
services, including all ambulatory curative services, would be paid out-of-pocket by consumers. This,
of course, raises equity issues which are discussed further below.



Annex C.6
Page 7 of 11
Provider payment methodology
24.           The provider payment system is a key issue to the success of a health reform process in
any country. Different methods generate incentives that influence decisively how providers produce
health services. Depending on the nature of those incentives and on the context they are applied,
payment mechanisms may induce, or not, movement toward improved efficiency, equity, consumer
satisfaction and health status.
25.           The reimbursement system designed by the MoH for the basic package combines
essentially two methods: a case based system for hospitals and capitation for some public health
activities. In the case-based methodology for hospitals, several nosological categories were defined
under the 'basic package', and for each one a standard level of health services was defined and priced.
Hospitals will be reimbursed by the State Health Fund for all patients who are admitted with diagnosis
covered by the basic package. The unit prices include the quantity of medical consultations and tests
for diagnosis purposes, the 'required' drugs and treatment medical acts and also the number of inpatient
days for each category. One concern is the paucity of available information. This kind of payment
methodology requires a significant amount of reliable information on costs and health services
production, which does not seem to be available. This information system is indispensable to build the
model and also to monitor its operation.
26.           The reimbursement system for hospitalization designed by the MoH team is clearly too
complicated, administratively costly, and provides perverse incentives to providers and consumers.
The possible perverse effects of such a reimbursement methodology are quite well known and
described extensively in the literature. One particularly telling example from Georgia is the case of
child deliveries where the price of a normal delivery has been set at US$61, while for a Cesarean the
price is US$ 300, and for a Cesarean with ICU the price is US$1200. These prices, paying almost
twenty times more for a delivery which includes a stay of the mother in an ICU relative to a 'normal'
delivery has some negative impacts: (i) it is a strong incentive to increase artificially the utilization of
more sophisticated resources, causing an increase of health care costs (exactly the opposite to the
intentions of the reimbursement system), thereby reducing efficiency; and (ii) it may cause some
iatrogenic effects on the patients.
27.           For public health services covered by the basic package, such as immunization and
some matemal and child health services, providers will be paid an age-adjusted capitation fee ranging
from US$1 to US$2 per capita. The details of how this system will be implemented, however, remain
unclear
28.           The most important source of revenue for health care provision will continue to be
direct payments from patients The co-existence of these reimbursement systems raises an important
issue, because the official one intends to be an incentive to efficiency, standardizing all the diagnosis
and treatment procedures; the direct payments are an open-ended system with incentives to providers to
increase sector expenditures. There is a clear issue of adverse consequences for patients. This
problem with cost containment will add to the economic difficulties that the GoG has to finance even
the very narrow basic package to be covered by the state health program.



Annex C.6
Page 8 of 11
Coverage and Access
29.          Historically, coverage of health services has been quite high in Georgia. Although
there were regional differences in the availability of medical services, coverage and access were
adequate. The country's financial crisis, however, has greatly limited the availability of affordable,
adequate quality health facilities. Helicopters which once served the mountainous regions in winter no
longer function. Ambulance services have dropped dramatically, further limiting the physical access
for many people. Furthermore, there is evidence that many poor people cannot afford to pay the
amounts currently required for medicine, drugs, and food in addition to the payments they must make
to physicians and nurses. A 1994 national household survey found that 27 percent of the poor were
unable to afford medical care. These physical and financial limitations to access may explain the
decline in utilization rates for hospitalization and outpatient visits over the last three years. As an
example, in 1994 around 10 percent of pregnant women gave birth at home because they could not
afford the costs for a normal delivery.
30.          With two thirds of the population under the poverty line and approximately 800,000 in
the most vulnerable groups (including more than 270,000 displaced people), the Government is
exploring ways to ensure that the very poor will have access to the essential health services which are
not included in the basic benefits package. The two issues which remain unresolved are: (1) how to
identify the poorest in an economy where a large part of the activity is informal and poverty is a
widespread yet new social phenomena and (2) how to finance a subsidized program to support access to
basic health services by the poor when public financial resources are extremely scarce and institutional
capabilities are very weak. In the short-term, the Government chose to address the needs of the poor
by designating Hospital Number 9 and several specialty hospitals to serve the needs of the poor. A
total of 275 beds were designated to treat the most vulnerable groups in Tbilisi: single unemployed
mothers with three or more children, single pensioners with pensions below US$2 and single invalids.
Other institutions, like the Veteran's Hospital, are financed by the State to continue providing care to
their clients. The Government has since refined the system by providing vouchers to the groups
identified above and allowing them free choice of provider. Improved means testing with a World
Bank supported household income survey planned for 1996 and a second household demand and
expenditure survey, replicating the one prepared by UNICEF in August-September 1995, is planned
for September 1998, to provide information on poverty evolution and its implications on health. In the
future, the government may wish to improve the horizontal equity of its health system by encouraging
risk adjustments other than age in its capitation payments to providers and to improve vertical equity by
looking at sources of revenue that are more progressive.
E. The Health Financing Component
31.          The health financing component has three major objectives: (1) assure that scarce
resources are used in a cost-effective manner to improve the health of the Georgian population; (2)
assure the equitable distribution of financing both in the collection and allocation of public funds, with
special emphasis on the position of socially vulnerable groups and the balance of individual and
collective responsibility for health; and (3) assure sustainable financing of the health sector in order to
meet population needs. The project proposes to meet these objectives through the following activities:



Annex C6
Page 9 of 11
Development of the State Health Fund System
32.          The objectives are to increase the technical efficiency of financing by introducing
improved accounting and revenue collection methods, as well as to strengthen allocative efficiency
through the introduction of provider contracting and payment methods, and the introduction of a
management information system. This activity will be implemented by the State Health Fund. Specific
activities include:
33.          Management information system (MIS): A centrally defined MIS will be an essential
tool to drive the reform successfully. Information on resources, utilization and outcomes must be
standardized and routinely collected, worked out and distributed to all the key actors. The MIS will be
designed from the central level, on a normative way, defining the general routines. Also, an analytical
and critical function will be performed, monitoring all the economic issues of the health system, with
special focus on the provider payment mechanisms and on the provider competition. A methodology to
fix prices will be developed in order to legitimize the direct payments from the patients. The objectives
of MIS include improved management capacities, regulation of resources flow, maintenance,
technology management, quality assessment and cost effectiveness studies. The project will: (i) define
the needs of routine information from the providers (financial, production and resources), from the
Regional Health Funds and from other central departments; (ii) define the routine tasks on
management information: circulation, time limits, responsibilities, methodologies of data aggregation
and processing, and information users; (iii) develop methods for the assessment of the provider
payment system and its impact on the supply and production of health care and access of patients; and
(iv) develop methods for the evaluation of the provider competition in terms of the impact on technical
and allocative efficiency.
34.          Develop capacity in financial procedures and accounting: Technical assistance will be
provided in financial management to revise basic operational procedures of the SHF. It will review the
existing budget and provide advice on the planning process for future budgets. Assistance is also
required in the management of fund reserves, since the present expectations are very imprecise. This
consultancy will help the Fund to improve the management at a national and regional level. Technical
assistance will also be provided in accounting methods development and in designing the training
curricula in accounting and auditing systems. In the first year the support will concentrate on training
staff in concepts like double entry book-keeping, accrual accounting and depreciation of assets. In the
second year the focus will be in auditing systems and developing curriculum for further training
actions.
35.          Develop planninW capacity: The sustainability of the health reform depends a lot on the
self-financing capacity of the SHF. It is vital to anticipate revenues and expenditures. In present, it
lacks any expertise in actuarial planning. Technical assistance will be provided to conduct an initial
actuarial analysis and assess the available information to calculate risk factors for the development of
models that enable the SHF to forecast expected expenditures both at national and regional level.
Technical assistance will also be required to use the results of the actuarial analysis and combine it with
present data on health care demand, assumptions about revenue collection (for which, specific
methodologies should be developed) and patterns of service delivery. The resultant models will permit
the SHF to measure the expected impact on revenue and expenditures under different scenarios.
36.          Economic evaluation of health care technologies and programs: The SHF must be able
to assess the efficiency of the financing and provision of health care. This information is indispensable



Annex C.6
Page 10 of 11
to decision makers who must define the basic package, assess the desirability of introducing
sophisticated new equipment, and make other vital decisions. Technical assistance will be provided to
help to identify the local staff, to define the routine collection of data, to develop methodologies of
economic assessment and to advise how to distribute  the results of the studies.
Development of Regional Health Funds
37.          Regional Health Funds (RHF) will be in place during 1995. The RHF will administer
the budgeting, monitoring and data collection of the system at the local level. The project will provide
technical assistance to develop its capacities, with special focus on the management issues. According
to the responsibilities defined by the MIS sub-component, local courses must be carried out in the phase
of implementation. Also support in computers and software shall be provided. Specific activities
include:
38.          RHF accounting system: Under the new payment methodology, it is important that
hospitals and health Funds employ a uniform system of accounting and data management to allow
smooth exchange of information and reimbursement between them. The lack of an uniform system
hampers efficient management and the analysis of health care costs and the revision of the price list.
The system of cost accounting, budgeting, service monitoring and evaluation should be strengthened.
Technical assistance shall be required to develop accounting procedures to be used by RHF in
accordance with the principals defined inside the MIS. Implementation of one accounting, budgeting
and financial management integrated system would occur in a first stage in one selected region. The
project would support: (i) a book-keeping system adapted to health care services, (ii) a cost accounting
system, (iii) a budgeting system, and (iv) hard and software for the above systems.
39.          RHF financial management system: Assistance will be provided to develop training
programs in financial management and actuarial models. A course developer will work with local staff
to start a first programme and annual courses for SHF staff. About 50 training places will be available,
on a basis of four per RHF. As soon as each RHF have trained people, in management and in
computer support, computers and software will be available, funded by the project. Procurement,
configuring, testing and installation of computers will be in place to implement the MIS and the
accounting system.
Development of provider / Health Fund interface
40.          Providers Accounting System. In most hospitals and other type of providers, there is an
important lack of systems for management and accounting procedures. Technical assistance shall be
required to develop accounting procedures to be used by providers in accordance with the principals
defined inside the MIS. Implementation of one accounting, budgeting and local provider financial
management integrated system would occur in a first stage in the provider institutions selected for
rehabilitation and management development. The project will support: (i) a bookkeeping system
adapted to health care services; (ii) a cost accounting system; (iii) a budgeting system; and (iv) hard and
software or the above systems (for the selected institutions).
41.          Health financing and management training program. The project will support the
development and operation of a management training programme that will provide training to RHF and
providers in health services management, financial operations and contract development. Technical
assistance will be provided to support the development of teaching skills and the elaboration of course
curricula.



Annex C.6
Page 11 of 11
42.           Provider financial management. In most cases there is an important lack of systems for
financial management and accounting procedures. An health financing specialist will help to develop a
financing management policy and accounting procedures to be used by providers. The specialist will
help to identify suitable technical assistance in curriculum development to work with local course
developers to prepare a training programme in financial management for providers and RHF managers.
Training will be provided for 200 people from provider units. A limited number of computers and
software will be available for providers. Procurement, configuring, testing and installation of
computers will begin soon after the project start so that adequate facilities are available for providers to
implement the system recommended by other sub-component projects - MIS and accounting.
Following initial training, the project will help with installation and follow-up user support of the
provider financial management system through local specialists.

ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. Introduction
1.             The proposed project is multifaceted. It addresses not only major macro policy issues
in the health sector such as health care financing, regulating health care providers, and changing
public/private roles, but also supports targeted interventions in maternal and child health and health
promotion in order to address the diseases which cause the greatest burden in Georgia. The following
economic analysis provides the rationale for why this project at this time, and describes the potential
impact of both the proposed project and the Government's health sector reform strategy. The analysis
follows the framework recommended by the World Bank's Human Development Department (HDD),
addressing the following issues: 1) linkages to economic and sector work (section B); 2) analysis of
alternatives (section C); 3) assessment of financial and fiscal impact, public and private affordability,
and cost recovery (section D); 4) analysis of cost-effectiveness, cost utility, and cost benefit (section
E); 5) sensitivity and risk analysis (section F); 6) analysis of institutional capacity (section G); 7)
analysis of poverty alleviation impact (section H); 8) analysis of environmental impact (section I); 9)
performance criteria for monitoring and evaluation (section J); and 10) overall coherence and
completeness of analysis (section K).
B. Linkages to Economic and Sector Work
Strategy for World Bank Assistance
2.             The objectives of the World Bank's assistance strategy to Georgia are:
to help reverse the sharp economic decline of the past few years, to assist the transition to a
market economy and alleviate the poverty which has emerged in Georgia in recent years.
With a particular focus on promoting private sector development and improving the efficiency
of the public sector ... through its lending program, the Bank will support economy-wide and
sector-specific reforms that promote economic growth as well as investments to rehabilitate
and build new capacity in critical infrastructure sectors. The Bank will also assist in
strengthening public management, developing human resource and institutional capacities and
strengthening the social safety net.
3.             Given Georgia's difficult fiscal situation, much of the World Bank's assista nce will
concentrate on improving the cost-effectiveness of government programs and on redirecting public
sector involvement in the economy. Strengthening the social safety net and targeting scarce public
resources to the provision of public goods and to fight against poverty are the major strategic
objectives in the social sectors. Plans to reform the health sector to improve service delivery to the
poor and to privatize the system where possible would not only reduce obligations on the state budget
and improve resource allocation efficiency, but would also help to strengthen Georgia's social safety
net.
I See 'Economic Analysis in the Population, Health and Nutrition Sectors: A Conceptual Framework for
Task Managers" by Alexander Preker (HDD), January 2, 1996. This framework is based on an upcoming book on Economic
Analysis by Pedro Belli, the World Bank, 1996.



AnnLex D
Page 2 of 17
4.            The proposed health project fully complies with and supports the World Bank's
assistance strategy in Georgia by attempting to: a) improve the efficiency of the health care system; b)
create conditions for privatizing health facilities; c) reduce the government's financial role to
providing a basic package of public health and essential clinical services so as to free resources for
targeting the poorest and reinforcing the social safety net; d) improve health services for the poor by
standardizing the quality of services and offering a package of public health and essential clinical
services free of charge; and e) in the longer term, improve the economic productivity of labor by
reducing the burden of disease, especially among adult males.
Economic and Sector Work (ESW)
5.            To gain a more in-depth understanding of the constraints and possibilities of the
Georgian economy, detailed economic and sector work has been completed. In health, most of the
ESW was carried out as part of project preparation. The objectives of the ESW, done in close
collaboration with Georgian colleagues, were to assess the current situation in the health sector, assist
the Government in designing a feasible reform for the sector, and identify and assess various
alternatives for a World Bank financed health project. Two series of technical notes were prepared
(January 1995 and June 1995) covering topics such as: health status, maternal and child health,
hospital efficiency, health facilities and medical equipment, health financing reform, poverty issues in
the health reform, and accreditation, licensing, and quality assurance of health providers. Analysis
done on the health sector as part of the upcoming Public Expenditure Review (PER) also provided
invaluable information on health financing and health expenditures. This ESW was important in
determining what the sectoral priorities were and how best to meet the Government and Bank's
objectives of improving the efficiency of the sector, reducing the burden of disease, and alleviating
poverty. The collaborative work allowed the World Bank to help the Government develop and refine a
health sector reform strategy. The components of the project developed naturally from the priorities
which were identified and the ensuing policy reforms adopted by the Government.
Adequacy of Policy Framework
6.            The Government is committed to a health sector reform which increases public
spending on health, ensures coverage of essential health services to the entire population, improves
quality of health care services, and increases efficiency of the system. Universal coverage and broad
risk pooling were positive features of the old system. However, with the total collapse of the economy
and civil unrest, public provision of services essentially stopped. Utilization rates dropped, indicating
reduced access. The risk pooling which characterized the old system was eliminated as patients were
forced to make illegal payments to doctors and nurses for health care services. The Government-'s
response to this crisis has been radical. It has changed the organization and financing of health
services by separating the provision and financing of health care, introduced new sources of financing
for the sector, and concentrated public spending on a package of public health and essential clinical
services.
7.            While these policy changes mirror many of the reforms taking place in other middle
income countries, it should be noted that the objectives of risk pooling and broad access are somewhat
compromised. First, although the reforms broaden the financial base of the sector beyond general
revenues by introducing new payroll and excise taxes, the existing low rates of tax collection and large
quantity of services that will be paid out of pocket mean that risk pooling is not universal. However,
risk pooling under the reforms will be broader than has been present in the totally unregulated system
of the last four years. Similarly, access to services will not be guaranteed as was the case during the



Annex D
Page 3 of 17
Soviet era, but the reforms, by guaranteeing a package of public health and essential clinical services,
should ensure broader access than has been present since independence.
8.            Restoration of economic growth should improve both risk pooling and coverage. In
that case, presumably the tax base would expand and the scope of the basic package would increase,
reducing the need for out-of-pocket payments by patients.
9.            One risk of the health financing reform is cost escalation. The old system was
controlled by the central government. Under the new system, health care providers will be reimbursed
for services provided on the basis of a diagnostic related group (DRG) type fee schedule. The
difficulties in controlling costs in such a system are well documented in the United States and other
Western European countries, and even more difficult to control in a country like Georgia with little
existing infrastructure to closely monitor provider behavior. Some steps have been taken to control
costs such as capitating public health services and establishing a reference price list for services not
included in the basic package. Perhaps as a reaction to the former system, however, the Georgians
have been very reluctant to adopt other payment methods in order to better contain costs. The project
will attempt to strengthen the Government's capacity to control health expenditures by closely
monitoring the State Health Fund (a credit covenant) and supporting the development of practice
guidelines and training programs to improve the quality and appropriateness of care provided.
10.          The reforms, as described above, are fully consistent with the Government's public
sector investment strategy. The project has been designed to support the Government's health sector
strategy and is consistent with both the Government's health sector investment strategy as well as more
broadly its public sector investment strategy.
C. Analysis of Alternatives
11.          The project's objectives -- to reduce the burden of disease, to improve the efficiency of
the sector, and to improve coverage and access -- complement both the World Bank's assistance
strategy for Georgia (see para 2) as well as the Government's economic reform strategy. Alternatives
in project design were assessed against their contribution to meeting these objectives. Factors which
influenced the choice of alternatives included a project budget constraint (IDA funding of only US$12
million at the time of appraisal and limited Government contribution), the relative cost-effectiveness of
the alternatives, and political will and implementation capacity.
Alternatives to Reduce the Burden of Disease
12.          The overall objective for the Government with regard to health is to improve the health
status of the population. The known determinants of health are socio-economic conditions, individual
life-styles, the health care system and the environment. Consequently, interventions which promote
economic growth will also have a positive impact on health status. Georgia is undertaking economic
reform with the assistance of the IMF and the World Bank and positive growth is expected to begin in
1996. However, improvements in living standards will be gradual and a return to 1993 levels of
consumption is not expected until the turn of the century. Improvements in health status attributable to
economic growth will lag even further. Improvements in the environment are not expected to greatly
improve health status in Georgia. A recent study found that less than one percent of the burden of
disease in Former Soviet republics is caused by inadequate water or sewerage systems. The project,



Annex D
Page 4 of 17
therefore, emphasizes improving the health care system and changing the life-styles of individuals in
order to reduce the burden of disease.
13.           A burden of disease analysis was undertaken as part of project preparation. The results
of the analysis, using the disability adjusted life year (DALYs) methodology, show that the two groups
where the most DALYs are lost due to premature deaths are infants (population under one year of age)
and individuals aged 60 to 64 years. Fifty percent of infant deaths occur in the perinatal period.
Looking across diseases, the study found that cerebro-vascular disorders and ischemic diseases are the
most important health problems in Georgia, followed by accidents for men and breast cancer for
women. On the basis of this study as well as what was learned from economic and sector work, the
project was designed with two very specific targets: a) reduce perinatal mortality and permanent
disabilities by improving the quality of maternal and child health services and b) reduce adult mortality
and morbidity by preventing circulatory diseases and accidents by improving public information and
prevention programs.
14.           Project activities which are intended to improve the quality of maternal and child
health services include: a) strengthening the MoH's institutional capacity to develop health policies,
health promotion activities and services for women and infants including development of protocols of
care, rules for licensing staff and facilities, guidelines for privatizing and reorganizing services, and a
perinatal mortality surveillance system; b) developing training programs in maternal and child health,
obstetrics and pediatrics; c) creating a national Task Force for Child and Maternal Survival, which
will build national commitment, increase funding, and develop public awareness of child and maternal
survival issues; d) creating regional Perinatal Centers in Tbilisi and Kutaisi with a referral system to
treat all high risk pregnancies and newborns with complications and demonstrate to residents and other
hospitals how perinatal services may be improved; and e) refurbishing and equipping of maternal and
child health services in the Tsageri District Hospital to demonstrate how to improve services in remote
areas; and
15.           The project aims to reduce perinatal mortality by improving the quality of existing
maternal and child health services. This was determined to be both more effective and less expensive
than introducing new services. Refurbishment of the two Perinatal Centers in Tbilisi and Kutaisi is the
most expensive input in the project. The Ministry of Health first identified 16 facilities nationwide as
potential candidates. An assessment was made of each of these facilities and the two facilities chosen
on the basis of their geographical location, projected cost of refurbishment, political commitment of
the mayors, willingness of the hospital directors to undertake reforms, and the preferences of the
Ministry of Health. The Tbilisi facility will also offer cardiac services and serve as the center for a
secondary cardiac care prevention program, which is discussed below.
16.          To address the problems of adult health, the project invests in smoking reduction,
cardio-vascular diseases prevention and injury control programs. The cost-effectiveness of health
promotion and disease prevention is well known and substantiated in a cost-effectiveness analysis
which was completed as part of project preparation (see paras 39-40). Given the lag in achieving
measurable improvements in health status for some programs such as circulatory diseases, the project
will also support a secondary prevention program which is intended to educate the public on the
symptoms of heart attacks and strokes in order to encourage them to seek timely treatment and also
educate health professionals on the best treatment. In order to improve the likelihood of success of
these prevention programs, the project will establish a health promotion fund which will provide grants
to comnmunities, NGOs, and governmental agencies on a competitive basis. Alternatives which were
rejected included administering the programs through the existing San-Epid system or distributing



Annex D
Page 5 of 17
monies to communities strictly on a per-capita basis. The project will also have a positive impact on
adult health by training health professionals in modem public health practice and family medicine.
Alternatives to Improve Efficiency and Quality
17.           Economic and sector work revealed that one of the major problems Georgia faces is an
excess supply of both doctors and hospitals. Furthermore, the quality of health services is poor
because health professionals are poorly trained and the health infrastructure is in a poor state of repair
and often inadequately equipped. In order to improve the efficiency of the sector, the project will
reduce the size and improve the quality of health sector personnel by: (a) removing physicians and
other personnel from the government payroll, allowing competition to rationalize the number in the
sector; (b) instituting a licensing exam for currently practicing as well as recently graduated
physicians; (c) creating a Center for Continuing Education to provide training in public health, family
medicine, nursing, and administration to existing health personnel; and (d) limiting the entry to
medical schools. These actions were chosen over other alternatives because they would rationalize the
supply of doctors in a transparent way and allow the best quality doctors to continue to practice while
providing training for others in critical areas. There was debate as to whether it was necessary to
regulate the entry to medical schools or allow the market to control supply. However, with more than
28 medical schools opening since 1992, the Government requested the Bank's support in taking such
drastic measures. Whether to base the Center for Continuing Education within one of the existing
medical faculties was also considered and ultimately rejected because it was felt that it would be
difficult to change the existing establishment, including the attitudes of the faculty and easier to have a
free-standing entity which could hire guest faculty on the basis of their qualifications.
18.           The project will reduce the number and improve the quality and efficiency of health
care facilities by: (a) privatizing most facilities and granting management autonomy to those that
remain in the public sector, creating a mixed, potentially competitive public/private market for patient
services; (b) reimbursing facilities for services delivered rather than by the number of beds and other
norms; (c) licensing and accrediting all health facilities; (d) introducing preventive maintenance of
facilities and equipment, and upgrading service quality and management; and (e) refurbishing three
existing facilities where perinatal services will be offered and as a demonstration of how to improve
efficiency within existing facilities. This approach is based on the belief that before undertaking major
physical refurbishment of the existing health care infrastructure, the number of facilities must be
reduced and incentive systems developed to improve the internal efficiency of the institutions. The
health facilities will be refurbished under the project, including the Kutaisi Pediatric and Maternity
Hospitals, which agreed to merge, will be a powerful demonstration of how to better efficiency as well
as provide integrated maternal and child health services. If more money were available from IDA, the
project would have financed additional refurbishment once rationalization of the sector had occurred.
19.           The project will improve the allocative efficiency of health services in Georgia by
supporting the Government's state health program which shifts scarce public resources from primarily
curative care to a basic package of public health (immunization, healthy children and safe motherhood,
prevention of socially dangerous diseases, epidemiological surveillance, and health promotion) and
essential clinical services (deliveries, treatment of children under the age of 14, treatment of TB,
inpatient psychiatric care, provision of drugs for terminally ill patients, and emergency services). The
remaining services are provided fee-for-service, with exemptions made for the most vulnerable
groups. The items included in the basic package were selected using six criteria: epidemiological
significance, social significance, life-threatening conditions, need for emergency care, effectiveness of
medical intervention, and cost of treatment. Support will be provided under the project to evaluate
and expand the contents of this basic package.



Annex D
Page 6 of 17
Alternatives to Improve Coverage and Access
20.           Between 1990 and 1994, public finance for health services collapsed in Georgia. The
system only survived because of surreptitious payments from patients to doctors and nurses. With
deliveries costing as much as $150 and cesarean sections between $300 and $400, it is not surprising
that utilization rates fell by fifty percent. The alternative the Government has chosen to improve
coverage is guaranteeing access to a basic package of public health and essential clinical services
described above. The Government did consider charging those who could afford to pay co-payments
for clinical services included in the basic package in order to be able to expand services for the poor.
However, this idea was rejected for now because of the difficulties in means testing. Ultimately,
universal coverage and access will only be achievable when the economy improves.
Public/Private Justification
21.           The government's strategy is to privatize most of the delivery of health services,
eventually retaining only regional hospitals in the public domain and converting the Ministry of
Health's role to one primarily of regulation. Until facilities are privatized, they will be managerially
independent in an attempt to improve their internal efficiency. Privatization of dental clinics and
pharmacies has already begun and is proceeding well. These are the types of services, however, for
which the private sector is most likely to want and be able to deliver. It is less certain that the
Government will succeed in privatizing a sufficient number of hospitals to ensure adequate
geographical coverage for the population. This mix of public and private facilities will need to be
monitored in order to ensure that socially optimal amounts of care are provided.
22.           On the financing side, market failures dictate a larger role for the public sector. While
the Government is shifting more responsibility of financing to a payroll tax, it will continue to allocate
funds from general revenues to health care to cover the costs of services which are public goods or
have large externalities. These include public health services with transmission externalities such as
prevention of communicable diseases, as well as services which can prevent disabilities, which place a
large economic burden on society. Maternal and child health services, for example, can prevent
disabilities associated with low birth weight and delivery complications.
D. Financial Assessment
Financial and Fiscal Impact
23.           While Georgia was part of the Soviet Union, the health sector received from 2.8 to
4.1 % of GDP. The situation grew worse with the collapse of the economy. In 1994, public
expenditures on health fell to 0.3% of GDP and 1.3% of public sector expenditures. Annual public
expenditures on health averaged less than US$1 per person in 1994, less than 10% of 1990 expenditure
levels in real terms. The fall in real expenditures was accompanied by a shift in the line item
composition of expenditures. The rising costs of heating oil and other hard currency supplies crowded
out spending on wages and maintenance in an increasingly limited budget. In 1990, wages and
supplements were 52% of total public sector health expenditures. By 1993, they had fallen to 39%
and in 1994 to 32% of the totals.



Annex D
Page 7 of 17
24.           The Government is aware that restoring health expenditures to past levels would be
impossible and undesirable, and is undertaking reforms (privatization and introduction of user charges)
to transfer to the private sector responsibility for the provision and financing of health services. But
with the current decline in income, this could lead to an irreversible deterioration of the human and
physical stock of the country, jeopardizing the potential for growth. At current levels of public
expenditure, it is also likely that the poor will have limited access to basic social services. The macro
economic prospects are such that even with timely implementation of reforms, Georgia's economy will
not reach the 1993 levels of per-capita consumption (already depressed relative to 1990) before the end
of the century.
25.           The government's policy response to increase revenues for the sector has been to
introduce a mandatory payroll tax for health with 3 percent employer contribution and I percent
employee contribution. Central budget allocations for health as well as revenues from the payroll tax,
privatization, licensing fees, and interest income will go to the newly created State Health Fund
(SHF). The SHF will act as a financial intermediary and reimburse both public and private health care
providers for a basic package of preventive and essential clinical health care services included in the
State Health Program. In addition municipalities are obligated to provide a package of services under
the Municipal Health Program. With these additional sources of revenues, public sector health
spending is projected to reach US$3.68 per capita in 1995 and US$9 in 1996. Using World Bank/IMF
macro-economic projections as well as reasonable assumptions about the health sector, public sector
spending on health should reach US$20 (1995 $) per capita by the year 2000 or 2.6% of GDP. Health
care services not included in the State Health Program or the Municipal Health Program will be paid
for out-of-pocket by consumers.
26.           The current level of payroll taxes are quite high in Georgia: 45 % combined
contribution rate for social security, unemployment insurance, and health insurance. Tax collection,
however, remains extremely low, increasing only from 3 percent of GDP in 1994 to 3.5 % in the first
half of 1995. There are a small number of contributors and low reported wages in the formal sector.
The World Bank has been sufficiently concerned with the adverse effects on labor and production costs
that a condition of the Structural Adjustment Credit (SAC) is to reduce the contribution rate to
Employment Fund by 2 percentage points and reduce the contribution rate to the United Fund for
Social Security and Social Insurance by 8 percentage points. Given the plans to reduce total tax
burden, the 4% payroll tax for health is believed to be manageable. The Government has kept the rate
low compared to other countries such as Hungary (14%) and Croatia (20%) by relying on other
sources of finance for health. The "3 + 1 " payroll tax accounts for approximately 25 % of the revenues
of the State Health Fund.
27.           Even with additional sources of financing, however, government revenues will remain
insufficient to finance critical public expenditures without increased efficiency and reallocation of
public spending. One key issue to address is the inefficiency of the low wages in the budgetary sector.
The budgetary sector in Georgia is marked by two distinct traits: overemployment combined with a
scarcity of key skills and extremely low pay. Low wages, a result of budgetary constraints, can cause
government workers to spend much of their time looking for additional sources of income and makes it
difficult to attract or retain skilled workers. This not only lowers efficiency, but also jeopardizes the
Government's ability to perform basic functions. Aware of these issues, the Government initiated a
policy of labor retrenchment, reducing the number of positions in the budgetary sector about 40
percent between January and December 1995. Nearly two-thirds (120,000) of these positions were in
the health sector. As part of the health sector reform, employees of the health sector were gradually
removed from the government payroll. Those that remain employed are paid directly by the hospitals
and clinics that have become managerially independent firms.



Annex D
Page 8 of 17
Public and Private Affordability
28.          Affordability of the reformslGovernmentperspective: The Government's reform
program is designed in such a way to be "affordable" given the country's current economic crisis.
Minimum allocations for health are being included in the SAC and health spending has been discussed
by the IMF and COD. Public sector financing for the sector is projected to increase, but still remain
at an affordable 2 to 3 % of GDP over the next decade. There are nonetheless, concerns about the
sustainability of central budget financing (approximately 50% of the Fund) and the ability of the
Government to collect the tax revenues earmarked for the Fund (25% from payroll taxes and 10%
from excise taxes). Furthermore, government projections show State Health Fund expenditures
exceeding revenues by approximately 11 million lari in 1996. These Government estimates of
expenditures are based on current utilization patterns and do not consider latent demand that is
suppressed by unaffordability or poor quality of services. If the Government is unable to control
utilization, the cost of the basic package will exceed projections and add to the projected deficit.  The
project will address these issues by requiring quarterly statements from the Fund and providing
technical assistance during the project, particularly for developing the MIS necessary to monitor the
system as well as for revising the provider payment method if necessary.
29.          Affordability of the reforms/private perspective. The affordability of health care
services for the population has been a major concern of both the World Bank and the Government. A
national survey found that 27 % of the population could not afford essential health services. The total
collapse of the public system has left patients responsible for paying for all health care. And the care
is not cheap. The average price of a delivery is US$150, compared to an average per capita income of
US$363 in 1995. The project, by supporting a basic package of essential and public health services
will make health services available free of charge. It will also increase household incomes by
decreasing the burden of disease for disability and death.
30.           With two thirds of the population under the Government's defined poverty line and
approximately 800,000 in the most vulnerable groups (including more than 270,000 displaced people),
the Government is exploring ways to ensure that the very poor will have access to the essential health
services which are not included in the basic benefits package. The two issues which remain
unresolved are: (1) how to identify the poorest in an economy where a large part of the activity is
informal and poverty is a widespread yet new social phenomena and (2) how to finance a subsidized
program to support access to basic health services by the poor when public financial resources are
extremely scarce and institutional capabilities are very weak.
31.          Initially, the Government has chose to address the problem by designating Hospital #9
and several specialty hospitals to serve the needs of the poor. A total of 275 beds were designated to
treat the most vulnerable groups free of charge: single unemployed mothers with three or more
children, single pensioners with pensions below US$2 and single invalids. Other institutions, like the
Veteran's Hospital, were financed by the State to continue providing care to their clients. In February
1996, the Government revised the system by providing vouchers to the targeted groups and allowing
them to use any health facility of their choice. In order to monitor the affordability of health services
under the reform, the project includes a study of utilization to monitor private expenditures and
financial barriers to access.
32.          Affordability of the project: The tentative project financing plan includes US$7.7
million in Government contribution. This amount includes all applicable taxes and duties, estimated to



Annex D
Page 9 of 17
be about US$2.5 million. The total government contribution, net of taxes, represents about 2% of
total public sector spending on health during the project implementation period. The estimated project
costs include US$1.6 million to cover incremental operating cost including operational and
maintenance expenditure for equipment, vehicles and buildings. The only incremental salaries
included in the project costs are those of component coordinators since it is assumed that all new
positions will be filled by reassigned MoH staff. These project costs are expected to be within
sustainable limits since measures are being instituted in the health sector to reduce recurrent costs,
improve efficiency and increase collection of user charges.
Cost Recovery
33.           Georgia went through an exercise of costing more than 6,000 disease classifications,
including the costs of personnel, drugs and supplies, hospital overhead, and profit. The services
which are included in the basic package are provided free of charge to patients and reimbursed to
providers on the basis of the established prices. Services not included in the basic package are paid for
by user charges, at prices set by hospitals. However, the reference prices have been made available to
all hospitals and those patients who are eligible for an expanded set of services (the poor and veterans)
are reimbursed on the basis of this list.
E. Burden of Disease Study and Cost-Effectiveness Analysis of Health Interventions
34.           As part of project preparation, a study was undertaken to analyze the cost-effectiveness
of several proposed programs in disease prevention and maternal and child care to be included in the
project: reduction of tobacco consumption; cardio-vascular disease prevention, and traffic injury
control. The project will finance perinatal centers in both Tbilisi and Kutaisi, but only Kutaisi was
included in this analysis because at the time of the study the site for the Tbilisi center had not been
selected and cost estimates were unavailable. The study had the following specific objectives: (a) to
estimate the burden of disease in Georgia, studying its distribution by nosologies, age, and gender; (b)
to estimate present health care costs associated with the health problems targeted under the project; (c)
to estimate potential health gains achievable through the interventions; and (d) to analyze the cost-
effectiveness of the three prevention programs and the Kutaisi MCH program.



Annex D
Page 10 of 17
Methodology2
35.             The 1993 World Development Report recommends estimating the burden of disease by
calculating the number of Disability Adjusted Life Years -- DALYs -- lost because of death and
illness. DALYs combine into a single measure the number of years of life lost due to premature death
and years of life lived with a disability. Unfortunately, morbidity data in Georgia are unreliable,
therefore, the global burden of disease analysis was completed using data only on mortality.
However, in order to estimate more accurately the burden of diseases targeted in the project's disease
prevention program, morbidity data from Estonia were combined with Georgia's data to produce
estimates of "disability" DALYs for the following diseases: ischemic heart disease, lung cancer,
chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), cerebro-vascular disease, and traffic accidents. The
study uses 1990 mortality data as the baseline because these data were determined to be the most
reliable.
36.             To estimate the cost-effectiveness of the proposed programs, the study compares what
would happen if the programs were not implemented with the expected cost and benefits as described
in the project's Staff Appraisal Report. The cost estimates took into account: a) direct costs of
diagnosis, treatment, follow-up and other health care services; b) quantifiable indirect costs (losses in
production); and c) costs of implementing the program. A discount rate of 3% was applied to all costs
as well as to all DALYs saved.3
Burden of Disease
37.             The two age groups where the number of life years lost due to premature death is
greatest are infants under the age of one and adults aged 60 to 64. In 1990, for example, 1,557
potential life years were lost (using standard life expectancies) because of partially avoidable infant
deaths. These results clearly justify the existence of a strong MCH component in the Georgia Health
Project. Further analysis of the DALYs lost by cause (Table 1) suggests that the project's emphasis on
preventing smoking, cardio-vascular disease, and traffic accidents is well targeted. The main health
problems of Georgia, in terms of years of life lost to premature death, are ischemic disease, stroke,
and atherosclerosis.   Other important causes of death are accidents and trauma, particularly for men,
and breast cancer for women. In the middle of this short list of main causes of death is cirrhosis and
other diseases of the liver, most likely related to alcohol consumption.
38.             Table 1 only considers the life years lost due to premature death, which
underestimates the importance of some diseases which are not major contributors to death, but do
result in significant losses due to disability. In order to more accurately assess the cost-effectiveness
of possible interventions, the study estimated disability DALYs for five conditions: ischemic heart
2 Data used in this analysis came from a variety of sources, including the Government of Georgia, the World Health
Organization, the World Bank, and other international and local publications. Local experts were also consulted. Nearly all
of the sources note the dearth of reliable statistics in Georgia, due in part to the old Soviet system that demanded 'politically
acceptable" health indicators as well as Georgia's recent periods of civil war, unrest, and economic decline. In order to avert
some of the problems with the data, the study used 1990 data on mortality and drew from the experiences of other former
Soviet Republics, principally Estonia, to estimate morbidity and program effectiveness.
I Sensitivity analyses were done using 5% and 8% discount rates and revealed no significant changes in the results of this
study.
' Unlike other countries, Georgian statistics classify atherosclerosis as a cause of death rather than as a risk factor. If all
deaths recorded under ICD 9 number 440 were classified as cerebro-vascular deaths, the contribution of stroke and other
CVDs to premature mortality would increase and clearly be the major problem to be dealt with by the Public Health
authorities.



Annex D
Page 11 of 17
disease, lung cancer, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), cerebro-vascular disorders and
traffic accidents. As shown in Table 2, these health problems and diseases represent in Georgia a
total burden of 432,000 DALYs lost -- 211,000 due to premature death and 220,000 due to disability.
The distribution across death and disability DALYs is not symmetric. As expected, COPDs have a
much higher disability burden than death burden. On the other hand, traffic accidents and ischemic
heart disease have a greater impact on DALYs lost due to premature death than to disability.
Cerebro-vascular disorders remain the most significant health problem in Georgia, accounting for
almost one third of all DALYs lost in this group.

Cost-Effectiveness Analysis
39.             Although it is difficult to forecast and to quantify the expected health outcomes of the
programs proposed under the Georgia health project, this study draws upon the considerable amount of
existing cost-effectiveness literature as well as Estonian experience to make assumptions about
program outcomes. These assumption are summarized in Table 3. 7 Using these assumptions, nearly
200,000 discounted DALYs could be saved by the prevention programs by 2005; nearly 30,000 from
smoking prevention programs, 117,000 from cardio-vascular diseases, and 52,000 from traffic
accidents.
Table 3: Expected Program Outcomes
Program                      Expected Program Outcome
Smoking prevention            15% decrease in total smoking prevalence8.
Ischemic Heart Disease       2% yearly reduction in incidence and mortality rates from 1996
prevention                   to 1999 and maintenance afterwards.
Traffic Accident prevention    5% yearly reduction in total accidents from 1996 to 1999 and
maintenance afterwards.
Cerebro-vascular             3% yearly reduction in incidence and mortality rates from 1996
Disorders Prevention         to 1999 and maintenance afterwards.
40.             Table 4 lists the DALYs saved as well as the discounted implementation costs for each
program (as provided in the Health Project Staff Appraisal Report, projecting out until 2005). On
average, it will cost US$2.83 (discounted) to save one discounted DALY with the disease prevention
programs. The ischemic and cerebro-vascular disease prevention program is the most cost-effective,
while the smoking prevention is the least effective. While the impact of the Kutaisi MCH component
will be large, the costs per DALY saved is significantly higher than the prevention programs. This is
due, in part, to the investment in construction and equipment of this component. Obvious benefits
such as demonstrating how to rationalize facilities, raising the standards of care in not only the
matemity and pediatric wards but also in other departments, and improving hospital management, are
likely to generate substantial savings which are difficult to quantify and not included in the analysis.
' Outcomes for the disease prevention programs are limited to the target population between the ages of 15 and 64.
' The outcome from a 15% reduction in total smoking prevalence was estimated as follows: reductions
in mortality and morbidity will affect mostly men and will start only in year 2001 for a program initiated
in 1996. Initially there will be reduction of roughly 2.5% for COPD and lung cancer and of about 1% for
ischemic heart disease. The yearly reductions will increase up to 2004, when we estimate they will reach
9% for COPDs and lung cancer and 4.5% for ischemic heart disease. For women, the most significant
figure is an expected 1% reduction in lung cancer by 2004.

Cost-benefit analysis
41.           In addition to a cost-effectiveness analysis, a cost-benefit analysis of the program was
also completed as part of the study. In contrast to the cost-effectiveness analysis, where the outcomes
of the program were measured in DALYs saved, the cost-benefit analysis places a monetary value on
the benefits of the program by estimating the amount saved in treatment costs as well as days of work
gained. These benefits are also discounted at a rate of 3 %. The study estimated the marginal savings
of the prevention program by comparing the direct and indirect costs with and without the programs.
Direct costs include the total costs of treatment, using the Government's estimates of average costs by
nosological category for 1995. Estimnates of the indirect costs of lost production were based on
assumptions of work lost, employment rates by age and sex, and average salaries. The total amount
of money expected to be saved is US$35.5 million. Table 5 compares, by program, the total costs of
the prevention programs with the baseline option of doing nothing. Most of the savings (78% or
US$27.6 million) will come in preventing ischemic and cerebro-vascular diseases. While the other
two programs, smoking prevention and accident prevention, will save less (approximately US$1.6 and
US$6.3 million respectively), there is a clearer rationale for public intervention.
Table 5: Total cost savings (benefits) of intervention alternatives
(US $1,000)

Smoking                             109,253          100,775         8,478
Ischemic and Cerebro-Vascular       260,861          234,201         2,660
diseases
Traffic Accidents                     37,018         36,637           381
Total                               407,132          371,613        35,519
42.           Table 6 illustrates the ratio of the "benefits" of each program, as defined above, with
the costs of each program which were presented in Table 4. The ischemic and cerebro-vascular
disease prevention program has the most favorable benefit-cost ratio. For each dollar spent on this
program, US$115 would be saved. Even the program with the lowest benefit-cost ratio, smoking
prevention, would yield almost US$15 in benefits for each dollar spent. For the entire package of
disease prevention activities proposed under the project, it can be expected that for each US dollar

spent it will be possible to avoid US$63 in total costs (treatment plus indirect costs). Because of the
difficulties in estimating the monetary benefits of the Kutaisi MCH program, it was excluded from this
analysis.
