43.           Taking into account all these issues, the main conclusions of the study are: i) the
three prevention programs included in the Georgia Health Project are related to major adult health
problems of the Georgian population, which caused in 1990 an estimated loss of 432,805 DALYs; ii)
assuming the effectiveness and the expected outcomes of the proposed prevention programs, there is a
potential to save 198,908 DALYs by the year 2005 at an average cost of US$2.83 for each DALY
saved; ii) for each US dollar spent on prevention under the project, US$63 can be saved in treatment
and indirect costs. These figures show that the programs have a high level of cost-effectiveness. In
addition, the Kutaisi maternal and child health component of the project targets one of the groups
contributing the most years of life lost to society due to premature death --infants. The component
could save 16,489 years of life at an average cost of US$138 per life saved.
F. Risk Analysis
44.           The Government faces substantial financial, political and institutional risks in
implementing both its ambitious health care reform program as well as the project. A full risk
assessment was not done as part of project preparation. However, to the extent possible, risks were
quantified as part of sensitivity analyses of the project's benefits. For example, sensitivity analyses
included as part of the cost-effectiveness study found that changing the discount rate or expected
program outcomes did not substantially change the study's conclusions. The project has also taken
steps to address the most obvious risks associated with institutional capacity in the MoH to implement
the project by setting up a strong PCU.
Financial Risks
45.           Given the collapse of the economy and the potential for high cost inflation in health
spending, one of the major risks the Government faces is the financial sustainability of the health care
reform. In projecting the fiscal impact of the reforms, simulations placed the range of per capita
expenditures on health between US$8.34 and US$11.40 (8.6% and 11.3% of public sector spending)
in 1996 and between US$20.64 and US$32.09 (8.6% and 13.5% of public sector spending) in the year
2000. In order to ensure continued Government commitment to the health sector even under a
pessimistic macroeconomic scenario both the Health Project and the Structural Adjustment Credit have
included minimum spending levels on health as conditions of lending. The Structural Adjustment
Credit specifies that at least 7 % of public spending must go to health and that this amount should rise
as revenues increase. Under the health project, the Government has been asked to provide assurances



Annex D
Page 15 of 17
that it would ensure the viability of the State Health Fund without reducing the scope of services
included in the basic benefits package and monitor and provide to the World Bank quarterly financial
statements of the Fund.
46.           Another financial risk the project faces is the ability of the Government to contribute
its part of project costs. Although the amount is felt to be reasonable given the size of the health
budget (see para 32), the experience of the Municipal Infrastructure Project suggests that the
Government may have difficulty meeting its obligations. In order to mitigate this risk, a condition of
credit effectiveness will be that the Government open a counterpart contribution account and deposit
US$400,000 into this account and continue to replenish this account quarterly. The Ministry of
Finance has already written a letter to the World Bank pledging the necessary quarterly contributions.
Political Risks
47.          In the recent past, Georgia has been plagued by political instability, highlighted by
civil wars and the assassination attempt on Mr. Shevardnadze. In July 1995, however, the Parliament
approved a new constitution, paving the way for presidential and parliamentary elections. In
November, Mr. Shevardnadze was elected president with a large majority. Since the elections, he has
succeeded in purging the mnafia and it is expected that the new Government will be fully committed to
the economic reforms. The Minister of Health, who is widely perceived to be one of the most reform
minded members of the Government, will remain in his position along with most of his higher level
staff. While the situation in Abkhazia has stabilized, a growing concern is the war in Chechnya and
possibility it will spread to Georgia and other countries in the Caucasus region.
48.           Another political risk is that the Government will encounter resistance to the reforms
from important stakeholders such as doctors, hospitals, and medical schools. Much of the project
emphasizes changing the behavior of providers, including what they practice (from highly specialized,
curative medicine to primary health care), how they practice (from low quality, unregulated medicine
to care guided by clinical practice standards), and the incentives they face (from salaried positions to
one where earnings are based on productivity). There will be identifiable winners and losers in the
reforms; a necessity if Georgia is to reduce the excess supply. The Government has tried to minimize
this risk by providing good unemployment benefits for doctors and providing retraining for those who
wish to change their specialty or leave the profession. Providers have had and will continue to have an
important role in the preparation and implementation of the project, including areas such as developing
licensing criteria, practice standards, and educational curricula.
G. Institutional Capacity
49.           Weak institutional capacity within the Government and the Ministry of Health remains
a major risk, inspite of the recent political and legislative advances. The major issues are the
management and administration capacity of the Ministry of Health, including its various departments
and agencies such as the State Health Fund. To mitigate this risk, component coordinators were
identified in each of the project's implementing agencies very early on in project preparation (October
1994). They will have primary responsibility for implementing the activities under their components.
Much technical assistance and training was provided during project preparation and will continue
during the project. The supervision plan for the project includes more than the average number of
staff weeks. A more thorough institutional analysis, covering not only the Ministry but also health
care providers, will be done as part of preparation for a second health project.



Annex D
Page 16 of 17
H. Performance Criteria for Monitoring and Evaluation
50.           Two sets of monitoring and evaluation indicators have been defined and are included in
the Project's Implementation Plan: process indicators (or input and output indicators) to measure
progress in project implementation and impact indicators (or outcome and impact indicators) to evaluate
improvements in the health system and in the health status of the population. The most important process
indicators (most to be measured annually) are described in Table 7. The project will also promote the
use of monitoring indicators throughout the country by requiring recipients of the health promotion grants
to document the impact of their respective projects. Finally, key policy benchmarks have been identified
in the Government's Statement of Development Objectives and these will be reviewed annually by the
Association and the Government.

I. Analysis of Poverty Alleviation
51.           The collapse of pubic financing for health care has left many Georgians without access
to affordable health care. The Government's decision to reform the health sector is instrumental to the
broader public sector reform, to the development of a sustainable social safety network, and an
example of efficient use of scarce resources to fight against poverty. The project has a direct poverty
reduction objective by creating conditions for the financially sustainable provision of a basic package
of public health and essential clinical services. While most patients will still be required to pay for



AnnexD
Page 17 of 17
some services, the total financial burden should decrease. This is because while health care was
nominally free, under the table payments have long been common in Georgia. For services not
included in the basic package, the Government has been working on ways of better targeting for the
poor. Action has already been taken in hospitals to set aside beds to treat the most vulnerable groups.
The Government is also planning a quota system for every hospital to provide care for patients unable
to pay for health services not included in the basic package of care.
52.           By the end of 1996, with technical assistance provided under the SAC, a new backup
program for social assistance will be established. This program is intended to address the problems of
those who are needy but do not qualify for benefits under any existing program and can demonstrate
that their resources are very limited. The test for eligibility under the new program will take into
account the presence in the family of at least one person in vulnerable circumstances as well as the
family's income and assets. Families will have to periodically demonstrate that they continue to meet
the conditions for eligibility. The eligibility limits for this program will be set so that now more than
a small proportion of the Georgian population -- 3 percent -- qualifies for assistance. These people
will also be eligible for free care under the State Health Fund. The Government would also like to
institute co-payments for the basic package for those who can pay once a reliable means testing system
is in place, further freeing resources for the poor.
J. Assessment of Environmental Impact
53.           The project is expected to have a positive impact on the environment through energy
efficiency measures that are part of the overall program to improve hospital efficiency. Medical waste
disposal systems in hospitals and polyclinics were assessed during project preparation and under the
project standards and norms will be developed for the rehabilitation of health facilities. It has been
designated in Environmental Category C.
K. Overall Coherence and Completeness of Analysis
54.           The project is designed to support the Georgian health care reform program which is
amongst the most progressive of all the former Soviet republics. In developing the reform, the
Government has had to make tradeoffs in meeting conflicting objectives. For example, the new
payroll tax for health raises additional revenue for the sector, but also will affect the competitiveness
of industry and possibility of tax evasion. The new system will do less well at risk pooling and
ensuring universal access than the former Soviet system, but will improve quality and ensure coverage
of the most essential public health and clinical services. Most people will have to pay for health
services not included in the basic package, but these payments will be legitimate and rationally priced
rather than the illegal and unregulated system of gratuities which existed before. Government
spending on health remains low, but it is higher than before and affordable, given the current
economic situation. In the end, in the opinion of the project preparation team, the benefits of both the
reform as well as the project outweigh the possible negative consequences and risks. The project will
monitor closely the financial sustainability of the reforms and their effects on the poor and make
special efforts to strengthen the capacity of the Ministry of Health to proceed with implementation.



page 1 of 33

of the components and sub-components will have at least one component coordinator who manages the
activities in his or her component. A Project Coordination Unit (PCU), located in the Ministry of Health,
will be responsible for the overall management of the project and will be staffed by local consultants with
expertise in planning, administration, accounting, procurement and disbursement

B. Terms of Reference for Project Coordination Unit
Overview
These Terms of Reference set out the tasks and services to be provided by the Project Coordination
Unit (PCU) of the Georgia Health Project. The PCU will provide overall coordination of the Project,
in cooperation with the component coordinators who are responsible for managing the implementation
of activities in each component. The PCU will be located within the Ministry of Health.
Responsibilities of the PCU
Implementation of the Project Administration Component:
*  Procure works and goods
*  Procure services
*  Support component coordinators in the arrangements for in-country training, overseas
fellowships and study tours
*  Maintain project accounts
- Administer payments
* Prepare and submit applications for disbursement
- Maintain and assist component coordinators to maintain project and component accounts
and records
*  Update project costs
Coordinate and Manage Project Implementation Activities:
-  Develop, clarify, and disseminate procedures and guidelines consistent with Government
and Bank requirements, regulations and guidelines, covering: administration, procurement,
disbursement, accounting and record keeping, planning, evaluation, and reporting;
-  Based on plans made by component coordinators, prepare and update detailed overall
project implementation plan covering: implementation schedule, budget, financing plan,
procurement of civil works, packaging and procurement of goods, foreign and local
technical assistance, training and fellowships, disbursements estimates and other project
activities; and
*  Prepare, periodically update and distribute lists of names and contact address and numbers
of: PCU staff, component coordinators, key contact persons at the MoF, donor
representatives, World Bank Task Manager and Disbursement Officer.
Project Supervision, Monitoring, Evaluation and Reporting:
*  Conduct supervision and monitoring activities at component and sub-component levels,
including: conduct critical path analysis; monitor resource utilization; review progress;
resolve bottlenecks and issues; ensure accuracy, quality and consistency; and disseminate
best practices;
*  Coordinate and facilitate supervision requirements of the Government and external
agencies. In particular, act as counterpart to World Bank supervision missions and mid-

IV. MONITORING, EVALUATION, AND REPORTING
The selection of monitoring indicators should be guided by the following principles: 1) keeping the
indicators simple and measurable and 2) linking the indicators to decision making. The choice of
indicators is an ongoing process and the indicators below may be modified during project
implementation. Strengthening the capacity of the Government to collect and analyze health data is
included in the project. The project's Health Promotion Fund will also strengthen local capacity to
monitor health activities by requiring grant recipients to demonstrate measurable outcomes of their
projects.
Two sets of indicators have been defined: process indicators (or input and output indicators) to
measure progress in project implementation and impact indicators (or outcome and impact indicators)
to evaluate improvements in the health system and the health status of the population. Most project
inputs will be monitored as part of the Project's Implementation Schedule which was prepared in
Microsoft Project. Key policy benchmarks included in the Statement of Development Objectives will
also be monitored and reviewed annually by the World Bank and the Government.

B. Progress Reporting
Throughout the execution of the Project, semi-annual reports shall be sent to the World Bank from the
project coordination unit. The following outline is proposed: (i) an executive summary; (ii) two pages
of text describing progress, status, and problems encountered with the project component; (iii) updated
implementation schedules, based on those presented in this annex and revised to highlight any
differences between actual and anticipated project execution; (iv) fully completed monitoring indicator
tables; and (v) financial statements. These elements of the reports are described in more detail below.
(i)    Executive Summary: A brief summary of the principal information contained in the
report. The summary should emphasize, for the six month period in question, significant
project developments, major problems encountered, corrective measures taken to overcome any
implementation difficulties, and unusual events. An opinion should be given as to the overall
progress and effectiveness of the implementation of the project components.
(ii)   Project Status: The document shall report on the progress of the component's
implementation. Reference shall be made to the updated implementation schedules and any
differences shall be explained in the text. Problems encountered in project execution shall be
discussed and solutions proposed. Any other unusual events shall be presented. Staffing
difficulties, both within the implementing agency and/or project coordination unit itself as well
as those related to consultants and contractors involved in the Project, should be reported. This
section should include information on any changes in project management arrangements and
summaries on the performance of engineering and other consultants retained under the Project.
A written synopsis shall be presented of the work carried out in the preceding six months and
that to be undertaken in the coming six months.
(iii)  Implementation Schedules: The implementation schedules presented in this annex for
the major components of the Project will be made available to the respective component
coordinators and implementing agencies as software. In this way the execution of all the
components and subcomponents will be effectively tracked and compared with the initial
program. Graphic print outs of the schedules should be appended to the semi-annual progress
reports.
(iv)   Monitoring Indicator Tables: The tables given above, which document changes in the
monitoring indicators, shall be reproduced for each report and incorporated as appendices.
(v)    Project Financial Statements: Statements sunmmarizing project accounts should be the
appendix to the semi-annual progress report.
